If the clot is expelled by raising the finger from the orifice of the artery and extinguishing the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from those that are prepared in the fire.
Therefore, do not stop doing it with one after another until the blood is stopped.
And beware lest you burn the nerve that is there and another evil befall the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to stop it unless by one of four means, especially if the artery is large. Either with a cautery, as we said. Or with extraction, when it has not been extracted. For when it is extracted, its ends contract and the blood stops. Or let it be tied with a strong thread.  /> Et scias quod hoc potest fieri in omni loco corporis, praeter caput et manibus, quoniam ibi non est uiuentem, sed mortuam carnem. Or apply to it medicines that have the property of stopping blood, and bind it with a decent bandage.
But he who applies its stopping with a ligature and tightening with cloth or the application of burning substances and similar things does not help it at all, except rarely.  /> Sed quando arteria est parua, tunc possunt abscindere sanguinem per unam de his quatuor rebus, nisi quando est in capite vel in collo.
But if this happens to someone and there is no physician present or medicine, then let him begin with the position of the finger on the orifice of the vein itself, as we have said, and very tight binding until the blood is stopped from above the wound and onto the artery. And do not remove the finger, and pour cold water of very intense cold over it until the blood freezes and thickens and stops.
And meanwhile, he should consider what is needed from a cautery or from medicine.
On Incision, Perforation, and Bloodletting in Wounds and Similar Conditions.
We have already said in the first chapter about every illness in which it is suitable to use hot iron or burning medicine, its occasions, causes, instruments, and forms of cautery.
And there I placed chapters from the head to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter along the same path, so that it may ease the search for its questioner.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you to know, O sons, that in this chapter there is a similarity to what is in the first chapter about cautery.
And because of this, greater care is necessary, because in the operation that is in this chapter, evacuation often occurs with blood, which stands at the opening of a vein, or incision above an abscess, or opening of an exit wound, or treatment of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision above a stone, and similar things: all of these are associated with trembling, fear, and death occurs in many of them.  />
And I warn you not to allow doubt to fall upon you, for sometimes people with various illnesses come to you in this art. Among them is one who becomes angry with his illness, and for him death is light because of the intensity of what he finds from his illness and the length of its evil. And with his illness is fear that signifies death.
And among them is one who gives you his flute and enriches you with it, hoping for health, but his illness is deadly.  />  />  />
It is not appropriate for you to agree with the one who comes to you with such a method. Let your caution be greater than your greed and your impatience. And do not presume over anything from it, unless after a certain knowledge which verifies to you the praiseworthy outcome to which it has come.
And use in the treatment of all your patients prediction and indication concerning that to which it is suitable for health.
For in this you have assistance towards gaining fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, sons, with His understanding, and may He not take away your guidance and inspiration from you, for that is in His hand.
And I myself have arranged this Chapter by dividing it into sections, following what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck to the feet: so that your search for what you want from it and need may be made easier.
¶ On the treatment of water that collects in the heads of children.
Chapter I
Know that this illness often happens to children at birth or when the midwife tightly grasps the child's head without gentleness. ‌
And sometimes it happens also for an unknown cause that is not known.
And I myself have not seen this illness in anyone other than children, and every one of them that I saw died quickly because of it; therefore, it seemed to me to pass over treating it. ‌
And I myself saw one of them, a child whose head was filled with water, and his head swelled every day until he could no longer sit up because of the size of his head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture either collects between the skin and bone or accumulates beneath the bone on the skull (cranium).
And the operation in it, if there is moisture between the skin and bone, and the abscess is small, then you should make one slit in the middle of the head; and let the length of the slit be like two knuckles, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of the instrument.
If, therefore, the moisture has increased more and the abscess is larger, then make those two slits according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides; and water sinks when you press it with your hand towards the inside, and it does not hide itself from you: then you should make three slits in the middle of the head according to this manner.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the slits with cloths and wool. Then dress it on top with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and care for the wound with soft linens and ointments.
And do not stop binding the head evenly. And feed the patient with all dry food of little moisture, until the part is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is that you should observe where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
For sometimes it is more on the back of the head, or on the forehead, or on the right side, or on the left side.
Therefore, with your incision go to where you see the abscess and the collection of water; and cut it according as is possible for you.
And take care not to cut an artery, lest blood flow out and the patient die from that flow along with the evacuation of moisture.
¶ About the Incision of the Arteries that are behind the ears. ›
Chapter 2
When someone has catarrh in the eyes or chest and it becomes chronic, and medical treatment is of no benefit, then the final treatment for that person is to cut these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, it is necessary for the patient to shave his head. Then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries become apparent. Then let the patient tie his own neck with any of his own cloths. ‌ Then observe where the pulse of the vein is felt. And the locations of both are in the depressed areas behind the ears, and rarely are they hidden except in some people.
Then mark both locations with hot iron and cut each with a sharp razor, making the incision reach the bone: and do this across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the razor under the artery and cut it from above with the same incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' width joined together.
For when the vein is cut, blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which jumps frequently to the inner parts. ‌
But if the artery is not perceived by touch, then measure from the ear the distance of three fingers' width. Then mark with hot iron and find it down to the bone.
And that which should be emitted from the blood is six ounces, and perhaps you will emit more or less than that. All of this according to the amount which appears to you from the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery. ‌ ‌
Then observe at the wound. Then if there is anything left at the bone from the scab, cut it away so that a hot abscess does not occur. Then bind the wound with cloths of linen fabric.
Then care for him with ointment until he is healed.
About the extraction of the temporal arteries.
Chapter 3
When someone has an old migraine or severe cold due to the acrid humors and heat in the temples, or when it is a severe old headache of a similar nature, and medical treatments are applied according to their methods but do not help: then we have found, through experience with these illnesses, that extracting the temporal arteries or cauterizing them, as we have described, is effective.
And the method for extracting them is as follows: let the patient shave off the hair on their temples and expose the visible temporal artery. For it is made apparent to you by its own pulse, and rarely does it remain hidden except in a few people or during severe cold.
But if it is hidden from you, then have the patient tie their head with any cloth they have; afterwards, rub the area with a rough cloth. Or apply steam to the area with hot water until it appears clearly before you. Then take the spatula that I mentioned earlier. Then use it to easily peel away the skin until you reach the artery. 🙂 Then insert the hook into it and pull it upwards until you have extracted it from the skin and made it exit completely from all the underlying layers. 😊
But if the artery is thin, then wrap it around the tip of the hook. Then cut off a section according to how much the ends can be extended and contracted without causing any bleeding. For if it is not cut or severed, the blood will not rise at all. 😊
Then evacuate from the blood vessel six ounces of blood until three remain. 😊
If the artery is large: then you must tie it in two places with double-threaded, strong silk or gut string, so that putrefaction does not rush to the wound before its healing, and blood loss may occur.
Then cut the excess between the two ties. Do this at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery until the edges are cut off, using a surgical knife; so that it may stand in the place of that operation.
And this is a better operation, unless the patient has a fever or a hot constitution. Because a cautery is among those things which help to end moistures. Therefore, it is a better remedy.
And after the removal of the arteries, the place should be filled with old cloths, and fine bandages should be placed on top in a pleasing manner.
And after the dissolution, it should be cleaned with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow, and with ointments, until it heals.
If during the operation blood flows from the artery, then quickly cut it with a cautery or fill the place with tow; and keep your hand pressed until the bleeding stops. But if you do not have either of these at hand, then place your finger over the spot until the blood clots. And soak the place with very cold water, until the sharpness subsides and it is bound as needed.
And because it is easier and simpler to extract the vein by cauterizing it with this double-bladed cautery; let the sharpness of the two blades be like an almagdal.  />And after placing the blade, burn until it reaches the second tie.  />And this operation should not be done without a ligature, lest putrefaction enter into the wound before its healing. Truly, they are much sharper than two knives. For if they were sharp like two knives, the cold would hasten to both; and they would not cut flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained within them, and flesh is cut quickly; thus this work is better than any other, lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space between the two knives be the thickness of a finger after you mark two places with cautery; and place both of them very hot until they reach the mouth, and cut the vein in two places so that what is between its ends is lengthened. For they do not fully coalesce with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
¶ On the care for acute and persistent tear flow from the eyes.
When tears are persistent and their flow comes from the veins that are outside on top of the head, and medical treatment with medicines does not help them, and you see the face of the sick person already redden, and he feels in his forehead a sensation like the movement of ants: and his eyes are emaciated, moist, so much so that their hair is corroded by the corrosion: and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tears; then the ancients needed this operation for their treatment, and they named it a cure with the sword. 》
And you should advise the sick person to shave the hair from his forehead. Then make three equally spaced cuts in the forehead along its length; and let the length of each cut be like two fingers, and one cut should be halfway along the nose on the middle of the forehead, and the second one should be a little way from the movement of the temples, and the third one on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries that are on both sides; and keep your hand away from the continuation of the two jaws, and let the space of all the cuts be the size of three connected fingers; and have ready a piece of sponge, or many dry cloths, with which you may absorb the blood.
Then insert a thin reed with two sharp ends, the shape of which is shown next. From the incision near the temple to the second incision; and scrape off with it all the skin that is between the two incisions, along with the flesh that is over the mouth. Then do likewise from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove that reed and insert another instrument into the first incision, which is called a sharp scalpel, smooth on one side and not sharp on the other side. And this is its shape.
And place the sharp edge towards the attached flesh with the skin, and the smooth edge towards the mouth; and push it until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut along with it all the blood vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, except that the incision continues to what is visible on the skin.
Then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure. ●
And after an equal amount of blood has run out, press upon the places where the blood is clotted.
Then place a liniment of old lard into every fissure, and upon them put little poultices soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And see to it that no hot abscess occurs.
And on the third day, release the bandage, and administer a large quantity of infusion with warm water. Then treat it with balm ointment and the other things by which wounds are treated until it heals.
Chapter V
¶ On the treatment of acute tears and colds that come to the eyes from inside the head.
When someone has acute and painful catarrhs that are persistent: and you see their eyes weakened, small, and their sight already impaired; and the eyelids ulcerated, and the hairs fall out from them, and there is an acute pain in the depth of the head, causing distress, and sneezing follows: then know from these signs that those humors and catarrhs do not come except from many veins. ● ● ●
And a better treatment for them is this. And it is, as you would advise the patient to shave his forehead. Then make an incision in that fissure one in the middle of the forehead or slightly to one side according to its width, and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right: and let the cut go as far as the mouth; and keep your hand away from the temples which move during chewing until the mouth is revealed; and dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with an old cloth or with linen strips. Then bind it above with little woolen bands; and moisten the woolen bands with wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you loose that [bandage], and you see that the hot abscess is already diminishing, then it is necessary for you to scrub the mouth until flesh begins to grow there.
Then care for the patient with a drying diet that causes flesh to grow, as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and prepare from them an ointment, and apply it to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter Six.
¶ On the care of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a liquid substance, such as water and vinegar and things similar to them; or some sort of animal.  />
Therefore, when a stone or something of the kind that does not grow nor is added [to the ear] falls into it, expose the ear to the sun; and if you see the stone, then pour into the ear some oil of violets or sesame oil. Then administer an extraction and make him sneeze with condiments, and close the nostrils at the onset of sneezing, after you have placed pieces of cloth or wool around the ear and stretched the ear upward.
Often indeed it goes out with this treatment.
But if it does not go out, then administer the extraction of it with Gesty, that is, fine pizigares; the shape of which is this.
If therefore it goes out with gesty, it is good.
And if not, then administer the extraction of it with a fine blunt hook of few folds.
If it does not go out through that, then make a tube of bronze and insert the end of the tube deeply into the ear hole, and close what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there is no way for air except through the tube: then extract with a strong exhalation.  />
Often indeed it goes out with what we have described. But if it does not go out this way, then take from hekalimbat or from visco, that is, prepared glue with which birds are caught, a small amount; and place it on the end of a stick after you have wound over it a cotton cloth neatly placed: then insert it into the ear hole easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.  />  />
If it does not go out with all that we have described, then hasten to cut open, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of cutting is to bleed the sick person first in the cephalic vein, and extract from him blood according to his strength. Then make the sick person sit between your hands, and turn his ear upward, and find a small opening at the root of the ear in its fatty part in a depressed place itself; and let the opening be lunar-shaped, until you reach the stone: then remove it with what is possible for you from instruments.
Then wash the opening from your hour with speed and take care of it until it heals.
But if what falls into the ear is one of those seeds that grow and swell: then you should extract it.
But if it does not come out on its own, then take a thin, fine probe in this shape.
And apply with it the removal of that kind of seed which falls into the ear and does not come out when you think the seed is already moistened by the heat of the ear, until you break it up into many pieces: then remove those pieces with a hook or with a fine probe or with suction, as we said, for its removal is made easier.
In the case of water which enters the ear, it is necessary for the sick person to sneeze first, and already his ear has been filled with some old cooked food: and he lies down on that ear in which there is water. If it therefore comes out through it, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones the length of a finger, thin and smooth, and heat them briefly with fire, and let the sick person insert one into the opening of his ear: and he should jump on one foot, and strike the stone which is in the ear with another stone. Do not therefore cease to do this with stones until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out so that you take one of reed or rush and insert its end into the ear: and light the other end with fire until it is almost consumed. Then take another reed and do this many times until all the water comes out: and remove it with a syringe as previously described for the stone.
But in the case of an animal entering it, if it is small-bodied like a flea, care for it as I said in the division.
"But if it is of the body's main part and is noticeable to the senses, then administer its removal with tweezers and hooks, and its resolution is easier than all those held in the ear.
"However, when treating a worm born in the ear, if you care for it with what we mentioned in division and in dealing with fumigation, your treatment is of no use; then you must look at the ear in sunlight. "Therefore, if something appears there from the worms, then remove it with tweezers or with fine hooks.
"But if nothing appears there, then take a cannula whose form is such that it is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top; insert its fine tip into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; then suck with strong suction. "Do this many times until all the worms come out.
"But if they do not obey you in coming out, then coat the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said about the stone.
"But if they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the fumigations which the ancients have already experienced in killing worms; and you will find it in the section on fumigations.
"And let your pouring be with which you pour oil and medicine into the ear using this instrument, whose form is depicted here. "As you see, make it of silver or bronze, narrow below where there is a small hole, and wide above.
"So let the plunger that is within the hollow of the cannula be made neatly of iron. "And if you wish, take a reed and wrap its tip with cotton in a neat layer; then pour oil or juice, or whatever you want from these medicines into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then insert the reed with the cotton on top, and press it gently with your hand until the oil is forced into the hollow of the ear, and the patient feels it inside; and let what is poured into the ear already be slightly heated by fire; and take care that what is distilled into it is not very cold; for medicines do not tolerate this.
Chapter VII
The care of the accidental fall of gold.
Sometimes certain infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it has happened to some people that the fall of gold occurs in the ear canal from a wound or flesh born within it.
And this fall of gold sometimes occurs deep within the ear canal, which vision cannot follow; and sometimes it happens near the outer edge of the ear canal, and vision follows it.
Therefore, that which occurs deep within the ear and which sight does not grasp is difficult to cure in most cases.
And in that which vision follows, you must place the ear of the patient in the sun and look into it; then if you see an apparent fall of gold, open it with a fine spatula, whose form is as follows: let its tip be small in width, of which one part should be sharp, and let the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides so that it does not injure the ear.
If, however, the fall of gold is from flesh born within the ear canal, then hold it with a fine hook and cut it with great ease until all the flesh is removed.
But if the fall of gold is deep within the ear, then take a fine, smooth wire and heat it slightly with fire; then insert it into the ear canal. ething.
If you feel the fall of gold with it, which the patient finds with difficulty of hearing, strive to open it with ease; and beware lest you wound the nerve of the ear.
Then place in the ear a piece of linen according to the width of the ear canal, which you have rolled in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are confident about the healing of the wound.
Or take linen and pour rose water over it, and sprinkle finely ground sarsaparilla over it, and use it.
If you see the ear already infected with boils: then you must extract the pus, and allow it to be covered with another poultice, which you will wrap in a cloth that has been made with rose oil, until the hot abscess is settled; then care for it until it heals.
If there is bleeding: then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear; and use the rest of the treatment which cuts off the aforementioned bleeding in the treatise on distillations.
About the care for sty in the eyelids.
Chapter Eight
These styes that occur in the eyelids of the eye are sometimes wet and sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with tweezers or with a hook, and cut them off at their roots with a scalpel. Then place powdered sulfur on the places.
If there is bleeding from them, then cauterize them with a fine lens-shaped cautery.
And it is better to cauterize after the incision. For often they return when some of their roots remain. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return. )))
About the care for chalazions in the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter Nine
Often there occurs in the eyelids something which resembles a chalazion in its strength and hardness, and on account of this it is called "grain"; and it is an accumulation of thick fluid in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
The treatment for them is to observe whether the grain has developed inside it, as can be seen from the eyelid, and they move in every direction, and its nature is easy. ))) )))
Then find a slit of equal width on it. Then peel it from every part with ease until it comes out. Then hang it with a hook and cut it off.
But if its cutting is not prepared for you until after you penetrate the eyelid with a slight cut, then that harms the patient somewhat.
But if the opening is large: then join it with stitches and care for it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For an ointment restores it and makes it fleshy again.
And if the grain is inclined towards the inner surface of the ear, then turn back the eyelid and hang the grain with a hook, except that you need to make a cut and press it from all sides.
But if the eyelid is lengthened by the cut, this causes no harm at all. 》
Then wash the eye after removing the grain with salt water and care for the place with what makes it fleshy until the patient heals.
¶ About treating trachoma of the eyelids.
Chapter 10
Chapter X. Trachoma is a fat that forms in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and this happens especially to children. It weighs down their eyes, they get colds in the mornings, and they cannot look at the sun because tears rush into them. For this reason you see them constantly lying on their faces, and sleeping on them. Their eyelids are wet under their eyebrows, which inflammation has already covered; and a visible Axirnath appears to the eye. And when you press the place with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of treatment is for the patient to put his head in your lap.
Then take a cloth of flax, and prepare bandages in it, and make from them a circular bandage according to the size that contains Axirnath on all sides. Then place it on top of it and press it with your fingers on every side, so that Axirnath is collected in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that wetness a small opening with an incision knife across its width, and let the cut not be deeper than that made in bloodletting.
Or you should look for the whole skin until you reach Axirnath, for in many cases Axirnath proceeds from the cut according to the shape of a white piece of fat.
Therefore extract it with the cloth that you have put between your fingers on the outside; and you roll your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed; and be careful not to add anything into the cut so that you do not reach with the knife to the eye, and injure it.
But if Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the cut, then you should add a little into the cut with ease until Axirnath proceeds out. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the spot, and bind it with small bandages.
And there are some people who grind salt and place it on the cut so that whatever remains of that wetness is dissolved.
Then take care of it until it heals.
But if a hot abscess occurs in that place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
¶ About the kinds of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye becomes soft, so that it blinks as it should not.
Chapter 11.
When hairs grow in the eyelids beyond their natural course and are ignored, they harm the eye and cause various illnesses such as constant tears, softening of the eyelids, and cataracts, eventually leading to the destruction of the eye.
The treatment of ataxia of the eye is carried out in four ways. Either by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp instrument, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery.  /> Or ataxia of the eye is treated by incision and suture. Or by means of reeds, as I will explain.
You should place the head of the patient in your lap. Then, with your left hand, turn back the eyelid of the eye.
If it is turned back, that is good. If not, insert a needle with thread from the lower part of the eyelid and make the needle penetrate with the thread above it, near the hair itself, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow; then pull the thread upwards with the eyelid and turn it back with a rod.  />  />
Then find, within the eyelid under the added hairs, with a small spoon from the greater tear duct to the lesser tear duct.
Then pull out the thread and place a small piece of cotton or cloth under the eyelid; then mark the eyelid with wax in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be made according to the size and thickness of the eyelid; for it varies greatly among people.
There are some who need a large piece to be cut out of the eyelid, corresponding to the amount that is softened by the eyelid.  />  />  •
Then, when you find the eyelid space over the two lines that you have marked, begin from the greater tear duct to the lesser tear duct, and let there be one cut near the natural hairs, as thick as a finger.
Then insert the hook into one of the two corners of the skin. Then remove it completely. Then join the two lips with stitches using a fine woolen thread and a needle: clean away the blood, and make the excess threads stick together above the two eyebrows with some adhesive substances, if you wish to do so; but if not, it does not matter.
Then leave the stitches and threads for three or four days; afterwards, treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitches and treat it with something that dries and closes it. For the eyelid will be raised at the sealing of the wound and its joining, but it will be better afterwards.
Ancient people taught this method of treatment to Ataxmir.
However, there is concern for the patient in this, and it comes from good treatment; there is no fear in it.
And another method is also found in Ataxmir, and it is to mark a shape on the eyelid like the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have said.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate or joined hooks according to this following shape; then cut away the excess with small scissors according to this shape, with an equal depth of cut.
But if you cannot hold the hook and it does not match up, then take a needle with thread in it and insert it in the middle of the shape; and insert another thread near the greater tear duct and a second thread near the lesser tear duct; and gather the threads between your fingers with equal tension.
Then raise your hand with an equal lift: and raise the skin above which the knot is marked, as we have said.
Then bind together the two lips of the wound with a stitch: and care for it until it heals.
But if an abscess occurs at your incision or cut: stitch that abscess with a suture and similar things from the soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelids as well, so that its hairs turn inward.
Therefore you must use what we have said about the incision; but the stitch is more artful for both [eyelids]. ¶ On the care of trachoma with conjunctivitis.
And it is [done] by turning back the eyelid and cutting the fissure that is inside, as I have said. Then skillfully make two cannulae or two fine sticks whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of a spindle. And you have already cut at the extremities of both from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then bind each one with care over that which protrudes from the eyelid of the eye, and tie together both sticks from both sides with a strong knot, and leave it for several days. 》 For the skin held tightly will die, and turn black, and putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
Therefore, when it is consolidated, raise the eyelid: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of the two cannulae which you should make according to this figure and measure in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
¶ On raising hairs that prick in the eye with a needle. 》
The operation for this is as follows: take a fine needle and insert into it a fine, soft silk thread. Then you will tie the ends together with two very fine knots; and the length of the threads should be one palm.
Then prepare another fine thread under the Alinxotati in a short section, and if you wish, tie the ends together.
Then let the patient place his head on your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the operation can be clearly seen; for this is a delicate or subtle operation.
Then insert the needle with the Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid and make it penetrate quickly into the eyelid until you extract a natural hair from above. ↑
Then pull the hair up to just above the Alinxotati and insert that hair into the Alinxotati; if there is one, or two, or three, no more than that.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair comes out with the Alinxotati and the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if the Alinxotati is pulled out and the hair does not come out with it, pull the Alinxotati downwards with the thread that you prepared in it until the Alinxotati comes out of the upper hole, and you see it with your eyes; then you will pull the Alinxotati and the thread, and extract both, and close the eye; and leave it closed for two days or three, until the hair adheres to its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate a long hair with it from the natural hairs and lift it up together; or leave it until it lengthens over time, and then lift it up.
Chapter 13
When this happens to the eye, it is called leprosy.
And this leprosy is either natural or accidental.
The accidental kind occurs from the healing of a wound, cut, or cautery, and similar things.
And the manner of operating on it is that you should cut through that healing, separate its edges, and place a linen thread between them, and tie it until it heals.
And it is not fitting to use things that dry and constrict in its treatment, for if you do this, the leprosy returns worse than before; rather, use things that soften, such as fenugreek, embrocation with water in which althea and flax seed have been cooked, and diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil. And wrap a linen thread with it and treat with it.
And your treasure of treatment is to strive by all means that it does not heal according to the first form.
And this operation which we have spoken of is nothing but a correction of the leprosy, so that the form of the place does not entirely return as it was before.
¶ On the treatment of leprosy which occurs on the lower eyelid and is a wrinkle or contraction upward toward the right and left.
Chapter XIV
This leprosy which occurs below is what is truly called leprosy. It is both natural and accidental.
And the accidental kind occurs from a wound, or cut, or cautery, and similar things. 
And the way of operating on it is that you should take a needle with a double thread, and dip it in ink, and make it penetrate from the left lacrimal to the right lacrimal, until the thread reaches the two ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with a needle, and cut it with a broad scalpel. If therefore the figure of the eyelid returns as is fitting, it is good.
And if not, take a thread and place it in the slit, and turn the eyelid with it. And find two slits on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two slits be from the two corners of the incision, until they meet and form a corner; until when they come together the figure will be like this figure, which is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh in the amount that its sharp side is below that which follows the eyelid. Then gather the separated parts: or sew them together with a thread of wool.
Then treat them with what we have said about softening ointments and linens until they heal.
And if ectropion occurs from the slit or suture, or from cautery, then it is necessary to cut a flat slit under the eyelashes, even as has been said before. Then separate between the two lips with a linen thread, as we have said.
And the summary of the treatment for ectropion, whether it is above or below, is that this operation should be performed according to the preparation from the type of ectropion.
For indeed it [ectropion] is of many diverse forms; and a skilled craftsman prepares his ingenuity in whatever way is possible for him, so that he may bring the figure back to its natural form, or close to it, and the patient himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter XV.
¶ On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid consolidates with the white of the eye and prevents its natural operations.
Therefore, you should insert the tip of a needle under the eyelid and lift it up, or extend it with a hook; then cut the adhesion with a fine spatula that is not sharp like the incision spatulas but rather slightly blunt so as not to disturb the patient during the operation, and remove the spatula, lest it injure the eye with its sharpness. And let your incision be as if you were excoriating that adhesion easily, until when the eyelid returns to its natural shape, and the entire adhesion heals.
Then pour salt water into the eye and wash it with water, or distill onto it from a sieve made of crushed gum dissolved in water.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen cloth; and place on the eye wool soaked in egg white; and after the third day, use a sieve and bandages until it heals.
¶ On the incision of the nail and the prominence of the tear gland.
Chapter XVI
Know that the nail is in two ways. Either it is nervous and similar to a dry sponge, thin. Or it is not nervous and similar to frozen white moisture; when this is touched with iron or you try to catch it with a hook, it breaks off and the hook does not stick into it.
And both types do not begin except from the larger tear gland, until it gradually extends to cover the vision and prevent light and eye movement.
And the way of operating in its incision is: so that the patient places his head in your lap. 》 》 Then open his eyes, and raise his eyelid with your hand. Then gather the nail with a small hook, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle and insert into it a hair from the hairs of a horse, ox, or cow; and let the thread be strong, and slightly double the end of the needle, and place it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards: and remove the skin with the hair on the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you sew it with the hair to its very end.
Then cut the remaining part at the root of the lacrimal gland with a fine scalpel or small scissors: and leave the natural lacrimal flesh as is, so that persistent tearing does not occur from the incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone; or with a thread, as we have said. Then it should be scraped with a soft scalpel of this shape.
And take care that the scalpel does not touch the corneal membrane, lest it cause a tear and pierce the pupil.
Then after the incision, put some crushed salt or a sieved powder of ziniar into the eye, and leave it until another day. Then care for him with what is necessary until he is healed.
If the nail is not fleshy and you cannot insert a needle into it or fix a hook in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medications and heals as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the patient and take a thin, soft, and weakly sharpened scalpel of this shape. And scrape the surface of the nail with great fineness. If you see that it becomes rarefied and fragmented, and a furrow is impressed upon it, then distill into the eye from an hourglass made of tin or red glass, or crushed salt: and fill the eye until another day.
Then repeat the same procedure until all of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in your eye; then leave that [procedure] alone and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the procedure with the furrow until it heals.
If the prominence of the tear-producing flesh causes great harm in the eye, hang it with a hook and cut off a portion of it; but do not finish the incision, so that the flow of tears may not be interrupted.
Then distill into a red glass or tin hourglass until it heals.
§ On the Incision for Alguardenic and the Flesh Born in the Eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes a red fleshy substance is born in the eyes of some people, either composed or placed over [the eye], until it covers the pupil or approaches it, or presses on the eyelids and perhaps turns the eyelids outward; and it resembles a rosebud.
And the procedure for this is also that the patient should place his head on your lap. Then open his eyes and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks; and hold it with one forceps or tweezers. />
Then cut the flesh first, until all of it is finished with a scalpel. And be careful of the eye, so that you do not harm it during the procedure; and let your incision by which you cut [the flesh] be either with a spatula, as we have described in the incision for a fingernail; or with small forceps, with which asebel is collected, according to its form after this chapter.  />  />
When your incision is complete and all the white of the eye has been removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt or sprinkle red powder into it, and similar things from corrosive medicines; and place a cooked egg-white over the eye to secure it from hot inflammation.
If some of the white of the eye remains and hot blood prevails in you and you fear a hot inflammation, then leave the eye alone and treat it with that which settles inflammation.
Then repeat the treatment on it until it heals.
And do similarly with the flesh that happens to be in the eye from this type.
However, it is necessary that a stranger should not undertake the treatment of any weak eye from natural or accidental causes by operating with iron, and you should not interfere in any of these operations until you see that this member can bear that operation.
¶ On collecting Sebel (pus) from the eye.
Chapter Eighteen
Pus consists of red veins that spread over the eye and hinder its vision; and with the passage of time, they weaken the eye.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye with pus is strong and has no other illness except for pus; then collect the pus from it.
And it is necessary that you instruct the patient to place his head on your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one or two hooks according to your judgment; and let the hook be of fine wire, as shown in this figure [image not provided], or let there be two hooks on one body, as shown in this figure [image not provided].
Then collect those veins with fine scissors carefully, and wipe away the blood hour by hour until you see those veins removed from the eye, and they dissolve; and be careful not to injure the eye with the ends of the tweezers.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and be steadfast in your operation very much: so that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have cleared it out, then distill into the eye either green or red: so that its sharpness may eat away what remains of the barley seed.
But if you are not able to remove all of it at that time, then bandage the eye with something that will alleviate the pain of the eye; and leave it for some days until its pain is relieved: and protect it from a hot abscess; then repeat the same process over it according to the same method until it heals.
And this is the shape of the forceps.
About reducing a broken nose back to its normal position.
Chapter 19.
Doctors call the broken nose a fistula, which they treat with a cautery or with a sharp burning medicine, as was mentioned before in its description.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it unless you pierce over the abscess at its origin and draw out all the moisture and pus that is in it until the bone is exposed.
Therefore, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument whose shape is like this. ■
And its name is "rasp for the head.
Let it be made from Indian iron and let its head be round like a needle, which should be studded with the studs of a file or the studs of a fine rasp; and place it on the corrupted bone. ■ ■ ■
Then turn it between your fingers and press your hand a little until you know that the corruption is scraped off; and do this several times. ■ ■ ■
Then restore the place with dry astringent medicines.
If therefore the place consolidates and flesh grows in it, and the course of pus ceases: and it remains for forty days without renewing or becoming inflamed, and no accident occurs in it: then know that it has already been healed.
And if not, then there is no wisdom in it, except to reduce the fistula to the opening of the nose, according to this form.
And it is that you uncover from the bone with a second iron or with a sharp medicine: and when the opening has been uncovered, then take a trepan of this form, its end being iron and triangular: and its handle be a slender pyramid up to the extremity, as you see.
Then place it over the very opening: and let it be near the tear duct: and extend your hand a little from the eye. Then turn your hand with the trepan until it penetrates the opening, and let the patient feel it: because he finds air escaping from it when his hand is placed over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent and drying medicines, as I have taught you.
Therefore when flesh grows and the place hardens, then the matter that was flowing out to the outside returns to the nose: it is therefore easier for the patient.
¶ About the Reduction of an Eye's Prominence.
Chapter XX.
When an eye protrudes with all of itself and no harm or decrease occurs to the sight, then it is necessary that you release the patient; then phlebotomize him: afterwards place cupping glasses on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place over the eye a plaster made from acacia, and aloe, and frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye above with many fine threads on top of the plaster.
"And the way to make poultices is this: take soft cloths, many of them, and fold each one into four layers. Make many of these. Then place one on top of another according to the amount needed for the eye. Then bind them together with a hard cloth, tightly.
"And repeat the procedure with cupping glasses, using suction without scarification.
"Then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bandaged day and night.
"However, if the ligature becomes loose again, then it must be tied tightly. Then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we have said.
"If you see that the eye has already returned to its normal state, then it is good. If not, then repeat the treatment with plasters, poultices, bandages, and cupping glasses until it heals.
"Chapter on Incising the Conjunctiva.

"When a rupture occurs in the conjunctiva and a lump protrudes from the eyelid like a grain of barley, disfiguring the face, and one wishes to incise it, then the procedure is as follows: insert the needle at the root of the eyelash from below to above.
"Then insert another needle with double thread from the lacrimal part and make it penetrate; then leave the first needle in its original position.
