About the treatment of alcomnatum.
Chapter 22.
This illness, which is called alcomnatum, is nothing but a discharge that accumulates in the eye, similar to water descending, and it is not itself.
And the way to treat this is by having the patient sit on a prepared or straight seat.
Then take his head with your hands on both sides and move it until you see the discharge reach below his eyes. Then it should be fixed and not move downward, and the eye should be released; and the patient should see things as he used to see them; and if it does not move downward, you should know that it is water.
But if the descent of the discharge does not occur as we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands. ️ Then take a fine needle and slightly puncture the skin where the conjunctiva joins with the cornea, near the edge, until the discharge comes out.
When it comes out, then drip warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey; or water that has already been boiled with fenugreek and honey. Then treat it with the rest of the remedy until it heals.
§ On the treatment of water descending into the eye, or cataracts.
Chapter 23.
Now we have spoken about the types of water according to its division, and which is appropriate for piercing, as the declarative and explanatory speech has stated; then take that same [water] according to health.
Then it is necessary for you to have the patient sit between your hands, facing the light, near the sun; and cover his healthy eye, and bind it very tightly; then with your right hand, if the eye in which the water is, is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one. ️
Then take with your right hand the collyrium, if the eye is left: or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
Then place the tip of the collyrium near the cornea according to the thickness of the radius, in the same whiteness of the eye where the tear duct is smaller; then press the collyrium with force: and you turn your hand with it until it penetrates into the sclera of the eye: and feel that the collyrium has already reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the measure of the journey of the collyrium be to the depth, the measure of the space which is from the pupil until the end of the blackness; and this is the cornea of the eye. For you will see them in the very pupil of the eye, because of the clarity of the corneal membrane.
Then place the collyrium until it reaches the watery part. Then press it downwards once after another.
If therefore the water immediately comes out, then the patient sees that immediately on which he opens his vision, and the collyrium is in his eye.  ()
Then wait a little while. If water comes out, then put it aside a second time, except that the collyrium emerges.
When it becomes fixed and does not come out, then remove the collyrium with ease: and you slowly turn your hand.  ()  ()
Then dissolve some of the clear salt crystal in water: and wash the eye with it from within. Then place outside the eye all cotton or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white: and bandage the sound eye with it.
But we, and those who are of our time, place on top of it ground barley with egg white.
If the collyrium does not obey you at the entrance into the eye because of its hardness, since it is made from humans whose eyes are very hard: then it is necessary that you take a small stick, which is called Albard, and this is what it looks like.
So grasp that same membrane with it, and do not thrust your hand through the opening; there is nothing to do but to make a way to the almagda, a subtle place.
Then introduce the almagda as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare for the patient a decently made bedchamber in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room; and let him be kept from all movement and coughing; and give him food by which his nature is soothed; and let him not move his head at all to the right or the left.
And let the bandage remain according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then unbind it in that dark room, and test his vision; and make him look at things. Then rebinding it until the seventh day.
And it is not right to do this at the time of the operation or immediately after the incision; but rather it should be avoided, because water rises quickly through a powerful gaze.
But if a hot swelling occurs, then it is necessary to unbind the eye before the seventh day; and treat it with what soothes the swelling until it subsides; then let out its vision, and place on his face a coverlet that regulates his sight under it for several days, while he remains in that dark room.
Then let him go forth from the house gradually, and turn to his affairs.
And know that a student is not exempted from vision many times; then indeed he may venture upon the operation.
And indeed a certain man from those of Alarcos has come to us, who said that at Alarcos an almagda was pierced through which water is sucked out.
Truly I have not seen anyone in your land who has done this; nor have I read it in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that it is new. 》
And these are the forms of species from dough, so that it may stand on it who does not know them.
Let them be made properly from air: and their extremities should have this same triangular subtlety, and an acute tip.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
¶ On the care for flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes in the nose, various kinds of flesh are born, one of which resembles a scorpion with many feet; and from this is cancerous flesh, stone-like, dark in color; and from it is gentle flesh, not dark in color.
Therefore, in that which is gentle from these fleshes, not deceitful nor cancerous, it is necessary for you to make the patient sit between your hands opposite the ground, and open their nostrils, and throw a hook into those fleshes; then draw them outwards, and cut what you have grasped from them with a thin sharp spatula on one side until you know that all the flesh has now been removed.
But if something of it remains whose cutting is not possible; then scrape that with one of the fine instruments easily, until nothing remains from it. But if blood prevails over you, or a hot aposteme occurs; then oppose it with what is necessary: or it is from malignant apostemes, then hurry to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the fleshes are removed.  /> But if the patient's nose bleeds frequently and persistently, or there is a severe headache, or fever, or other harmful symptoms appear; then it is necessary for you to stop these, before treating this disease.
Then after the incision, vinegar should be poured into the nose, and water, or wine. But if the nose is opened, and moisture comes out of it as far as the palate, then know that he is already healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that inside there is flesh born in the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then you should insert a linen thread with some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and place between each knot the length of a finger or less. The patient will then make one end of the thread enter the nose with a bone, or whatever is possible for him to make similar to an awl: and he should pull on his olfactory until it reaches the skull, and exits over his throat.
And often boys at school do something similar to this Atom; and it is an easy thing for one who wants to.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end that exits from the nose and the other that remains inside the nose. Then the flesh is cut with a saw along with the knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is now together with the knots of the thread.
Then remove the thread and place a linen cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose after washing away the blood. Do this for three days or more until the remaining ointment has completely eaten away the flesh.
Then at the end of the treatment, place a lead tube into the nose for several days until it heals.
If, however, it needs drying treatment, use this one.
For making a head-purge, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make this from silver or bronze, similar to a small lamp, open like a shell, and its channel similarly.
And if you want to make it as a closed tube, make it like a reed.  />
And the shell of the instrument for making a head-purge should be full and open; it should have a handle at its end with which you hold it when heating oil or whatever liquid substances you wish in it.  />  />  />
Chapter XXV.
A swelling often occurs at the tip of the nose and increases over time, until it becomes unsightly.
And because of this, it is necessary that at its first appearance you cut into it and completely remove the whole thing. Then place on the spot either a hot iron or a burning medicine to take its place.
If therefore its incision is delayed until it grows large, then consider whether it is stony, hard, dark in color, and painful; do not touch it with iron because it is a malignant tumor.
For many times I have seen that whoever cuts into such a tumor has suffered great harm from it.
And if the tumor is soft to touch, not dark in color, and you see that it can be cut into entirely, then evacuate the patient and cut into it without fear or dread; and treat the spot with that which dries and contracts until it heals. 😊
Chapter XXVI.
About stitching the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, an operation on it is of little benefit, except in some people.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, you should consider whether it is a recent wound with its own blood; then bring together the edges of the wound with stitches and treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of separation have already separated and each part has become healthy; then it is necessary to scrape off all parts that are visible from their skin until they bleed. 😊 Then bring the two lips together with stitches and tie them; and sprinkle on them powdered blood of a dragon and ground frankincense; and place on these powders a piece of diafinicon plaster and another of the consolidating plasters; and leave it tied for two or three days. 😊 ️ Then dissolve it and replace the medicine; and let it go until the threads are cut off by themselves. Afterwards, take care of him with ointment until he is healed.
The method of suturing is to join the continuity with a needle, as we have described in the case of the abdomen, or to join it with thread, as I have taught you.
Chapter 27.
¶ On the Extraction of Lip Nodes.
Indeed, many people develop hard lumps inside their lips, similar to grains of barley, and some are smaller while others are larger.
Therefore, you should turn the lip inside out and flatten over each node; then hang it with a hook and cut it from all sides.
Then fill the place after cutting with powdered barley until the blood is cut off.
Then wash it with vinegar and scrape the place where the astringency is until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 28.
¶ On the Incision of Flesh Above the Gum. ➤
Often there appears on the gum a growth of flesh which the ancients called abulus.
Therefore, you should hang it with a hook or hold it with forceps and cut it at its root, allowing the pus or blood to run out.
Then place on the site powdered barley or other astringent and drying powders.
If the flesh returns after treatment, for it often does return, then cut it a second time and cauterize it, for it will not return after cautery.
Chapter 29.
¶ On the Filing of Teeth with Iron.
Sometimes there form on the surfaces of teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums, rough, foul growths which blacken and turn yellow and green until corruption reaches the gum, making the teeth foul.
It is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and his head on your lap, and shave the tooth and its pulp, in which you see cortices and something similar to sand: until nothing remains of them, and do likewise with the blackness, the greenness, and the yellowness, and other things until they are cleansed.
Therefore, if what is in them is removed at the beginning of the reasoning process, that is good; and if not, then you must repeat the reasoning process over them on another day, a second time, and a third time, until finally your effort achieves what you desire.
And know that the tooth requires razors of diverse forms and many shapes according to how it is prepared for your operation; therefore, because the razor with which the tooth is shaved from below is different from the razor with which the space between the teeth is shaved, according to other forms.
And these shapes of the razors should be prepared for you, as you see.
Chapter XXX
On the Extraction of Toothache
It is necessary to treat the tooth for its pain with all skill and be slow in extracting it. For it is not a matter of replacing its place when extracted: because it is a sublime substance. But when there is no excuse for extracting it, then if the patient is forced to have the tooth extracted, you must firmly secure and establish with yourself the aching tooth.
For often the pain deceives the patient and makes him think that it is in the tooth and he extracts it; then the pain does not go away until a weaker tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen this in the practice of bloodletting.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is firmly established with you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, on which there is some power, until the gum is dissolved from every side; then move the tooth gently with your fingers or with fine forceps little by little until you shake it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and hold the patient's head between your knees so that you have held it well enough that it does not move; then draw the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it beneath it from all sides with ease, and try to move it, as you did first.
If the tooth is perforated or corroded, then you must fill that hole with cloth; and tie it with a fine string, so that it does not break at the time of your squeezing on top of it with forceps.
And you must act very carefully with scarification all around the gums from every side greatly.
And be strong so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the patient with greater evil than his first pain.
And beware of doing what ignorant bleeders do out of haste and their own boldness in removing it, besides administering that which we have narrated.
For many times they cause great diseases to befall men, which are very difficult to break and all or some of its roots remain; or that they extract it with some bones of the jaw, as we have often witnessed this.
Then let it be rinsed after its extraction with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If bleeding occurs from the site (for this often happens), then take some of zegi and fill the site with it; and if not, cauterize it if zegi does not benefit you.
The tips of the fine forceps with which the tooth is first moved should be long, and have a short, thick handle so that they do not bend when pressed on the tooth with them; they should be like you see with thick handles, until when you squeeze with them, they do not give way to themselves or bend; they should have short tips, and be made of Indian iron or well-made steel, straight at the tips; and in their tips there should be teeth, some fitting into others, and binding with a decent, strong constriction.  />
And sometimes they become like the beak of a bird called a stork, and are very narrow.
Chapter 31.
About the extraction of teeth, and the removal of broken jawbones.
When something remains after the extraction of a broken tooth, then you should place a cotton pad with butter on it for one day or two days until the area softens; then introduce to it tweezers or forceps whose tips resemble the beak of a bird called a stork. This is the form of forceps.
Let their tips be made like a file within, or like a chisel.
If it does not yield to you with these forceps, then you should make an incision on the root and expose all the flesh; then introduce a small lever whose form is this.
Let it be short of tip, somewhat thick; and let it not be brittle, so that it does not break.
If the root comes out with it, that is good; and if not, you must help with these other instruments whose forms are these. ¶ Adhuc alii instrumenti faciuntur de ligno, et non franguntur.
The form of the first is triangular at the tip, in which there is a certain thickness.
The form is a thin triangular tip.
And with the other instruments and tools that have been mentioned earlier regarding teeth.
Also help with this instrument that resembles a large hook of this form, having a twisted triangular tip with some thickness so it does not break; and let it be not brittle.
And know that the instruments of teeth are many; and likewise the other instruments are not counted, and an expert craftsman, subtle in his art, finds by himself an instrument according to what the operation and the illnesses themselves indicate.
Because of the variety of kinds of diseases about which the ancients did not speak of instruments.
If, however, the bone is broken from the jaw or other bones of the mouth, or if it becomes rotten; then explore over it in its place with what suits it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned for root extraction, and help with this hook, whose shape is this:
Let there be in it something thick gradually, so that the bone may be held or grasped with it, and not slip away, until the bone comes out; and restore the place with suitable medicines for it.
But if there is only a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape it from its rottenness, and care for it, until it heals.
Chapter 32.
About the binding of protruding teeth above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, the form becomes ugly through that; and especially when this happens to women and girls, namely whores.
Therefore, you should consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and is not placed, or its binding or filing is possible; then extract it. And if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is this:
It is similar to a small beak and is made from Indian iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your cut, by which you cut it, be over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, so that another tooth may not be disturbed.
But if it is protruding and ready for filing; then file it with an Indian file, whose form is this: ›
It should be all of Indian iron, and its handle of very small points, as the file with which needles are made; with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days with ease, so that the tooth may not be disturbed or fall out; then smooth it, finally scrape it with certain scrapers.
If it is a tooth, from which a piece breaks off and injures the tongue, then it must be filed evenly until the roughness of that break is removed, leveled, and smoothed so that it no longer injures the tongue or corrupts speech.
Chapter 33
Concerning the extraction of teeth using golden threads.
When a concussion or injury occurs to the front teeth from a blow or accident, and the patient cannot bite down on what they are eating so that it does not fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but the treatment has not been effective, then there is a method for securing them using a golden thread. Because silver turns green and rots after several days, but gold never changes.
And the thread should be of medium thinness and thickness according to the size between the teeth.
And the manner of this extraction is as follows: take a thread and insert its double between two healthy teeth; then weave it with both ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or several, until you reach with the weaving to the other healthy tooth on the opposite side. Then repeat the weaving back to where you started; and bind with finesse and wisdom until it does not move at all; and let your binding of the thread be near the roots of the teeth, so that it does not slip out.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the thread with scissors; and join them together and turn them back with a probe; and hide them between a healthy tooth and a loose tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue; then let it remain thus bound until it heals.
But if it loosens or breaks, bind them again with another thread, and they will remain secure in this manner for the whole time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of the extraction of two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one tooth or two return to their original places after the accident, and are retained as we have described, and remain there; and this is done only by an skilled craftsman, wise and experienced.
And sometimes a bone from an ox is prepared; and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in its position, by which the tooth is supported and fastened, as we have said; and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs beneath the tongue is natural, present from birth, or it may be acquired, resulting from an old healed wound.
And the procedure is to open the patient's mouth wide and lift up their tongue; then cut through that nerve-like ligament across its width until the tongue is freed from its constriction.
But if there is something hard and knotted in it, and it is due to an old healed wound, then insert a hook into it and cut through its width until you reach the ligament and free the knot. ›
And be careful not to create a deep cut in the flesh, lest you cut an artery and cause bleeding.
Then wash the site of the incision with rose water or vinegar or cold water.
Then place a linen cloth under the tongue for the patient to hold every night, so that it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, then apply powdered alum to the site. But if the bleeding is too much for you to manage, then cauterize the site with a suitable cautery iron; then treat it with the usual care until it heals.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue that resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from performing its natural function; and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth.
And the procedure is to open the mouth of the patient in sunlight and examine the abscess.
If you see that it is dark or black in color, hard, and the patient cannot feel it, then do not touch it, because it is a cancer.  
And if it is inclined towards whiteness and has moisture, then place a hook into it, and cut it with a thin scalpel, and free it from all parts. If blood overcomes you during your procedure, then place crushed zea on it until the bleeding stops; then return to your procedure until you extract it with all of its contents.
Then wash it with vinegar and salt, and care for it with the remaining appropriate treatment until it heals.
¶ Of the treatment of an abscess in both tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes abscesses occur between the throat and the glands, which are similar to glands that appear externally; they are called tonsils. If you treat them with what we have said about incision and they do not heal, then consider them.
If the abscess is hard, dark in color, and has little sensation, then do not touch it with an iron instrument.   
And if it is red in color and its base is thick, then do not touch it even with an iron instrument, fearing a flow of blood; rather, leave it until it matures.       And then either you will pierce it or it will burst on its own.      
And if it is of white color and round, and its root is fine: then it should be cut.
And the operation on it is this, that you should consider before its operation, if the aposteme becomes quiet after a complete quietude, or is diminished by some diminution: then indeed make the sick person sit before the sun, and his head be in your lap; and open his mouth, and let the servant receive between his hands, and press down his tongue below with an instrument, of which this is the shape.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which should be as fine as a knife.
So when you press with it the tongue and the aposteme is shown to you, and your sight falls on it: then take a hook, and insert it into the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can, except that you should also pull some of its tunics with it.
Then cut it with an instrument, the shape of which is as follows. It resembles scissors; however its extremities should be curved, so that the opening of each is directly against the other's edge. Therefore ensure that it is made of iron of Indus, or steel tempered by immersion in vinegar.
But if this instrument is not present, then cut it with a scalpel, the shape of which is as follows.  /> It resembles a knife, however its extremity should be sharpened on both sides. Let it be sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And after you have cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same form of incision.
Then after the incision, let the sick person rinse with cold water or with vinegar and water.
But if bleeding occurs, let him rinse with water in which the bark of oak apples or myrtle leaves and similar astringents have been boiled, until the flow of blood is stopped; then treat him until he is cured.
And sometimes there arise on the throat other abscesses besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut those according to what I said about cutting the two tonsils evenly.
And indeed I myself have already treated a woman for an abscess that had arisen in her throat, which was drawing toward blackening, scarcely any sensation remained, and it had nearly closed the throat: and the woman was breathing with difficulty, and it had already prevented her from eating food and drinking water; and she had almost reached death if she had remained for one day or two.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent forth two branches, until they came out through both nostrils.
Therefore I hastened and inserted a hook into one of them. Then I pulled it, and with it was drawn out a large piece.
Then I cut off the piece where it had been caught at the nostril opening.
Then I did the same to what came out of the other nostril opening.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed down on her tongue; and then I inserted a hook into the abscess itself; then I cut off part of it, and only a little blood flowed out. Therefore the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, and then took food.
I did not stop cutting from that abscess many times over a long period; and the abscess would come forth in place of what I had cut, until it was stretched out too much along with her.
Therefore, I incised and cauterized the abscess in the throat.
It stopped from growing; then I left her side, and I do not know what God did with her after that.  /> 120.
¶ About the incision of an abscess on the lip.
Chapter 37
When the catarrh descends to the mouth and forms a swelling that is long, it is called "perpendicular." And if it is thick and round at the bottom, it is called an "uvula.
When it is treated with what we have said regarding division, and the treatment does not help, and you see that the hot swelling has quieted from it, and it is subtle, then you should cut into it.
And if from it a round swelling has formed, which does not have length, and is red or dark-colored or black, and does not have sensation, then you should avoid cutting into it. For in this there is danger for the patient.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner that I have said concerning its whiteness and length, make the patient sit before the sun; then press his tongue with the instrument whose description has preceded. Afterwards, insert a hook into the uvula and draw it down; then cut it with one of the two instruments which I have mentioned for the incision of the two tonsils. ︎
And you should not cut from it except that which is added beyond the natural limit without additional growth.
For if you cut more than this, you will harm the voice and speech.
After the incision, use what we have described for the incision of the two tonsils; and care for it until it heals.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, then you should use ingenuity in cauterizing it without fear or dread.
And the method of cautery for it is not except with sharp water, and this is that you place the patient's head on your lap. ︎ ︎ ︎ Then press his tongue with the instrument which I have mentioned. Then take from the water which I have mentioned to you in the chapter on cautery and make a cup not yet extinguished with it, and place it not too thick nor too thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, whose form is thus.
Let there be a depression at its end where the medicine is placed, like the hollow of a spoon.
And put the instrument with the medicine on the wound itself: and let the sick person lie on their side, so that the saliva runs out of their mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing from it descends into the throat and harms them.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you should do this on the wound for half an hour until you see it become blackened and the bite of the medicine has calmed down.
And if you wish to use a bandage, wrap it around the end of the spoon and pour the medicine over the bandage, then insert the spoon with the bandage into the wound until the bandage sticks to the wound.
Do this many times until you achieve what you want from the cautery of the wound. Then leave it; for it will dry up and fall off after three or four days.
If you need to renew the medicine, renew it; and after cauterization, clean around the wound with a bandage soaked in butter, and cleanse it with what remains of the medicine around it; and then rinse with cold water.  />
And let it be scraped externally with ointments, and internally with gargles, until it heals.
Therefore, this method of treatment is more wholesome than cutting and further from danger.
And sometimes the wound is also cured with something finer than cautery and cutting; for it is cured with fumigation, in this way. Take calament, hyssop, oregano, rue, saffron, chamomile, and southernwood, and similar herbs; and gather all of them or some of them in a pot and let them be soaked with vinegar and boiled; and the pot should be well daubed with clay; and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which should be placed a concave instrument in this way.  />  />  />
Let it be made of silver or copper, and its tip, where the seed is, should be inserted into the mouth of the patient until the vapor rises to the top over the tube, until the mouth becomes very dark.
Then repeat this many times on the same spot until it dries out.
And be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the abscess formation. For it often increases the swelling, and you should not do this unless the abscess is cooled down.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then take a reed and put the shell of an egg on its tip so that the patient's mouth does not burn, because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth, and this is a good treatment with safety.
¶ On extracting thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone or a fishbone gets stuck in the throat.
Therefore, you should extract whatever is visible from the throat after pressing the tongue with an instrument against the sun, and it will be revealed to you what is inside the throat. ➤
And whatever does not appear and is hidden in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food settles in the stomach. For perhaps the object will come out with the vomit.
Or the patient should swallow a piece of nap, or a lettuce root, or a dry biscuit; or he should take a small piece of soft sponge, tie it on a string, and then swallow it. ➤ When it reaches the place where the bone or thorn is, it will be pulled out, and then it will come out.
If it does not come out with what we have mentioned, then use an instrument made of lead in this manner. ➤ ➤
It should be somewhat thicker than the radius and have a curve at its tip, which it can easily insert into its throat; and it should have its head raised upward.
And let it be careful not to touch its epiglottis, lest coughing occur; and let it expel with it bone, or spine, or let the physician insert his hand into it.
But what the patient introduces is better; on account of his knowledge of the place where the thing is attached. And let it push downwards or draw its hand with the instrument upwards, all that according to the quantity prepared for it, until it comes out.
¶ On the Extraction of a Leech.
Chapter 39
When a leech is treated with what was said in the division of treatment with medicines, and it does not work, then observe in the patient's throat before or after the sun, after you have pressed its tongue with the instrument that I told you about.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook or with delicate tweezers appropriately.
If it is not possible with them, then take a hollow reed and insert it into the patient's throat until almost to the throat; then insert into the hollow of the reed a heated iron with fire. Do this many times, and let the patient abstain from water all day long.
Then let him take a glass full of cold water and open his mouth into it; and rinse his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and move the water with his hand one hour after another. For the leech, as soon as it feels the water, will fall out.
But if it does not come out with what we have said, then fumigate the patient with fleas or with a pan using the instrument that I told you about for fumigating the neck.  ·
"For this happens often, because it falls off.
"And the manner of operation in fumigation is this: take a pot with living coals and fire; and let the pot be covered with a lid, on which there should be a hole in the middle; and place the end of the instrument into that hole.
"Then throw the fumigation onto it, and let the patient place his mouth on the end of the tube and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. "For the leech will immediately fall off.
"If it does not fall off, then repeat the fumigation many times.
"And let the patient endure thirst, and eat salty foods, garlic, and do not drink water.
"It is therefore necessary that it should come out with this treatment.
"And this is the shape of the instrument with which the leech is drawn from the throat when it falls upon it.
"It is similar to tongs, as you see; except that it has this curve which is inserted into the throat. "And its end is similar to the beak of a bird called a stork, which has the roughness of a file; and when it grasps something, it does not let it go at all.
"Chapter on the opening of abscesses, their incision, and general care.
"Chapter 40.
"There are many kinds of abscesses, in many forms, as their remembrance comes to mind, that is, one and one in this book; and they differ in their opening and incision in two ways.
"One of them is due to the nature of the abscess itself, and because it contains moisture.
"And the second way is due to the places where it occurs in the body.
Because an aposteme occurring in the head is different from an aposteme occurring in the anus; and an aposteme occurring in a fleshy place is different from an aposteme occurring in the joints; and each of them has its own judgment based on its location.
And of the apostemes, there are some that should not be pierced unless after the maturation of the pus in them and its completion; and among these are those that should be pierced while they are still fresh and do not mature according to their time.
Just as are the apostemes that are near the joints.
Because when an aposteme occurs near a joint and its duration extends until it putrefies, the pus around it may corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint; therefore, it is a cause of closer contraction of that limb.
Or the aposteme is on a principal member; for if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either the member or through that principal member.
Or it is near the anus; therefore pierce it while it is immature: and you should not pierce a fresh aposteme that has not completed its digestion, except one that is near the anus, so that it does not putrefy deeply and extend to the inner parts of the anus, and become a fistula, or end up with something that does not heal.
You should know the time of perforating a matured aposteme according to its completion: and it is at the relief of pain, the subsiding of fever, and the reduction of redness and pulsation, and the sharpness of the head of the aposteme; and other signs.
And you should make the perforation in the lower part of the aposteme and its more prominent swelling; and let the perforation be along the length of the body if the apostemes are in the parts of the hands and feet, and the places of the arms, and tendons, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those that are reflected upon, go with a hole through that membrane.
When the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better to leave its perforation until its ripening is confirmed, as we have said according to completion.
For if you perforate it before that time, the course of the pus will be prolonged from it, and there will be much filth and stench, and perhaps its edges will harden, as well as its depth.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the body, as needed; or according to what the part needs.
And you should administer a small perforation in small abscesses and a large perforation in large abscesses; or many incisions similar to the size of the abscess.
And among the abscesses, there are some where it is necessary to cup the skin and cut it when it has become like a cloth, and its end has reached that which is already dead; as happens with many abscesses, such as with buboes and carbuncles.
And among them are those which are cut by a three-cornered incision. And one of these is the incision in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an abscess of the groin.
And among them are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are administered; and similar ones from the other incisions.
However, in an abscess that does not have a head, such as are the mild and superficial abscesses, it is necessary to perforate it with a superficial or flat perforation only.
And when the abscess is thick and has already collected much pus, and you perforate it, do not hasten to draw out all the pus at that time; rather, draw out some of it from it. Then fill the abscess until another day. Then extract some of the pus again.
Do this many times according to the degree, until it comes out; especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old person who has reached decrepitude, for the vital spirit of the living being is often released with the discharge of pus, and perhaps the patient dies and you do not perceive it.
Therefore beware of this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, through which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary to clean the wound and be mindful. Therefore, if the abscess wound is small or flat-cut, then apply a linen cloth or an old cotton cloth. And if the abscess is large and there are many openings from the perforation, then it is necessary to insert a cloth into each opening until they meet.
And if you cut off a part of the skin from the abscess or hollowed it out in its circumference, then it is necessary to fill it with old cotton or as when weaving a cloth or shaving linen hair dry and bind it until the third day.
Then throw it away and treat it with what is necessary from an ointment, until it heals.
However, if bleeding occurs during your procedure, then apply cold water and vinegar after the cloth has been soaked in them; and place it on the site of the bleeding many times.
However, if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary to apply a diet, treatment, and powders which I have described in many places of this book of ours, and from the division.
However, if it is during the winter and the site of the abscess has many nerves, then it is necessary to pour warm wine and oil over the spots and place them on the sites.
And if it is summer, and the places are fleshy, then you should put small cloths soaked in water and oil, or with wine and oil, and all of that cold. When it is the third day, as we have said, then you should release the abscess, and cleanse it, and administer to its treatment whatever is suitable for it, until it heals.
This, therefore, is what is necessary for the universal knowledge of treating abscesses.
According to the second half of the division, I have already spoken about every abscess, how it is the way to its treatment individually by chapter.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur on the skin of the head.
Small abscesses occur on the skin of the head, and they are of the kind called nodules, which are enclosed by membranes that are their sheaths: as if they were little pouches of a hen.
And there are many kinds of these.
Some of them are fatty; and some contain a moisture like mud; and some contain a moisture like kneaded bread or porridge; and others similar to these, and there are those which are hard as stones.
And in the incision of all of them, and their removal, there is no fear, provided that at their incision the artery does not cause a counteraction. ¶
And the operation in their incision is to examine them with an instrument, the form of which will come later, which is called a probe, until you know what they contain.
For if it contains moisture, then cut it with a longitudinal incision with a superficial incision in this manner.
And begin the incision from line b to line g. When therefore the moisture is emptied, then strip off the membrane that contains this moisture, and leave nothing of it behind. ¶
"For it often returns if something remains of it.
"Then dip the cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is available; and if not, in water with salt, and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. "For this will eat away what remains of the abscess.
"Then repeat the Egyptian ointment on the same cotton a second and third time, and if you need it, continue doing so until you are confident that nothing remains of the abscess; then treat the wound with ointment until it heals.
"If the abscess contains a fatty nodule, then cut it according to this form, with an incision in the shape of a cross; and place the hooks between the edges of the incision, and strip it from all sides; and strive with your skill to extract the membrane that contains it.
"But if a vein opposes you, then do what I will tell you afterwards.
"And similarly do this for an abscess if it is stony, by the same incision and treatment, as we have said.
"And the incision on a stony abscess is easier because it contains little blood and moisture. "And sometimes there are certain of these abscesses that occur in the head of some men, with which there is no moisture at all.
"This is because I cut into an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess to be like a solid and hard, rough, white stone that could not be broken; and if anyone hit it with something thrown at it, it would hurt them.
"In other abscesses that occur in the head, besides these, namely the abscesses that occur in children's heads and at the roots of the ears, let the incision on all of them be a superficial incision.
"However, place the perforation always from their lower part, so that the flow of blood is relieved towards the lower parts.
Then take care of it with what suits them in treatment.
§ On the incision of swellings in the neck.
Chapter 42.
These swellings often occur in the neck and under the armpits and groins; and sometimes they occur elsewhere on the body.
And among those that occur in the neck are one and many; and they arise from each other; and every swelling from them is within its own proper tunic, as it is with the nodes and swellings of the head, as we have mentioned.
And there are many kinds of these swellings. For some of them are stony; and some contain fluids; and some are malignant, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, when you see one that has a good arrangement and feels good to the touch, whose color is close to that of the skin and moves in every direction, and which is not attached to the nerves of the neck or jugular vein or artery, and it is not deep: you should cut it with a superficial incision from the upper part of the body to the lower part along the line b to the line g, and excoriate it from all sides. And spread the two lips of skin with hooks, or with many hooks if you need them, as we have said regarding the swellings of the head, and gradually remove it.
And it is important to take care that no vein or nerve be cut.  />
And let the knife not be very sharp; so that it does not give you trouble with the cut or disturb the patient, and so that you do not cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or artery and it hinders you from operating, then place ground zegi or certain powders that dry up blood in the wound, and bind the wound, and leave it alone until the swelling of the aposteme calms down and the wound softens; or it begins to want to become putrid.  />  />  />
"For blood is cut off by the skin, so return to your operation until you complete it.
"Then, with your index finger, check if any other small strophuli remain; cut them out and clean them.
"But if the strophulus has a large vein at its root, you should not cut it off from its root; rather, you should tie it with a strong double thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
"Then fill the wound with old cotton that you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. "Then tend to him.
"If the entire strophulus is cut off, then you should bring together the two lips of the wound and suture it immediately after ensuring that there is no remaining excess.
"But if you see that there is remaining excess due to the size of the strophulus, then it is necessary for you to intend to cut its root and extract it; and apply the suture as we have said.
"And for those strophuli that contain humors, pierce it with a superficial puncture so that you may know the place of its maturation; and make the puncture from what follows below in the body, as we have said.
"Then apply a poultice with ointment and similar substances after the puncture to corrode what remains of the corruption until you see that the wound is clean; then tend to it with regenerative flesh ointment until it heals.
Chapter XLIII
"About the incision of the epiglottis due to a swelling occurring in the throat.
"The ancients spoke of this incision on the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it; and this is their written account.
In those having synanche, it is necessary to avoid cutting the epiglottis, as it does no good; because all the veins and lungs are weak.
In those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no illness in the esophagus, it is necessary to use cutting of the epiglottis to avoid death from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we should cut the epiglottis under three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small section by width, in what is between two circles, such that the section is in cartilage, not in bone: and this place is suitable for cutting because it is free from flesh, and blood vessels are far from it.
If the doctor is timid, then he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook. Then let him incise the skin until he reaches the esophagus; avoid blood vessels if he sees any.
Then let him cut the cartilage as we have described; and it is indicated by the phlegm that comes out from there, along with its rupture, and the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
When the hour comes that you fear suffocation, bring together the two lips of the skin wound and close it alone without including the cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow until it heals.
Said Albucasis. The summary of this discourse that we have described is not except when they see that any one of these abscesses has blocked the throat of the sick person: and the sick person has been raised up to death, and his breath wishes to be cut: they go to cutting the epiglottis so that the sick person may breathe over the wound with some breath, and he may be saved from death. And because of this, they have ordered to leave the wound open until the malice of the illness is consumed, and its malice is three days or thereabouts; then therefore they order to suture the wound and treat it until it heals.
And that which I saw with my own eyes is this: a maidservant took a knife and sent it into her throat, and cut off a part of the lung's canal with it.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her moaning as if she were dying.
Therefore, I exposed the wound, and I found blood that had come out of it to be small. So I became certain that she had not cut a vein or an artery: and air was coming out of the wound.
I hastened therefore, and closed the wound, and cared for it until it was healed: and nothing happened to the maidservant except for a hoarseness in her voice; and after some days she returned to better health.
Therefore, I say that in cutting the epiglottis there is no fear.
On Cutting a Throat Hernia.
Chapter XLIV.
This tumor called a throat hernia is a large tumor, according to the color of the body, and it occurs very often in women.
And it exists in two ways: for either it is natural or it is accidental.
Certainly, the natural one has no intellect.
The accidental one, however, exists in two ways: of which one is similar to fatty knots. ‌ And the other way is similar to a tumor that occurs from the knotting of an artery. ‌ ‌ And there is danger with it, and one should not presume to approach it with a knife unless it is small; and you should test it and examine it with an intromitter. If you find it to be like a fatty knot and it is not attached to any veins, then cut it as nodules are cut; and remove it with whatever it contains of pus, if there is any pus; and if there is not, then explore all of it. Then care for the place with what is necessary for healing until it heals.
Chapter 45.
About the incision above the knuckles.
There are many kinds of knuckles, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division. And it is necessary for me to explain here the difference between a knuckle and a protrusion, since there is some confusion about this.
I say therefore that when a protrusion occurs, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the boiling over of the excess matter ceases and the process of decay is completed; then the fever and heat subside.
But with a knuckle there is no heat, nor fever, nor pain, and they are contained by the sac called sifachin, which is their own pouch, and it matches the color of the body. And their beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a fig, larger or smaller. 》
And they are in two types: either fleshy, or they contain moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I have said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the treatment of a knuckle, it is necessary to examine it and investigate it first with an instrument called "intromissum", as its form will be shown in the chapter that follows this one.
And the way to investigate all knuckles and tumors is as follows: take this instrument and insert it into the moistest part of the tumor that you find; and then slowly turn your fingers with it until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then place your hand according to the size of the tumor. 》 》
Then withdraw the intromissum and observe what comes out in its path.
If therefore liquid oozes out, whatever its color may be, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I have said for other tumors.
And if moisture does not exit after insertion, know that it is fatty; therefore, make a cut above it in the shape of a cross as I have taught you: suspend it with hooks and easily remove the skin from every part: and keep kistin over it, if possible, to extract it safely with a knot.
If the kist (i.e., abscess or swelling) bursts during the operation and it is not possible to extract it safely, for often this happens; then remove it in pieces until nothing remains of it.
For if some small or large part remains, the knot (i.e., the abscess) will return again according to the most.
But if it overcomes you and some part remains; then fill the wound at its completion with some sharp corrosive powders: and tie up the wound, and place a warm poultice on top of it; and care for it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Therefore, if it is a large knot (i.e., abscess), then either its two lips or care for it with that which cures flesh.
But if a contrarily pulsating or non-pulsating vein is present to you; then hasten and fill the place with crushed henbane seed, and leave it tightly bound for two days or three until the wound putrefies and the boiling of the blood calms down: then return to cut that which remains from the knot.
Chapter 46.
¶ Concerning external instruments for cutting and piercing.
OF THESE are the forms of the probes, and there are three types.
From these is a large one, from these is a medium one, and from these is a small one.  />
They should be made of square-ended steel, fittingly, so that they hasten entry into the abscess.
And these are the forms of Experiences, and yes, that is, piercing: and one of them is called beris, which means, piercing: and there are three kinds of it.
Of these, indeed, there is the large, and the medium, and the small.
They are suitable for investigating apostemas, exiturae, fistulas, and buboes due to what is between the bones among them.
And others are made round, smooth, and blunt, like the large needles with which cushions are stuffed, from citrinum, or bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the best of them are those made from citrinum.
And experiences are also made from black lead, which are suitable for investigating fistulas, whose cavity is a reflection, so that they may be reflected with their gentleness and those reflections.
And there are again three kinds of them. Because from these [types] there are the long ones, and the medium ones, and the short ones, according to the width of the fistula and its narrowing.
The form of knives, and they are of many kinds.
Because from these [knives] there are the simple ones, that is, with one curvature; and they are of three kinds as you see, large, medium, and small.
And from these [knives] there are blind knives; and they are of three kinds.
And from these [knives] are hooks with two curvatures, and they are of three kinds; and from these are hooks with three curvatures, and they are of three kinds; and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels, with which nodes and apostemas are cut and scraped; and they are of three kinds, because from these there is the large one, the medium one, and the small one.
Their tips, which are used for cutting, are sharp; the tips of others are not sharp: and they are not placed this way except to bring benefit during the scraping of nodes due to fear of incising a vein or nerve; and so that through them the patient may heal and find temporary relief from the burning sensation found in the scraping of an aposteme.
They are made of bronze and resemble a ray; with it, one makes alcohol. At the tip, there is a hidden spatula-like point, and it runs to the inside or outside when desired, as you see.
The shape of the spatulas that are hidden among the fingers during the perforation of apostemes; and patients do not notice them; and there are three kinds: large, medium, and small.
The shapes of the ventoses with which the flow of blood is cut off; and there are three kinds: large, medium, and small.
They are made of bronze or brass, round and somewhat long, as you see; and they should be thin.
And it is necessary that you have these two kinds of ventoses, large and small, so that you can quickly cut off blood when needed, especially when the medicine is not present.
Nevertheless, you should not use them to cut off blood in every part of the body, and you should only use them in fleshy areas such as the thighs of the legs, hips, arms of the shoulders, breasts, belly, groin, and similar fleshy, moist places.
And sometimes they are made into other small instruments resembling chestnuts according to their shape; with them blood is cut off when it flows from a place of phlebotomy, or during the incision of a vein or artery; and sometimes they are made round according to this shape.
Chapter XLVII.
On the care of the nipples in humans which resemble the nipples of women.
Sometimes the nipples of certain individuals swell at the end of sleep (that is, when they begin to ejaculate during sleep) until they resemble the nipples of women, and they remain disgustingly prominent.
Therefore, if one is repulsed by this, it is necessary to make an incision above the nipples in the shape of a lunula, according to the following form from line b to line g. Then, remove all the fatty tissue. Then fill the wound with the flesh-forming medicine. Then join the two lips of the wound with a stitch and care for it until it heals.
If the nipple inclines downwards and softens due to its size, as happens with women, then it is necessary to make two incisions on each of its superior sides in the shape of a lunula, each meeting at the ends of both, so that one line contains the other, according to this form from line b to line g. Then, remove the skin between the two lips and take away the fatty tissue. And administer what we said about stitching and powders, which is necessary for healing, until it heals.
If you do not complete the incision as required due to the discomfort of the patient or excessive bleeding, then it is necessary to fill the wound with cotton soaked in a caustic ointment and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fatty tissue. Then care for it until it heals.
¶ On the perforation of an abscess that occurs under a ticklish spot.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses that occur under ticklish spots are of the hard kind of warts, which contain a pouch, and among them are those which contain moisture.
"Therefore, those that contain humors should be cut with a lunar section, according to this form: from line b to line g. When everything in the abscess has been evacuated from pus, then fill it with old cotton and leave it until another day.
Then remove the cotton and scrape with an appropriate ointment until it heals.
If the abscess is of the type called "nodules" or "strophulus," then cut over it as we said for strophulus, equally.
If the wound spreads to the point of emitting pus, then apply a cautery to it, as we said previously.
Chapter XLIX.
§ On the incision of an abscess that occurs due to artery and vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the skin above it becomes consolidated, then very often an abscess occurs from there; and similarly, this happens with veins as well, so that an abscess and swelling occur in them.
And the signs by which it is known that an abscess and swelling are due to an artery or vein are: for when an abscess is due to an artery, it is long and deeply seated in the body; and when you press on the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a grating sound.
And that which is due to a vein is round and situated superficially in the body.
And incision over this abscess is harmful, and especially if it is under the armpits, groins, neck, and many other parts of the body; and it is very dangerous. Therefore, one must avoid treating it with a knife, and those that are also in the extremities or head must be avoided.
So, for those that occur from the swelling of the opening of an artery, make a cut in the skin along its length. ⋅ Then open the incision with hooks; afterwards the artery, and free it from the tunics until it is exposed.
Then introduce a needle under it and make it penetrate to the other side; and constrict the artery with a double thread in two places, as I have taught you in the extraction of the two temporal arteries.
Then puncture the place that is between the two ligatures with a probe until all the blood flows out of it and the abscess dissolves.
Then apply a treatment that generates pus until the ligatures fall off; afterwards treat it with suitable ointments for it until it heals.
But if the abscess is due to a vein section, then you should hold as much of the abscess with skin as you can in your hand. Then introduce a needle under the place which you have held in your hand and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it until you draw it out from the other side. Then bind the abscess with the same ligature as you bind the grape ligature, from both sides with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread may be drawn out, then introduce another needle with another thread under the whole abscess at the place of the first needle; press and tie your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle until what is inside comes out; then cut off the excess skin and leave what has been bound from it.
Then place upon it a little piece of cloth which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then apply a treatment that is made with linens and ointments until it heals.
Chapter 90
¶ On the abscess which occurs from twisting of nerves.
As an aposteme occurs in arteries and veins, so also does it occur in nerves; this happens to them from a blow or from excessive labor and similar things; and it is most common in the places of the joints, or the heel, and in every place where the joints move; and it is a grave aposteme.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is generally painless in most cases; but when pressed with intensity, the patient feels a sensation like numbness.
And the aposteme is not deep within the body but rather under the skin; and it moves to all parts of the body, except that it does not go forward or backward, neither to the front nor to the back.
Therefore, when such an aposteme is found in the joints, it should not be pierced with a knife. Perhaps then contraction may occur.
However, if it is found in the head or forehead, incise the skin with a razor. If then the aposteme is small, hold it with forceps and cut it off from its root.
And if it is large, then hang it with a hook and scrape it away; afterward remove it, and close the wound with sutures; and care for it until it heals.
Chapter LXI
¶ On the incision of an aposteme that occurs in the abdomen.
It happens to some people many times in their abdomen and other parts of their body that small lumps appear, which are called fungal, because they resemble fungi.
Whose root is delicate and whose head is thick, and their lips are turned back.  /> ¶ De sectione thelili, quod accidit in ore. And some of them are small; and others grow very large.
And I have seen a man, and in his abdomen there were two lumps, which were more like fungi than anything else: there was no difference between them; they were white, with a small root, and their lips turned back, and they were moist, and moisture always flowed from them.  /> ¶ De sectione thelili, quod accidit in ore.  /> Et est ex eis paruum: et est ex eis quod magnificatur ualde.
I cut them open and found that one weighed nearly 10 and 8 ounces, the other almost 6 ounces. The procedure for cutting into them is as follows: first observe if the patient is moist and has a whitish-lilac color; if so, they are humid and have delicate roots. In this case, cut the patient with a broad spatula, and have cauterizing irons ready in the fire.
Often indeed a great deal of blood is expelled when cutting it open; if the blood overwhelms you, hasten to cauterize it then.
If you see that the patient is cowardly and afraid of the incision with iron, then take a neatly made lead thread and tie it around the area as we have described, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the tying of the lead. Do not cease from tying the lead whenever it prints on the area, until it is cut off and falls away by itself without labor.
If, however, the area has thick roots, then there is some danger and fear in cutting it open; especially if it is in the abdomen.
However, it is possible to cut half of it open. Then cauterize it so that it does not return.
Then care for him until the wound heals.
And beware of presuming to cut open a black-colored area with little sensation and foul appearance; for it is a cancerous abscess, and I will remember Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
¶ About the care of the navel projection.
A navel projection occurs for many reasons. ■ Or from a tear, which is on the abdomen; and from it comes out either an intestine or intestinal content, as happens with other tears. Or from blood that flows from a vein, or an artery, as mentioned before. Or from constricted flatulence within it.
If it is due to a fistula and the discharge of pus: then the color of the swelling resembles that of the body, and it is soft, without pain; and its position varies. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestines, then its position has a greater difference, as we have described. Therefore, when you press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it increases greatly at the entrance to the bath, and causes great labor.
But if it is due to moisture, it is soft; then when you press it with your hand, it does not disappear nor become constricted, nor does it increase.
If it is due to blood, then with these signs the swelling appears dark red, as if from a cliff.
But if it is due to dead flesh: then the swelling is heavy, thick, hard, and remains in one quantity.
If it is due to flatulence, however, then its touch is soft.
And the operation in it is, that then you should consider whether the swelling of the navel is due to blood from an artery or vein, or flatulence; then you must abstain from treating it. For there is danger and fear in it, as I taught you in the chapter where I spoke about swellings due to an artery and vein.
But if the swelling of the navel is due to the intestines or pus: then you must advise the sick person to hold his breath and stand straight. Then mark the signs with cautery all around the navel.  ?> Et praecipe ei, quatenus non uadat ad balneum, neque respi-scant, aut lauant uenter suum per diem. Then instruct him to lie on his back between your hands over his back. Then encircle it with a wide bandage around the navel over the place you marked with cautery. Then extend the middle of the aposteme upwards with a large hook. Then tie it tightly around with thread or silk cord; and let the knot be alexostate.
Then open the middle of the extended aposteme above the ligature. If you find that it has already been captured by the ligature, then remove the alexostate and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine back inside the abdomen; and if you find the zirbum, then take it with a hook and cut off its excess.
But if the contrary is an artery or vein to you, then bind it very tightly. Then return to your procedure. And take two needles and insert two strong threads into them; and insert the two needles into the circle you made around the aposteme in the form of a cross, which you make to penetrate.
Then draw the aposteme taut in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles and leave it until the extended flesh becomes rotten and falls off by itself; or cut it when it softens and rots.
Then treat him with whatever is necessary from the salves until he heals.
But if the protuberance of the navel is of fleshy growth or of moisture therein, then you should make the aposteme concave, as we have said. And you should draw out the blood, or moisture, that you find in it.
Then treat him with what solidifies the place.  •
Chapter LIII.
§ On treating CANCER.
We have already spoken about the types of cancer and how to treat it with medicines; and we must be careful not to use iron for its treatment, lest it ulcerate; and we spoke about cancer generated in the womb and the caution necessary for its treatment.
The ancients said that when cancer is in a place where it can be eradicated, such as in the breasts or thighs and similar parts of the body from which it can be removed entirely, especially if it is small and just beginning, ...
... when it becomes advanced and large, one should not approach it.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it; nor have I seen anyone before me who has reached that point.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you should precede and resolve the patient with black bile several times. Then perform a bloodletting on him if you find him with clear signs of fullness.
Then prepare the patient with the preparation in which he will be located for the operation.  />
Then place hook-shaped instruments into the cancer that fit it. Then hollow it out from all sides with the skin, according to the investigation, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood run, and do not cut it quickly, but press the places and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever is possible there among the instruments.
If, however, the flow of thick blood from the cutting of an artery or vein causes difficulty for you, then cauterize the vein until the bleeding stops. Then care for him with the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
¶ On the Treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter Forty-Four. We have already narrated in the division of the types of dropsy, how water is collected; and all the signs of every type, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with the iron is only the sole type of ascites; and one does not approach with iron the type of tympanites; nor the type of hypersarcus completely, for that is fatal.  /> ♥
"When you have been treated for this kind of hydropic tympanites with medicines, and your treatment does not take effect; then be aware. "For if the patient has already become weakened, or there is another infirmity besides hydropis, such as coughing, or loosening of the belly, and similar things; then beware of treating him with iron, for there is danger in that.
"But if you see the patient is of abundant strength and there is no illness with him except hydropis alone; and he is neither a child nor an elderly person; then the manner of operation on him is that you should lift the patient, standing between your hands, and the assistant behind him should press his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the abdomen.
"Then take a thorny probe of this shape. "Let it be of two sharp ends, long, with a sharp tip like a probe. "However, let there be some gradual narrowing in it, so that you do not penetrate with it during the operation all the way to the intestine and injure it.
"Then be mindful. "For if the hydropic growth comes from the part of the intestines, then it is necessary that you extend the incision from the navel by three finger widths, straight down to above the pubic bone.
"But if the cause of the hydropic growth is due to liver disease, then let your incision be on the left side of the navel by three finger widths.
"And if the cause of it is due to the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side; and it should not be an incision on the side where the patient wishes to lie, so that the discharges do not flow to that weak place.
"Then pierce all the skin with the instrument. Then insert into that incision, and lift your hand with a spatula between the skin, and scrape, as if you are removing it: and let the amount removed be the size of a nail or about that.
Then pierce with the scalpel until the spatula reaches the empty place, and it is the place of water. Then extract it, and insert into the opening an instrument of this form. And it is similar to a cannula: made of silver or bronze or brass, smooth and polished; at its lower part is a small opening, and on its sides are three openings, two on one side and one on the other side; and its end is cut in the manner of cutting reed, as you see: at its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it immediately comes out above the instrument, therefore evacuate from the water in one hour a medium quantity.
Because if you evacuate more than is necessary from it in one hour, perhaps the patient will die from a weakening of his vital spirit, or a syncope which approaches death will occur to him.
Indeed, evacuate from it according to the power of the instrument, and according to what the patient's condition indicates to you by his pulse and by its goodness.
Then remove the instrument, and keep the water.  />

Then draw it out and make the urine run through the path of urine: and repeat with the syringe, and do not cease doing this until the bladder is emptied, and let the patient find relief.

Chapter 60.
¶ On the Extraction of Stones.
We have already spoken about the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and I have explained the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and one in the bladder, along with its symptoms: and I have spoken about a stone that can be removed by hand or incision; this is a stone properly formed in the bladder. Therefore, I say that a stone generated in the bladder most commonly occurs in children.

Therefore, when we come to the cure, it is first necessary to cleanse the patient with an enema that removes all the feces from his intestines; for this itself sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone at the time of examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet, and shaken, and moved to a lower place, so that the stone may descend to the bottom of the bladder, or sometimes leap from its high position.

For perhaps the finger which is in the anus presses the stone toward the incision, and it comes out without difficulty.

And if it is smooth, like a hazelnut, and round, its exit is easy.

But if the stone is more than one, then expel first the large one toward the mouth of the bladder. Then cut over it. Afterward, expel the small one after that; and do similarly if there are more than two.
If it is very large, it is ignorance to cut over it with a large incision. Because to the sick one of two things happens: either he dies, or there occurs to him a continuous dripping of urine; therefore, because the place does not heal at all.

Chapter 61.

Chapter LXII.
¶ Concerning the incision upon a hydrocele.
A hydrocele is nothing but a gathering of moisture in the white membrane called the sifac, which lies beneath the skin containing the testicle: and it is named the sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature has prepared for it in the part of the egg until it becomes hard: and this is another egg, and it lies between the white membrane that we mentioned and the skin of the testicle: and this occurs rarely. ›
And indeed the origin of this vein is from the weakness that occurs in the testicles; therefore, this material flows to them. And sometimes it happens due to a blow on the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors. For indeed its color is inclined toward a yellowish hue, or it is blood red, or dark and dusky, or watery white, and the one that predominates is this last kind.
And the signs by which you can know where the collection of water is are these: For if it is in the white sac which we have mentioned, then the swelling is round and a little longer, like the shape of an egg; and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in its own little bag, then the swelling is round like part of an egg, and because of this a man thinks that there is another egg there.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sac, then it falls under the senses.
Therefore, when you wish to know the color of the moisture, then test the color of the moisture in the swelling with the square tool, whose shape has been mentioned before.  />  And if it is red and dark as we have said, or yellowish or white like milk, it is healthy; but if it is black, it is corrupt. It resembles the Greek letter.
