For of these there are Fistulas which reach the large veins or arteries, or nerves, or spleen; or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to some of the ribs; or to some of the vertebrae of the back, or to some of the compound joints by composition; such as the hand and foot. And there are those which go towards the vicinity of the principal member, and similar ones.
Therefore, among these Fistulas, the one of this kind is difficult to cure from illnesses and does not accept treatment, and dealing with it is laborious and ignorance.
But if there is any of the Fistulas whose depth does not reach these places which I have mentioned; and you hope and trust in its treatment, and you wish to attain knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron or silver, if the Fistula extends straight, and search it with this.
But if there is any crookedness in the Fistula, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead. Because lead, on account of its softness, adapts and curves around the crookedness.
If the Fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to indicate upon it with a probe. Therefore, irrigate one of its openings from its openings.
For the moisture by which you irrigate it goes towards other openings and runs out from them.
Then carefully investigate how it is possible for you: so that you may know whether there is an orifice or a nerve there, and the cavity of the Fistula is distant or near; or whether it is one Fistula which has many openings, and stand over all of it, with your utmost strength, with your questioning of the sick person, and with the discovery of pain upon pressure with your hand on that place, and similar signs and causes happening to an abscess.  />  />
When you have stood over all those things with true steadiness, then proceed to treatment with confidence: and it is that at that time you should consider whether there is a visible Fistula nearby or in a safe place far from the joint, or from the nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places I mentioned to you. Then cut the Fistula as described in my account, and remove what is in it from filtration and corrupted flesh, which comes out of its opening from flesh, pus, and similar things: and care for it until it heals.
If the Fistula is deep and straight, then you must cut it as deeply as possible. Then cleanse it from all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply strips wrapped in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the Fistula which you have grasped with the iron; do this several times until the sharp medicine has corroded all that remains in the cavity of the Fistula from corruption.
Then heal it with ointments that produce healthy flesh until it heals.
But if it does not heal through this, then cauterize it as we have described.
If the cause of the Fistula is a bone, and you have ascertained this, then examine it, and cut it as I have told you: if nothing prevents you from the vein, or nerve, or main member, as we said. 
If the bone is exposed and there is some corruption and blackness on it, then scrape it until all its corruption has been removed. Then heal it with what causes it to become fleshy until it heals.
If it does not heal and exudes pus as before, then you should know that you have not completely removed all the corruption.
Therefore, clean it thoroughly a second time, and carefully make an incision into it, and cleanse it with all your strength.
Then restore it. If it heals, that is good; but if not, then know that the corruption is deep within the body, which you have not reached. You have no skill in that, unless you leave it to nature.
But if the wound is small and possible for you, attract it with fine forceps that are suitable for it.
But if there are many bones, then carefully attract all of them, and do not leave any out with your strength.
But if anything resists you because of moisture and there is no fear or danger in cutting it, then cut the moistures and flesh if they are there and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at that time, then place on the wound something that will cause it to putrefy; and leave it until what is around those bones of flesh and moistures has putrefied.
And watch the wound so that it does not become healed. And let the incision you have made in your procedure be tightened. Put on it, if you wish, a cloth soaked in ground sulfur mixed with oil; or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or any of the green ointments.
For when those moistures have putrefied and the bones are separated from them, their removal and attraction is facilitated.
But if the wound is one large wound, such as a thigh or hip wound and the like, and if what has become corrupted from it is a large part, then scrape it with the utmost care until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then heal the wound.
If however the corruption is in it and the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to saw through the bone and cut it entirely up to the place where the corruption reaches; then heal it until it heals.
And I tell you about a ulcer that happened to a man on his leg; so that you can use it as an example and assistance for your procedure.
There was a young man about thirty years old to whom pain occurred in his leg, from a cause that moved within him inside the body, that is, from internal causes, until the material reached the leg and formed a large swelling: and there was no external cause for this.
Therefore, time passed with him in error by the physicians, until the swelling was opened and many materials flowed out with it. And his treatment was neglected until the leg formed a fistula and many openings were made in it; all of which discharged pus and bodily fluids.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no one among them skilled in the art of surgery, until I was called, and I saw his leg and the materials that flowed from those openings in large quantities; even the entire body of the man had already become emaciated, and his color was yellow.
I therefore inserted a probe into one of the openings of the places, and the probe reached the bone.  (lit.: "and the probe came to the mouth")
Then I examined all the openings and found that they communicated with each other from all parts of the leg.
I hurried therefore and cut over one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone; and I found it corrupted, which was already eaten away and blackened, and putrid, and perforated until it reached the marrow.  (lit.: "until it had penetrated up to the marrow")  ()
I have discovered and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I determined that there was nothing in the bone except for that corruption which I cut out and sawed off. And I have already removed it.
Then I applied a restoration to the wound with medicines that promote healing for a long time. And it has not healed.
Then I tried again and uncovered the bone a second time above the first exposure, and I found a continuous corruption with the bone, and I sawed off what appeared to me from that corruption as well.  />
Then I attempted to restore it. And it was not restored.
Therefore I uncovered it again and did not stop cutting the bone into pieces and trying to restore it, and it was not restored, until I had cut off about one hand's breadth of bone with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines and it healed quickly and was cured. And this repetition of the operation and cutting of it would not have been necessary, except for the condition of the patient's weakness and his small capacity to endure, and my fear for him of death, because he fainted from the excess of the evacuation at every hour with great difficulty.
Therefore he was cured with a complete healing: and in the place of the bone, hard flesh grew, and a good constitution developed in his body, and his strength returned: and he returned to his usual habits, and it did not cause him any harm at all in walking.
But if the bone protrudes from a place that was already fractured, then you should saw it off in this manner: and that is, take a bandage and tie it around the end of the protruding bone, and command someone to pull it upwards, and put another bandage of thicker wool underneath the first bandage. Then tie it on the flesh that is above the bone, and extend its end to draw the flesh downwards, so that you expose the flesh over the place you wish to saw, lest the saw injure the flesh.
Place a stick or board under the bone below in an orderly manner, because when you do that, it will not prevent the sawing of the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be done on the corrupted place for a short while, so that corruption does not befall the concavity of the bone; and so that it does not become apparent to the senses in its appearance, compelling another part to be sawed instead.
If the bone is corrupted and not prominent, but rather one of its parts is connected to another part, and the corruption is in the middle or in a part of it, then expose all the flesh from every part completely. Then place a stick below.
Then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing continues to the other side; and let the sawing be according to the length of the corruption for a short while, as we have said.
But if the corruption is at a joint, cut the corrupted joint and saw the bone where it continues on the other side.
But if the corruption is at a continuation of two joints, then there is no skill required except scraping.
But if the corruption is in the comb of the hand or the comb of the foot, then the matter in it is very difficult. Truly, you must examine how the corruption appears to you. And scrape it and clean it in whatever way is possible for you and with whatever skill you can manage, when a vein or nerve does not stand in your way.
And know that there are many instruments and saws for cutting these bones according to the position of the bones, their preparation, their thickness or thinness, their size or smallness, their hardness or softness. 》 Therefore, you should prepare an appropriate tool for each type of operation.
And know that these operations indicate to you the form of the tool you need when your study is prolonged. And the knowledge of the methods of this art is evidence of the methods of illnesses, for then you will find through yourself that which is suitable for each from the tools for every ailment.
And I confirm to you at the end of this chapter the number of tools, which you will set as examples on which you will operate, and the test by which you will experiment on others. 》
The shape of another saw.
The form of the scissors for joints. Let the head of this scissors be according to the form of the head of a star-shaped nail, and its points according to the form of the points of a spindle whorl.
And it is not suitable except for when the heads of joints are rubbed with it, when they become corrupt or when the mouth is wide and large.

The shape of a small and fine-pointed pair of scissors.
This resembles a rib, and its end is like a saw: it appears in many places due to the reason of bones.
The shape of the scalpel fits the nature of what has been penetrated by the bones.
Its end is triangular and sharp.
It comes from India. And likewise, all that we have said must be made from scalpels, incisors, and saws.
¶ On the incision of limbs and sawing of bones.
Limbs become putrid either due to an external cause or an internal one.
When you have treated that corruption with medical treatment and it does not help, and you see the corruption spreading to the limb without any cause; then you must incise that limb up to where the corruption reaches, so that the patient may be saved from death or from a greater defect of the limb.  />
And the sign of this is that the limb becomes blackened until it stinks; whether fire has burned it or it has become putrid after the blackening. Until that putrefaction reaches what follows the limb and spreads throughout the whole body.
Therefore hasten with its incision, and likewise if the cause of the corruption is from the bite of some venomous things, such as a sea-scorpion, viper, asp, or similar.
If the corruption or bite is on the tip of a finger, then cut off the finger and do not leave any space for the corruption to spread to the rest of the hand.
And likewise if it occurs on the hand, then cut it off from the root of the wrist, and do not leave any space for the corruption to spread until it reaches the wrist.
And if it invades that part, then cut off the arm at the elbow joint.
If corruption passes through and you see it about a foot long: then in that is the death of the sick person. And to administer beyond that from treatment is the quantity of strength.
And likewise do for the foot, when corruption invades a toe, then cut it off at one of the joints; and if it reaches the sole of the foot, then cut off the whole foot. But if it rises to the knee, then cut the leg at the knee joint.
And if corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill [to save it], except dismissal and leaving the patient to death.
And the way of cutting off a limb or sawing it is to tie a ligature below where you wish to cut: and tie another ligature above that place. 》 》 》 And let the assistant stretch one ligature downwards; and let the other assistant stretch the upper ligature upwards. And you separate the flesh that is between the two ligatures with a wide spatula until all the flesh is laid bare. 》 》 》 》 》 》 Then let it be cut or sawed.
And it is necessary that you place a linen cloth from all sides so that the saw does not touch the healthy place; and a secondary injury may occur to the patient, and a hot abscess [may form].
But if bleeding occurs inside while you work, then quickly cauterize the place; or put on it certain powders that cut off the blood. Then return to your treatment until it is complete. 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable ligature and care for it until it heals.
And I tell you what happened to a man on his foot, this same thing that I am telling you about. 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》
What this means is: "This is because a blackening occurred in his foot due to the burning that resembles fire's burning; and that corruption first affected his toe until it began to affect the entire foot.
Therefore, the man cut himself after he saw that the corruption had fallen on his member with great force, which he felt through pain and burning; and it fell upon him at the joint, and he was cured.
And after a long time had passed, that same corruption occurred in his index finger of his hand.
So he came to me, and I tried to cure that excess with what I applied on his hand from medicines after cleansing his body; and the excess did not go away, but began to spread into another finger until the corruption affected the whole hand.
He then called for me to cut off his hand; and I refused, hoping that from me the excess would be cured; and I also feared that he might die if his hand were amputated. Because the man's strength was greater than his condition.
After he lost hope in me, he returned to his homeland; and I heard from him that he cut off his hand entirely; and he was cured.
And I have not told this story except to be of help for what falls under this type of illness; and that it may be understood and acted upon accordingly.
§ On the Treatment of Gout and the Preparation of Purgatives with Medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus; then it bursts or is perforated, and what is in it exits with the pus, and there remains a hollow place as if it were a vessel; and the skin above it is like a thin cloth that has been worn away: and the corruption does not reach its end, nor does it press into a bone, nor into a nerve, nor into a ligament; then it deserves to be called Sinus; and it is not called Fistula. However, if its space is prolonged until it presses into one of these members, then it is called Fistula or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut the skin completely as it rolls: and especially when it has been made thin, and turned into cloth: and you can be confident that it will not adhere to the place, due to its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and that this final corruption does not follow: and if there is thickness in the flesh of the skin, then treat it with an enema: and consider it towards the cavity.
Then if it is large, and the pus that comes out of it has a foul odor: then enema it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, oil, and quicklime, the parts being equal, combined in a vessel: and boil them over fire until the medicine turns red: and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then take as much of it as you need: and dissolve it with water and honey, and enema the cavity with it: and close its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the space of two hours.
Then withdraw it with an expression: do this every day until the cavity is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it should be enemaed with water of ashes, when this medicine is not available to you: and it is that you take ashes from grapevine wood or ashes from holm oak wood: and pour water over them. Then strain it, and enema with it until you are confident that the cavity has been washed out.
If the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or ash water, then enema it with water and honey, in which some crushed quicklime has already been mixed. Or enema it with honey and wine mixed together, because of the property of honey, which cleanses and washes: and wine makes the cavity adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you do that several times and are confident that the corruption of the sinus has been removed, then irrigate it with what makes flesh grow therein.
And it is necessary to take some of the plaster called diafinicon; dissolve it with rose oil and stiptic wine; or irrigate it with certain medicines and other ointments whose properties I have written about in the treatise on ointments.
But if the orifice of the sinus is narrow and does not accept the cannula for irrigation, then briefly enlarge it with an iron instrument; or place in it a wick wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in basilicum ointment, until it is dilated.
And similarly, if the orifice is also wide, then gather its lips with a stitch; and let out from it the quantity that can enter without increasing the irrigation.
And similarly, if the orifice from which the pus flows is raised higher; then cut it in a lower place within it, so that the pus may flow downward; for when the pus is constricted in the depths of the sinus, it prevents flesh from growing therein.
But if it is not possible for you to cut the sinus lower as you wish; then try to prepare the member with a preparation that allows the pus to flow easily, according to what is prepared for you. Try that nothing at all from the pus is retained therein; and take healing ointments which are placed on the sign, suitable for what you wish. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it covers the entire sinus. Then cut with scissors a hole directly into the orifice of the sinus, slightly larger than the orifice of the sinus itself.  /> Then spread over another cloth some of the soft ointments according to the size of the hole; and place it on top with a long-lasting position far from the opening of the sinus, so that the exit of pus from it may be alleviated; and do not remove the large cloth except after several days, once a day.
But the one that is over the opening of the sinus is the one you remove every hour so that what accumulates in it may exit; and so you may know whether that which runs out of it is much or little: or digested or undigested.
And with this, again consider the location of its sinus, whether its master feels pain there or whether there is no pain there and whether it has a swelling there or does not have a swelling there.
Therefore, when your treatment is extended in this manner and you see the matter that comes out over the opening of the sinus, then know that the sinus is already adhering or approaching adhesion; therefore release it in the morning or on the third day. And when you release the wound, then change another cloth and renew the ointment.
But if after a long time a thin pus comes out of the sinus, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching; since among men there are some who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds and there are those who act contrary to that.
But if after many days undigested pus comes out of the sinus, then know that the sinus is not adhering.
If therefore the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its process is prolonged; then in drying medicines, let the doctors make them so that they are humid in their substance and dry in their power; such as the ointment dyafinicon, when a superabundance of zegi is added to it.
And from the last chapter to be cured is this: take diaphanic ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, then sprinkle on it ancient wine equal in substance to itself.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And now make something similar to this procedure when you do not have diaphanic ointment: take honey and cook it until it thickens, then use it. Or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense: grind all of them together and sprinkle the powder over honey while it is on the fire. Then spread it on a cloth and tie it over the ulcer; and smear it with the honey you cooked until it thickens; and sprinkle powdered spices on it, and place another cloth over it, and tie it on.
And sometimes it is given in that long-stemmed birthwort, and the root of celestial lily, which is lily; and flour of herb, and centaury. Use them either simply or compounded; grind them and sift them; and sprinkle the powder on the honey I mentioned, and use it.
But if the ulcer is prolonged and not cured by this treatment, then know that there is corruption at its depth; or it has already made an impression on the bones, or other hard parts of the body, or ligaments; then cure it with the treatment for fistulas as was previously mentioned.
§ On the care for whitlow and a crushed nail, and an added finger incision and section.
Whitlow is an abscess that arises under the thumbnail of the hand or foot, and perhaps it may arise in other fingers as well.
So when its condition is prolonged and its care is neglected, it becomes an abscess with hot inflammation; or it becomes corrupt and emits pus until it erodes the root of the nail; and perhaps the corruption affects the whole nail; and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone so that there is a foul odor; and the end of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark. 》
When therefore you cure it with what we have said in division, and your treatment does not help, then you should cut off with a knife all the remaining overgrowth from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cautery is very healing.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy, and the nail's additions are already pressing into the flesh within, and they begin to sting it and harm it: then you should place a thin wire under the corner of the nail that is stinging the flesh, and push it upward; and cut off that part of the flesh with ease, and put on what remains of the flesh from medicines that burn and corrode until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
But if the corruption has already penetrated into the bone, then you must cut out that bone and remove it; for it will never heal as long as there is a corrupted bone within it.
But if you see the corrosion and corruption spreading into the finger, then cut it off at one of its joints, as was previously mentioned for its remembrance. Then treat it until it heals.
But if there is injury or bruising of the nail and it causes intense pain, then you should bleed the patient first. Then cut the nail with a sharp blade across its width from top to bottom as needed.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for by doing so you will cause intense pain to the patient and it will lead to the addition of new flesh in that place.
Then treat the area with something that eases the pain.
But an additional finger that is born in the hands of some people is perhaps entirely fleshy; and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps a nail grows on it; and sometimes its birth occurs at the root of a joint, or it is born in some of the finger's joints. "> That which is born at certain nodes of the finger does not move; and that which is born at the joint of the finger, perhaps moves.
Therefore, the incision of that which comes from it is easy. Which is, as you cut it at its root with a wide spatula.
However, the treatment of that whose birth is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, refrain from incising it.
But in that which is born in the finger at the last of the nodes, then you should cut its flesh with a round incision to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws that fits it. Then care for it until it heals.
However, the stiffening that occurs between fingers often happens either from birth, that is, it is born with it, or from the stiffening of a wound, or from burning by fire, and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut away that stiffness until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place between them bandages or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not quickly stiffen again, and separate them; or place between them a thin sheet of lead until the place consolidates as it should be.
And similarly, if stiffness occurs between some fingers with the palm, then cut away that stiffness according to how it is prepared and appropriate for the shape of the limb.
¶ On the Incision of Birthmarks.
Birthmarks are swollen, thick veins filled with melancholic humors that occur in many parts of the body; and their outcome is most common in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, workers, and those who carry heavy loads.
You should therefore administer purging of the body with black bile frequently and strongly.
Then perform bloodletting on the patient from a basilic vein.
However, their treatment with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they are cut and black blood is drawn out. And the other method is that a vein is elevated and the entire thing is drawn out.
Its cutting is according to this manner. Soak the leg first with very warm water until the thick, muddy blood dissolves. Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of their thigh to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three, with a wide incision.
Then elevate, that is, draw the blood into your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and first the phlebotomist should bleed the patient. Then cut the nail along with the lower part until an amount of blood that you see is sufficient and that the strength of the patient can bear comes out.
Afterwards bind it, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that generate black bile, and repeat evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are filled and it harms the patient.
Its elevation is according to this manner. Shave the patient's leg if it has many hairs on it. Then place the patient in a bath, or soak the leg in hot water until it turns red and the veins become engorged, or have them exercise strongly if there is no available bath, until the limb becomes warm.  />
Then cut the skin along the length of the vein's incision, or at its end near the knee, or at its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and strip the vein from every part until it becomes perceptible to the senses; and at its appearance you see that it turns red to black. When it comes out of the skin, you see that it is white like a string.
Then introduce under it a rod, until when it is lifted and comes out of the skin; suspend it with a blind hook, gently.
Then cut another incision near that incision by three fingers' breadth. Then strip the skin from above the vein until it appears. Then lift it with rods as you did before, and suspend it with another hook as you did at first.
Then cut another incision, or more incisions, if you need that. Then lift it and cut the last incision at the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision that is above it.
And do this until you pull it out of the higher incision, or all the incisions, until it comes out completely; then cut it off.
But if it does not obey you in being pulled out and lifted; then introduce a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and pull it out; or insert a rod under it: and turn your hand to every part until it comes out. And beware that it is not cut.  /> If you can't extract the vein as described above, use a strong needle with double thread and tie it around the vein. Then pull it out gently. Alternatively, insert a rod under the vein and turn your hand in all directions until the vein comes out completely. Be careful not to cut the vein during this process. For if it is cut, it will be very difficult for you to lift it up, and a great harm will come upon the patient from that.
Therefore, when you have pulled it out completely; then place on the wound site a wool cloth soaked in rose oil or oil; and care for him until he is healed.
If the tendon is contracted and has a twist such that it winds around parts, and is not straight as we said before: then you should cut through it at every point of its twist and places of its winding.
Then suspend it with hooks until you raise the whole thing: and be careful not to cut a vein or cause injury at your cut, for it will be difficult for you to raise or extract it. Therefore, be wise.
The Shape of the Incision by Which a Tendon is Treated.
The Shape of the Blind Stitch.
It does not have a curve like the other stitches, nor does it have an acute tip so as not to injure a vein; and it is of thick, flexible material, gentle: because if it were thin, it would cut through the vein with its thinness; rather, let there be some excess thickness in it, as we said.
On the Extraction of a Vein from the Leg.
This vein is found in the legs in hot countries, such as Alsace and the Arab lands, and in dry, hot countries of little fertility, that is, where neither grass nor trees grow.
And perhaps it occurs in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from the corruption that occurs under the skin, just like the corruption that occurs within the body with snakes and roundworms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein's occurrence is that a severe swelling occurs in the leg.  /> Then the place becomes blistered. Then the vein begins to come out from that place of blistering, like a root of a plant or an animal.  />  />
Therefore, when its end appears, then you should wrap a piece of lead around it, weighing from one and a half drams to two and a quarter drams, and tie it; and let the hanging lead be suspended from the leg.  />
Whenever something comes out from it to the outside, wrap it in lead and tie it off. If it is extended greatly, then cut off a part of it, and wrap up the rest. And do not cut it off completely until it has come out entirely. For if you cut it, it contracts and goes into the flesh, causing an abscess and rotting in that place, and a bad sore.
Therefore, it is necessary that it be gently handled and drawn out little by little until it comes out completely from its end, and nothing remains of it in the body; and this vein, in some people, comes out to a length of five palms and ten. And now it has come to me because it came out of a man from twenty palms.  />
But if it is cut during your treatment, then insert a rod through its opening and pierce it with a long hole, along with the body, until everything that is in it from matter is evacuated; and apply rotting of the place with medicines for several days. Then treat him with the care for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot itself; and many openings occur on it, and a branch comes out from each opening.
Therefore treat it as we said in the division, and with what was stated before.
¶ On the cutting of worms generated under the skin, which is called the disease of cows.
This disease is named in a certain land among us the cow's disease, because it often happens to cows, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.  />  />
And it goes through the whole body, ascending and descending; and it is perceived by sense during its motion from member to member, until it bursts into whatever place it bursts on the skin and comes out.  />  />  />
"And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. "Just as worms and snakes, that is, leeches and roundworms, occur in the stomach.
"And it is feared because of its malice. "Because when it moves through the body and is raised to the head and reaches that place, then perhaps it opens there and exits, destroying the eye; and this often happens.
"Therefore, when you wish to cure it and remove it, this can only be done during its movement and appearance to the senses.
"It is therefore necessary that you tie what is above it and what is below it with a strong ligature. "Then cut above it and extract it.
"But if it has penetrated deeply into the flesh and you do not find it, then place a cautery with fire on the spot until it dies.
"And what is more to be feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
"But if you see that it has already reached the head near the eye, then tie a good ligature around the eyebrow below it. "Then cut above it and extract it.
"And the patient should use purification of his body with medicines that dissolve bad, putrid humors.
"And he should avoid foods that cause putrefaction.
"Concerning the incision for Nakir's disease.
"This illness is called Nakir in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain members; then it changes from one member to another.
"And I myself have already seen it, as I am now telling you. "I was called to a sick woman in certain villages. "And she revealed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the cord of the arm vein." ‌
And after one hour had passed, I saw a swelling advancing with a torch, just like a worm, ascending to his shoulder more quickly than it possibly could be: as if it were living silver, running from place to place.
Therefore the pain retreated from that place and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I waited one hour, and it ran into the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then he told me that it was revolving throughout his whole body, as I saw.
Therefore I marveled at the speed of its change from member to member. And indeed, before that, I had not seen this illness with my own eyes in such a way. However, I have seen many who find pain changing from member to member; and I did not see it with my own eyes as I saw it in the woman. 》
And I do not think that is so unless it is because the woman was one of those who are from dry-bodied people with exposed veins; therefore it appears to the senses that the gaseousness has been changed.
And it is necessary that they do not appear according to this reason in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
When therefore you wish to cure it and the patient feels that pain, then if it is appearing to your eye as we have said, then bind swiftly above it and below it; and cut above it until the gaseousness emerges constricted; and cauterize the place.
If however you do not see it with your own eyes, then treat him with a body purge and with that which expels gaseousness, and mixes them with foul pills and pills of celery seed and the like.
On the Extraction of Arrows
Arrows are distinguished by their various types and the places where they strike the body.
For there is a diversity among them, since some have large tips and others small ones; some are hollow, and others blunt. And among them are those that have three points. And among them are those with tongues (barbs). ))) And among them are those with stars (fletchings).
But those that fall according to the members where they strike are of two kinds. ))) For the members are either from the main hollow organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidney, intestines, bladder, and similar ones. ))) )))) ))) When, therefore, it falls into one of these organs and the signs of death, which I will tell you about later, appear to you, then you must avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows the person in whom it is found in many cases. But when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden deep within the organ, then extract it and care for the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it and pierces through the bone and exits above the brain are: because a severe headache occurs from it, along with blindness, dizziness, redness in the eyes, swelling, redness of the tongue, spasms, confusion of the mind, and vomiting of bile. ))) ))) )))))) And perhaps blood may exit from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off and the voice is destroyed. ))) And from the place of the wound a white moisture like boiled starch exits. ))) ))) And it comes out of it resembling wateriness of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and extract the arrow if it does not come out.
The signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart are that it is near the left nipple and the person feels with it as if it were fixed in a hard substance, not an empty one. And perhaps there is a movement of the arrow similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood runs from the wound. And it is followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting spells. Know therefore that death has certainly come.
And the sign of an arrow that has wounded the lung is the issuing of frothy blood from the wound; and the veins that follow the neck swell up, and the color of the patient changes. And he breathes out a large, deep breath, and seeks cold air to breathe in.
If the arrow falls into the diaphragm, which is in the chest, then it is near the small ribs; and there is a great panting with intense pain, along with sighing; and all the limbs of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested comes out of the wound with it.
And if the arrow falls into the belly and is fixed there, then something comes out of the wound - either feces, or a piece of intestine that has already been torn. There is therefore no hope in treating it or in extracting the arrow.
If the arrow falls into the bladder and urine comes out, and something protrudes to the outside, and the pain is stronger upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
The remaining limbs, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and arm, and scapula of the back, and collarbone, and hip, and leg, and similar members are healed in many ways when the arrow does not find an artery or a nerve; and the arrow is not poisoned.
And I tell you that I have seen these arrows, so that you may indicate through it the treatment for yourself.
What happened was that a certain man had an arrow strike his tear duct at the root of his nose. So I removed it from the other side, from under his ear's fat: and he was cured, and no harm came to his eye.
And I removed another arrow from a Jew that had struck him under his lower eyelid in the eye's fat: and the arrow was already hidden, and I only got out of it a small part of its end, to which its wood adhered: and the arrow was large for Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there are no barbs.
So the Jew was cured, and no misfortune befell his eye.
And I removed another arrow from a Christian's throat.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and that is the kind with barbs. So I cut above it between two guides [muscles]: and it was already sunk into his throat. So I made myself fine with it, until I removed it: and the Christian was cured.
And I removed another arrow from a man that had struck him in his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed and nothing about his condition changed, I cut above the arrow: and I inserted a probe into it, and I removed it: and he was cured, and no misfortune befell him.
And I saw a man into whose back an arrow had struck, and the wound over it had healed. After seven years had passed, the arrow emerged from the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman into whose belly an arrow had struck, and the wound over it had healed, and the arrow remained within her: and nothing about her condition changed, and the woman found no harm in any of her natural functions.
I saw another man who had an arrow fall into his face; the wound became healed and remained, causing him little pain. And there were many similar cases like these.
I removed an arrow from a man who was one of the King's nobles; it had fallen into his nose and had already deviated slightly to the right side, and the entire arrow was hidden.
Therefore I was called for his treatment after the fall of the arrow until three days later. I found that the wound from the arrow was very tight.
I sought to examine it with a fine probe, but did not feel anything; and he himself felt the puncture and pain under his right ear. Therefore I hoped that the puncture was from the tip of the arrow.
I plastered the place with a plaster that had attracting and digestive power, confident in myself that the spot would swell up and give me a sign of the arrow so that I could cut over it; but nothing happened in the place indicating that the arrow had reached there.
Therefore I continued to plaster it with a plaster for many days; and no sign appeared, but the wound became healed in the meantime. And he remained weak from its extraction for some time until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. And he told me this.
And he told me this. I placed a sharp, caustic medicine on that wound for many days until it opened up; and with a fine probe I probed it, and felt the tip of the arrow which was attached to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared visibly; and for me, a period of about four months had passed with him.
After the wound had become enlarged and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, but it did not comply with coming out; then I did not stop seeking ways and contrivances over it until one day I began on it with well-made forceps, as will appear in their form at the end of this chapter, until I removed it. "> Posui ergo super illud uulnus medicinam acutam, corrosiuam diebus multis, donec apertum est: et cum tenta tentaui ipsum, et sensi extremitatem sagittae subtilem quae adhaeret in ligno. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed physicians judge that the nasal cartilage cannot be restored. Therefore I restored it [the nasal cartilage], and the wound was healed, and the patient was cured with perfect health; nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the method of extracting certain arrowheads, so that you may take this as a rule and interpretation for what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their divisions are not included with the narration; nor does the book contain them, and a skilled craftsman compares what is present to what is absent, and finds a new operation and a new instrument for unusual occurrences in this art when they happen.
I say therefore that arrows do not come out of the members to which they are fixed except in two ways. Either by pulling from the place through which it entered.  /> Or by cutting through the opposite side of the limb.
And that which comes out from where it entered is either an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place, and is pulled out, and comes out.
But if it does not submit to coming out from the hour in which it fell, then you must leave it alone for several days until the flesh surrounding it putrefies; and its removal is relaxed and its exit facilitated.
And similarly, if it is fixed in a bone and does not submit to coming out, then leave it alone also for several days; and continue to pull on it with every movement every day until it comes out.
But if it does not submit to coming out after several days, then you must bore around the arrow in the bone from every side with a fine awl until the arrow is enlarged. Then pull on it and extract it.
But if the arrow is already hidden in the body and concealed from sense: then seek it with probes. But if you feel it, then extract it with certain instruments that are suitable for its extraction.
But if you cannot reach it because of the constriction of the wound and the length of the arrow in depth, and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then enlarge the wound over it, until the wound is widened and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
But if there are any barbs with which it is held, then free the attached flesh from all sides by every means you can, and use ingenuity, if it cannot be pulled out because of the tearing of the flesh in the fracture and twisting of the bones, until it comes out.
And you, when you administer the extraction of an arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand with forceps in all parts, until you make it come out: and act subtly at last with great subtlety, so that the arrow is not broken, and the extraction of itself becomes difficult for you.
If you cannot heal it within one hour, then leave it for a few days until what is around it rots. Then revisit it: for the skin will be relieved. But if you experience a flow of blood, then administer what was said about treatment in its chapter: and guard your strength from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And administer skill in every way possible for the arrow's escape: and let it be with ease, tranquility, slowness, and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary that when you pull the arrow, you make it weak according to the shape as it was at the time of the arrow's entry into the body. Because this is more beneficial. But if that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
If the arrow comes out from the opposite side or if something already protrudes to the outside, or if the tip of the arrow causes a swelling that you can feel through the skin above it and you see it protruding, then cut over it, and let the incision be as wide as forceps can grasp.
Then pull it out, for it becomes easy to exit.
But if it is stuck in the bone, then twist your hand according to its roundness until the arrow impresses itself into the bone and enlarges its own passage; then pull it out.
And if not: leave it for a few days; then revisit it until it comes out.
If the arrowhead is wooden, then push it through with it.
But if the wooden part has broken off and you wish to administer a push: then introduce into it a concave instrument so that its concavity fits into the arrow's shaft; then push it out with it.
If however the arrowhead is concave, then push it with an entering tool into itself. The arrow is relieved by that.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cut out all of the flesh in which there is venom: if you are able to do that. Then treat it with what is appropriate for that.
But if the arrow falls into the chest or belly or bladder or side, and it is close to that which you touch with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Then stitch the wound if you see it and treat it until it heals.
And this is the shape of forceps with which arrows are extracted.
The ends should be like the beak of a bird, made of iron, and when they grasp onto the arrowhead, they do not release it.
And from them many forms are made: large and small and medium-sized, according to the size and slenderness of the arrow, and the width and depth of the wound.
The shape of a concave pusher.
And the shape of a deaf pusher.
This is the end like a radius, so that its entrance into the concave arrowhead may be relieved and pushed with it.
¶ On bloodletting of veins.
There are thirty veins that are commonly used for bloodletting in the body.
Of which there are sixteen in the head: two pulsating veins that are behind the ears, called the two herbs; and two arteries that appear in both temples; and two veins that are in both tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called the "watchers"; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein that is at the tip of the nose. /> And the two jugular veins that are in the neck. And the two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And the two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And the two veins that are under the tongue.
But the veins which are bled in the arms are five in each arm.
One of them is the cephalic, and it is from the side of the woods; and the common people name it the vein of the head. And the dark one. And it is the median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic; and the common people name it the vein of the body. 》 》 》 》 And the basilic, and it is placed on the domestic side; and it is also named the tickler. And the common people name it the vein of the belly. And the tendon of the arm, and it is placed on top of the faucet; and that is the one which is bled, and it is that which appears above the thumb in a clear manner.
And the little savior; and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the smallest one; and there are two branches to it.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curvature of the thumb on the side of the woods. 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 And the second one is the saphenous; and its place is near the heel on the domestic side. And the vein called nessis; and its place is by the heel on the side of the woods.
And in the other part of the leg there are three similar veins. 》 》 》 》 》 》 》
¶ On the bleeding of the two veins behind the ears.
Cutting the two veins behind the ears is beneficial for old catarrhs, migraines, scabies, and old sores of the head.
And the manner of bleeding it is as I relate: And it is done by shaving the head of the patient, and rubbing his occiput where the two veins are with a rough cloth until well rubbed. Then let the patient tie their neck with a band, until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears, in two depressed areas of the head. Therefore seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark there with a cautery.
Then take the phlebotomy knife, which is called an incision knife. Then insert it under the vein into the skin, until the phlebotomist reaches the mouth of the vein. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with the skin using an incising cut.  And let the blood flow from the vein until it flows of its own accord.  Then take a cotton bandage and bind the wound well with it, so that no air may reach the wound. And let the length of the incision be about the size of two joined fingers, or thereabouts; and let out as much blood as you wish.
Then bind it with wool bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And now, at the beginning of this chapter, there was described their incision and cauterization.
The bleeding of the two arteries which are in the temples is beneficial for old migraines, crooked squinting, chronic blepharitis, and the flow of sharp excess fluids to the eyes.
And the manner of bleeding them is as I shall tell you. The patient should tighten his neck greatly until two veins become clearly visible to the touch and their pulse is felt under your finger. Then mark those signs with cautery.
Then lift the skin above the vein with your index finger and introduce the lancet underneath it, lifting the vein upwards. Cut it as you did with the two other veins, and let the blood flow according to your need.
Then release the patient's constriction and place your finger over the vein for one hour. Then place a bandage and some wool over it, and tie it firmly with a constriction: leave it until it heals.
And now, the mention of them, their cutting, and their extraction has preceded at the beginning of the book.
The benefit of bleeding the temporal vein is after cephalic bloodletting for old facial diseases, and for migraine and redness, and foul ulcers. ›
And its quality of bloodletting is as I tell you. The patient should constrict his neck with a cord until the vein appears. › › Then take the instrument called a scoop. › ›
Place the pointed tip of the scoop over the vein and strike it with a comb or something similar to it; allow the blood to flow according to your will. Then release the patient's constriction and tie it firmly until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide lancet. However, it should not be a sharp-pointed lancet like the others; rather, it should have a broad point. › › › And with bloodletting she is according to transfusion, because the mouth is nearby; and perhaps the bloodletter will break there when it is delicate.
The treatment of the two veins that are in both lacrimal orifices is for the eye due to scabies and redness, and for inflammation in the eye, and diseases of the face.
And the quality of bloodletting them is that the patient should tie their neck with a bandage. Then bleed them, and you should be standing over their head.
And let the bloodletting be along the length of a little, with a small bloodletter, somewhat wide, for there is no flesh in that place. For if the bloodletter is delicate at the tip, perhaps it will break.
Then let as much blood flow out as you need. And place a poultice over them and bind them for one night, and then remove it.
The bloodletting of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and severe headache, and diseases of the face, such as migraine and redness that occurs on the face; especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of this bloodletting is that the patient should tie their neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long bloodletter, and insert it into the middle of the nostril itself, between what divides the nose lengthwise. ¨ Because the vein is not apparent there by touch, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you should remove your hand with the bloodletter a little and let as much blood flow out as you need. ¨ ¨ ¨ ¨ ¨ ¨ ¨ Then tie it up for one night. For it is quickly restored.
But Guidi's two venesections are beneficial for treating shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, melancholic illnesses, impetigo and severe ulcers, and gnawing pains, that is, herpes zoster.
And the method of these venesections is that the patient should draw tight under both armpits with a ligature; and the practitioner should stand over the patient's head, and the patient should be sitting on a seat.
Then make a small incision in the vein to the length of a wide venesection. Then draw out a measured amount of blood, or as much as is necessary for that purpose. Then do the same with the other vein.
Then loosen the ligature and tie both veins tightly in the middle, so that the patient is not suffocated.  And leave it like this until morning. For the wound heals.
§ On the venesection of the veins, according to al-Zahrawi. Al-Zahrawi's venesections are beneficial for treating mouth sores and rotten gums, severe ulcers and lip fissures, as well as those in the nose and around it.
And the method of these venesections is that you should make the patient sit before you and bind their neck with a cord. Then turn the patient's lip inside out and look for the two veins, one of which is on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and both are distinguishable by their dark color. This is true, because around them there are small, black veins.   Cut both with intersecting cuts.
But if you are in doubt and do not know which one it is: then make an incision into the larger and more prominent of the two.
And do the same with the two veins that are in the upper lip; for usually it runs with that one, and make an incision into the two veins that are in the lower lip.
On venesection: The vein under the tongue after a venesection of the cephalic vein. The venesection of the two veins under the tongue is beneficial because the cephalic venesection is for afflictions of the throat, sore throat, and mouth diseases.
And the method of venesection is as follows: let the patient sit between your hands facing the sun; lift up his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for another vein; and both will be dark in color.
Venesection them both; and take care not to finish too quickly when venesecting them. For there are arteries beneath it, and possibly a hemorrhage might occur from those arteries.
The three veins that are venesected in the elbow run with custom in all people. ¶ ・
And their venesection is done in two ways. ・ ・ ・ Either by pricking with a broad myrtle venesection needle, or with an olive wood needle sharpened to a point, or by cutting with a venesection knife, which is called al-nasil. And this is its form:
And from them are wide and thin varieties according to the width and narrowness of the veins. The form of the myrtle venesection needle should be broad, as you see. ・ And it is suitable for opening hollow veins, full, protruding, visible, large, and those which contain thick, cloudy blood.
And this phlebotomy is of lesser width and more delicate in its ends.
And it is suitable for phlebotomy of fine veins that contain fine yellowish blood.
However, the basilic is one of these three veins whose phlebotomy is beneficial. Because it draws blood from the illnesses that are under the throat and neck, since it follows the chest and abdomen: and it is fitting for the phlebotomist to be careful when phlebotomizing her, because under her there is an artery. If therefore he errs and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will cut that artery, and a flow of blood will occur. Therefore it is necessary not to phlebotomize her with the phlebotomy needle; rather let him cut with a lancet.
If the basilic does not appear with a manifest appearance, then it is necessary to divert her and change to another [vein], or seek some branch of her; or in place of her phlebotomy, the vein of the arm. For she is manifest, and cut her with the lancet phlebotomy, as we have said.
If you wish to perform the phlebotomy of that same [vein], then it is necessary before constriction of the arm, so that you touch the place, until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark the place with a cautery, afterwards bind the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using the lancet phlebotomy, as we have said. And beware that the blow does not fall through the space from the place of the artery.
When you see, as you tie the bandage, swelling in the place that you marked with a cautery iron, then that swelling is artery swelling: therefore avoid it.
If you see blood spurt like a boy's urine, and it is thin and red, then know that it comes from the artery. Then hasten and place your finger on it for a long time, and then remove your finger. If the blood is cut off (for often it is cut off), then bind the arm and leave it, and let the patient beware of moving it; and let him be very cautious about it.
And he should not move it for several days until it heals.
But if the blood does not stop and overcomes you, and there is no medicine at hand, then cut the artery if it appears to you.  /> Et ligabis eam cum digito: et adiunges super ipsum cataplasma, quae est ex pane farinaceo. For its ends are contracted and the blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of a fig tree and grind it; take one half and bind it over the place of the vein with a suitable bandage, along with threads and small pieces of cloth until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, that's good. And if not, then care for it with the same method mentioned before regarding the dust that cuts off the flow of blood; and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in most cases because of the smallness of the wound and the possibility of binding it from the arm.
The venesection of the dark vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; therefore, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic vein and a branch of the cephalic vein, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist that he be above fear of bleeding from her, because under that is a nerve. Therefore if he adds to the puncture of the bloodletting and finds a nerve, it causes numbness in her, and healing becomes difficult, and perhaps she does not heal at all.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. But if it is hidden in certain people and is subtle and does not show itself, then you should place your bloodletting where you bleed her, with a section by the Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with all your might.
But if the vein is between two nerves, then find the vein along its length.
¶ Concerning the cephalic vein. But the benefit of bleeding the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and helps with eye diseases. ↑ Return to text
And it is necessary in this vein, if you wish, that you bleed her by pricking with the olive phlebotomy needle or with the myrtle phlebotomy needle, because it is safer than all the other veins, because there is no artery beneath it, nor a nerve. ↑ Return to text
However, it is fitting for you during the bloodletting that you avoid with the phlebotomy needle only the head of the biceps, and seek a softer place: and it does not harm her if she is not found on the first blow, but rather it is repeated over her several times with the phlebotomy. ↑ Return to text ↑ Return to text
However, perhaps it swells in certain people when she is not found on the first blow, but that swelling does not harm her at all. ↑ Return to text ↑ Return to text
¶ Concerning the usefulness of bloodletting and the precautions that must be observed in it. The quality of bloodletting and its effects are what you should consider in order to correct them.
The first thing you should know is that bloodletting should only be administered for the preservation of health and to maintain its continuance, and as a precaution against the onset of illnesses.
And for bloodletting to be done in one of the three veins located at the elbow: the cephalic, the dark, and the basilic. And bloodletting should be done at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, in summer and autumn also on an empty stomach; but in winter after eating, when there are signs of fullness.
And let bloodletting be done on the first or third day, after three hours have passed since dawn.
However, bloodletting administered for illnesses does not have a fixed time; rather, it should be done whenever the circumstances and necessity dictate, whether at night or day, and at any hour and season.
And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should the elderly, who have already passed sixty years of age, be bled.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled for any reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels before the bloodletting with a gentle enema, if there is a large amount of retained feces; so that during the bloodletting, the veins do not draw in putrid waste from the intestines which can harm the principal organs.
Nor should a patient who is feeling nauseous or intoxicated be bled, nor someone who is hungover, until these conditions are removed from them. Nor should a patient with dropsy.
And bloodletting should be avoided again after suffering from colic, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual activity, labor, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, and anything else that weakens the body or spirit.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before that; if the blood is thick with food and drink, and medicines: if it is possible for him.
Then let him enter the bath, if nothing prevents him; or he should be exercised in some manner, so that the blood may be refined.
And place his phlebotomy on the chest, around the third hour of the day, as we have said; and strive that his chest be evacuated that day from all evil animal passions, such as constriction, anger, and fear; and from all physical evils, such as labor, pain, excess, and similar things.
And he should make present to himself things whose use has become habitual among people because of their fragrance and pleasant smell, such as festivity and similar things, in a quantity suitable for him.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a stool that is higher than the stool for bleeding; afterwards he should extend his arm and rub it himself with his hand two or three times. Then let him tighten the cord with a strap and wind it around twice.
And let the tightness be moderate, for if the tightness is uneven, or too tight, then it hinders the flow of blood; and if too loose, then it also hinders the flow of blood.
Then after tying, it is necessary that the person to be bled rub his hands together until the veins swell and become apparent.
Then let the phlebotomist cleanse the site of bleeding with a small amount of old oil properly.
Then let him place the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself, a little below the place where he wishes to make the incision, so that the vein does not flee and the blow is steadier. Because the veins are like a fleeing string during bloodletting; and there is one full of airiness; and when you place a lancet on it, it contracts under the lancet, deceiving the bloodletter, and the lancet does not open the vein; and if it opens it, it is narrow. Therefore, it is necessary that the bloodletter be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then he should put down the lancet. If the vein is opened on the first attempt, then it is good. And if not, then try it again a little below that place, or above it with haste; if the place does not become swollen.
If it becomes swollen or the patient is frightened, then leave him for a day or two, and let the bandage not be tight. Perhaps indeed a warm poultice will draw out the swelling. He should not enter a bath.
Then he may repeat the bloodletting, if he wishes.
But if the opening is small or the blood runs thinly; and you fear that not as much blood will come out as you wish: then try the lancet again in the same hole, following its straight course, and add a little to your work.
And do this quickly, before the place becomes swollen; for a swollen place is common in many people during a small operation.
If you see that it has already become swollen, then do not try again at all on the same place; for it does not excuse you from anything. And put some olive residue on it; because it relieves the flow of blood. It is better for this purpose than oil itself and than other oils.
And similarly use olive residue in all your bloodlettings, where you hinder the flow of blood; and they do this with theriac of alfarach and with sagzenea, when one of them is placed on the spot.
The blood is refined and dissolved when it is thick.
If an abscess occurs at the site of venesection, which often happens especially to those who are not bled except on that occasion or if the opening of the vein is small, then hurry and place a sponge soaked in water and salt, heated slightly, over the spot: and tighten it for one hour because it dissolves.
And you should do that after the blood has exited from the vein itself, at its completion or from another vein.
If something black or green remains in the spot after a few days, it does not harm; and if you wish, place on it some dissolved aloe or myrrh, or some juice of mint, that is, pennyroyal, or similar to it.
And indeed, abscesses and swellings often occur at the site of venesection on the basilic vein.
Therefore place your hand over it; if you find softness upon pressing it, then that swelling is bad. Beware of placing on it any of what we have said. For perhaps blood from the artery will flow from it.
But make a plaster for it with what has an astringent property, so that the spot may harden. Then care for him with the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
And it is necessary to extract from every person a quantity of blood according to his strength. And that which appears from the color becoming over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow out until it becomes red; and likewise if you see that it is thick, then let it flow until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, let its sharpness be taken away.
And he who is full-bodied, strong, and needs a bloodletting once a year, should have the phlebotomy performed on the large vein, and let the phlebotomist be on the side: and in one who is weak, it should be the opposite of that.
And it is appropriate to extract him at intervals, and that the opening should be narrow; and it is better that what is administered in the phlebotomy of the vein should be a transverse cut, not a prick.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from the flow of blood and from cutting the nerve; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy through length and width.
And he who is accustomed to fainting at the time of phlebotomy should eat before phlebotomy a little bread soaked in pomegranate juice, or if he is warm, let him take a drink: and draw blood from him three times, or four.
And if he is of a cold temperament, then let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in mulberry syrup, or in honey-syrup flavored with spices, or in aromatic fragrant syrup.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy, and the cause is the loss of much blood, then let him drink meat water and a thin fragrant syrup: and use aromatic spices with aloes, and anoint his chest with them; and use the other things that we have mentioned in the division in the chapter on fainting from evacuation.
But he who wants to draw blood from his arm and loosen his blood a second time, for him whose phlebotomy is because of much evacuation and whose strength is weak, let him loosen the blood little by little according to the amount of strength, over continuous days.
"Whoever wishes to heal their arm and reduce blood on the second [day], and their body is strong: then let them do it after seven hours or nine from their first phlebotomy.
"But whoever wishes to draw blood from their body toward the opposite side of where it is inclined: it should be treated on the second or third day.
"But in whose body the blood is already heated and sharpened, causing fever to occur: it is necessary that blood be drawn from them once, and a large amount be extracted; and the opening should be enlarged until syncope occurs. After preserving all the conditions of phlebotomy and placing your hand on their pulse along the course of the blood, so that death does not occur in place of syncope. "For this often happens when the person drawing the blood is ignorant and careless.
"And it is not appropriate, when one wishes to heal an arm and draw blood on the second day, after the vein's opening has already closed and blood flow becomes difficult, to press hard on it with force and quickly stretch and bend [the arm]. "For that is very harmful; indeed, leave it alone until it is bled the second time.
"Or remove with the sharpness of the phlebotomy the clotted blood from the vein's opening and place on it some salt dissolved in water. "And place on it some of the tyrica al-farukh or sajzeena, and place a gentle pressure on it until the blood comes out.
"But if the vein has already formed an abscess, then leave it alone and do not cover it until the abscess settles.
"But if necessity compels you to draw blood on the second day and there is no excuse, then either phlebotomize above that same place or in another arm with another vein.
The vein of the arm is bled in place of the Dark and the Palace, when they are not found or hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the manner of bleeding it is that the sick person puts his hand in warm water until it reddens and the vein becomes clearly visible.
Then it should be tied above with a moderately tight knot. Then make the incision crosswise little by little, not widthwise, nor lengthwise; and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be such that it is made over the wrist slightly.
But if the flow of blood is prevented, then repeatedly put your hand in warm water and let the blood run into the water until you achieve what you need.
However, if it is a summer day, then you may dispense with repeating the hand in warm water. And indeed the running of the blood into hot water is especially useful in wintertime.
And this vein's bleeding is safer than all other veins, because there is neither a pulsatile vein nor a nerve beneath it. 》
However, the bleeding of the little finger on the right hand is beneficial for liver diseases.
And the manner of its bleeding is that you tie the little finger with a string or your hand after putting it into warm water until the vein swells and becomes very noticeable to touch. Then make the incision crosswise. And if you cut it entirely, it will not harm it at all.
And be careful not to finish the bleeding while your hand is still being bled. Underneath it are the bare nerves of the fingers, exposed from the flesh. 》 》
Then put your hand back into the warm water and let the blood run into it. For if you do not irrigate it with hot water, the blood clots at the vein's opening and is prevented from flowing.
So when you have extracted the necessary amount of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not clot quickly.
And similarly, you should do this with every tied-off branch.
The remedy for bleeding that vein from the left hand is this: since it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen; and similarly do for its bleeding as you did for the second one equally.
The remedy for bleeding the saphenous veins is for diseases of the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb and retention of menstrual flow, and kidney disease, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar ailments.  />
And the procedure for bleeding that vein is as follows: let the patient put his foot into hot water and rub it until the veins swell; then tie above the ankle with a bandage. And indeed, the place of that vein is at the heel, visible toward the thumb; and many branches spread out from it on the surface of the foot.
Therefore bleed it either in its larger branch or at the heel where it joins; for this is better and safer.
But if you bleed it on the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your bleeding wound where you want to bleed it from the side, as if you wanted to cut it; and let the size of the incision be a Nesil.
But if the flow of blood is blocked, then immerse his foot in hot water and allow the blood to flow into it until it is evacuated.
If the bloodletter errs in the first attempt and pierces a vein, he should repeat the phlebotomy slightly higher up, for the place is safe, and you need not fear any harm if you avoid the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot evenly.
The place for the sciatic vein, as we have said, is by the heel on the outer side of the foot.
And the benefit of phlebotomy from it is for pain in the ankle when due to hot blood.
And the method of phlebotomy from it is that the patient should enter a bath and be in a hurry; and bind the leg from the side of the ankle up to above the heel with four fingers' width, using a fine long bandage, for it does not appear except for this reason.
When it appears, then phlebotomize it by whatever method is possible for you, or by transfusion; and it is better to cut it with a cut or to divide it with a division. For its place is safer, and the vein itself is very hidden in many people.
For its place is safer, and the vein itself is very hidden in many people. If you do not find it and it does not appear to the touch at all: then phlebotomize one of its branches, which is that one visible on the back of the foot, towards the little toe; and beware of the nerves, and let as much blood flow as you like.
Then loosen the bandage, and place a piece of skin over the site of the phlebotomy, and bind the site. For it heals quickly.
¶ On Cupping and its Usefulness
Cups are made from horn, from metal, and from glass.
And cupping is done in two ways. ■ "With scarification and bloodletting: another method is cupping without scarification.
"And this cupping that is without scarification is performed in two ways. "Either it is with fire, "Or it is without fire.
"And cuppings that are administered with scarification and bloodletting are in fourteen places of the body. "One of these is the cupping of Alnocrati, which is at the back of the head; and Alkehel, which is in the middle of the neck; and the cuppings of Alhagdahim, which are two flat parts of the neck on both sides together; and the cuppings of the chin, which are under the jawbone on the lower part of the mouth; and the cuppings of the two ribs; and the cuppings of Alachosos, which are at the end of the tail; and the cuppings of the two loins, which are in the middle of the two arms; and the cuppings of the two thighs; and the cuppings of the two calves.
"And cupping, indeed, does not extract blood from the small dispersed veins in the flesh; and for this reason, it does not cause weakness as in phlebotomy. "Nor should cupping be administered, whether with fire or without, for any disease that results from fullness until the entire body is evacuated.
"But if necessity requires cupping due to illness or habit, we administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in its middle, and at whatever time it may be.
"That is because there are people who, when their blood increases and they need to extract it through cupping, find heaviness and dizziness in their head.
"And among them are those who find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And among them is one who finds itching on his face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in his eyes.
And among them is one who scratches the place of his farting. And among them is one whose laughter is increased. And among them is one who finds the taste of blood in his mouth; or the tongue swells up and he spits out blood. And among them is one whose sleep is increased. And among them is one who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to these.
When therefore we see something of these things, and especially if it is in the third week of the month: then we order that a purging be done after two or three hours have passed from the day.
But the relief of purging the neck is: because it contributes to heaviness in the head and what flows out into the eyes. And indeed it is necessary that this be after the evacuation of the whole body. 》
And this purging takes the place of a phlebotomy of the head.
And it is not permissible for one who has a cold temperament or who has catarrh to use it. 》 For it brings him great harm.
And because of this, it is not right that old people and those who have cold illnesses in the head should use them.
