But he who becomes habituated to it generates forgetfulness upon himself.
And because of this, it is necessary to order the purger that he lower his hand with the purging slightly downward, fearing from the cause of forgetfulness. 》 》
The purging called Alkehel is in place of a dark phlebotomy and a basilica phlebotomy, and because of this it helps with asthma, constriction of breath, cleft palate, coughing, and fullness.
And it is necessary that a cupping glass be applied briefly to al-Khayl, because if it is placed lower, it causes weakness in the heart and stomach.
However, cupping the al-Hadhim area is effective for headaches, migraines, and toothache; it is an alternative to basal phlebotomy.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press his hand on the skin after scarifying, lest he cut an artery and cause bleeding.
Cupping under the chin is effective for mouth ulcers and gum disease, as well as similar oral ailments; it serves as an alternative to phlebotomy of the lip corners.
Cupping with a spoon is effective for al-Khaffakh, which is caused by indigestion and heat.
Cupping the inner parts of the two groins is as beneficial as phlebotomy of the three veins, the basilic, cephalic, and median cubital; for it draws blood from all the small veins in the flesh, which in turn draw from larger veins, until the drawing reaches the three main veins.
And you should instruct the cupper not to cut too deeply when scarifying, since the area is bare of flesh and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A cupping glass placed over the anus is effective for hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
And you should instruct the cupper to use a large cupping glass made of brass, since the area requires strong suction and fine glasses might break; he should also make a deep cut.
Cupping the legs reduces swelling visibly, since it draws blood from the entire body; it relieves old pains in the kidneys, bladder, and uterus, stimulates menstruation, and improves complexion and appetite. It serves as an alternative to phlebotomy of both saphenous veins and the great metatarsal vein. )))
Indeed, they greatly weaken the body and cause fainting in many people.
And the remedy for Ventosarum of the leg is similar to the remedy for Ventosarum of two legs.
The method for applying the Ventosa is as follows: first place the cup on an empty area and apply moderate suction. Do not prolong the position of the cup, but rather place and remove it quickly so that the humors may arrive at the site equally. Continue this process without interruption until you see the site become red and swollen, and the blood appear. At that point, make an incision, and then continue with gradual suction. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For those who have tender flesh and few pores, you should incise once only, no more, so that the site does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the person applying the cups to widen the incision slightly and make it deep enough; they should regulate the suction so that it is gentle, quick, and smooth in motion.
If there is thickening of the blood, then you should incise twice. With the first incision, the way is opened for the thinning and watering down of the blood; with the second, it is subjected to a thorough examination of the thick blood’s extraction.
If, however, the blood is extremely dirty, then a third incision should be made so that it may reach its final state.
And in the end, when we want to extract the blood little by little or briefly, one incision is sufficient for us. If we wish to make many extractions of blood, we should use many incisions.
And if we measure and find that the blood is thick, then it should be incised with a deep incision; and the equal limit of the incision’s depth is the thickness of the skin.
What is that which must be applied from the oils at the site of the Ventosa, and what should the one applying the Ventosa beware of?
IN the case of one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is necessary that the places to be ventosed be anointed with openings, softening, and dissolving oils: if it is in summertime, such as alkeiri oil, or violet oil, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of cucumber seed.
And if it is in wintertime, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or savory oil: and similar ones to these.
But if the excess is thick and cold, then it should be bitter orange oil, or balm oil, or alben oil, or dill oil: and similar ones to these.
But if the one to be ventosed has large pores and soft flesh, then it is necessary that he be kept from oil, and it is necessary that these wash the places to be ventosed with rose water, or cold water, or saltwater, or cucumber water, or purslane water: and similar ones to these. 》
But that one whose blood is very moist, should wash the ventosae with vinegar, or myrtle water, and sumac water: and similar ones to these.
But that one whose excesses are thick, should wash his ventosae with old wine, or sage water, or dill decoction, or chamomile: and similar ones to these.
And it is necessary that the ventosa be applied not in a bath or after a bath; indeed, it should be administered after an hour or two hours have passed since leaving the bath.
And it is not appropriate for anyone to sleep after ventilation.
What should be done to ensure that the patient who needs to be ventilated or bled is properly prepared before ventilation?
And after that you should consider first of all: whether the patient who has been ventilated or bled is choleric and if sharpness and inflammation prevail in their blood, then they should receive cooling substances such as pomegranate seeds, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and cucumbers, and jujubes, and similar things. ¶
And make his food soft, and give him cooked mutton, and foods prepared with sour sauce, and similar things.
And if the patient's complexion is cold, it is appropriate for them to take in drink syrup of honey or of almond, and a dessert of seeds; and let them take medium-aged wine that is between old and new; and they should be advised to use little food. And make his food soft, and let him have quails and small birds, and young pigeons is good for him.
And it is appropriate that on the day of ventilation and bloodletting, drink should be more than food; and it is appropriate that in some people's drink, there should be given theriac alfaroch or a medicine from musk, that is muscat, or alesselithe before ventilation: and before bloodletting, or after: so that the principal members may be strengthened, and the blood may be refined. And it is not appropriate for them to take it in hot drink.
About ventosas without scarification. But the ventosas which are without scarification are those which are placed on the liver and spleen, and on gout, and on the abdomen, and the navel, and on the places of the kidneys, and on the bladder; because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And it is not sought through them except either by contact of blood from one member to another, as we place Ventose on the breasts in the disease of nasal blood flow. And we use them to dissolve from the member cold and unseen windiness in the member, as we place Ventose on the belly and navel. For she indeed rarefies the member and heats it and removes the pain for this reason: because she dissolves that windiness.
It is also placed on the kidneys when an obstruction or stone occurs there. For perhaps by the power of its attraction it opens the obstruction or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly when it is placed on the liver and spleen for grinding windiness in both of them.
And these Ventose are applied empty only with a cut. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in diseases of pleurisy: and this is done so that the Ventose may be filled with great hot water alone or with water in which appropriate herbs have been boiled.
¶ The form of the Ventose applied with fire. ¶
Let its opening be two fingers wide as we have shaped it: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be on one of its sides, in the middle part, a small hole the size that can admit an awl: make it from copper or brass, thick-rimmed, smooth, equal, polished so that it does not harm the member upon which it is placed. And let there be in its center a transverse rod of copper or iron where a candle with fire is placed.
And now this Ventose is made large, larger than we have described and somewhat smaller than that one, according to the disease and age of the person using it. ¶
The swellings of boys and emaciated persons are different from those of men and robust bodies.
The manner of applying this swelling with fire upon the member is to light a small wick made from linen or a small wax candle; and place it on the middle of a crossbar that is in the center of the swelling: so that the fire is kindled around the lower part of the swelling, that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then place it on the member; and let your finger be over the opening that I mentioned, until when we have the swelling in hand; as long as we need it, remove the finger, and steam will come out through that opening, and the swelling will immediately dissolve.
Then extinguish the wick according to its manner, and repeat it, if you need it.
However, in the swelling that is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no crossbar nor opening; and it is not applied except when filled with water and placed on the member alone.
And this is its form.
And the larger this swelling is, so that it can hold much water, the better it is.
¶ On the suspension of leeches.
Leeches are not applied in many places except those where the position of cupping glasses is not possible due to their smallness, such as the lip and the gum, and similar parts. Or on members that have been stripped of flesh, such as the finger, nose, and similar parts.
And the manner of using them is: that you come from leeches which are in sweet, clean waters free from rottenness. ↑
Then they should be left day and night in sweet water until they are very hungry, and nothing remains in their stomachs. Then let the body be evacuated first with bloodletting and with clysters. Then wash the diseased part until it turns red. Then let them be placed on top of it.
When they are filled and fall off, and it is possible to draw the pus with a clyster, then it is more beneficial in the end; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then wash it thoroughly with water, and rub it, and squeeze it out.
But if the bleeding continues after the bloodletting, and it is due to sweating, then soak a cloth of flax in cold water and place it on top until the sweating stops.
But if the bleeding increases, then sprinkle powdered sumac or gall nuts and other astringents on it until the bleeding stops.
Or place half of a peeled bean on the spot and leave it until the beans stick to the spot, for then the bleeding will stop.
And if a repetition of bloodletting is necessary, whenever possible, change the site.
But if leeches are prevented from feeding, then wash the spot with fresh blood. Or prick the spot with a needle until some blood comes out. Then apply them, for when they sense something from the blood, they will immediately stick on.
When you want them to fall off, sprinkle some powdered aloes, or salt, or ashes on them and they will immediately fall off.
This chapter is also very often necessary in the art of medicine, and it deals with the reduction of fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know, O Sons, that the foolish doctors and common people boast about this Chapter without ever having seen an ancient book or read a single letter from it.
And for this reason, this method has been deprived of scientific knowledge in our land. And I never found any good in it at all and did not gain from it what I gained except through the long reading of ancient books and deep study of their meaning until I extracted that knowledge from them.
Then I devoted myself to experimentation and study throughout my life.
And now I have described to you in this Chapter everything that my knowledge contains on that subject: and what my experimentation has passed over, after I have abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the labor of prolongation, and abbreviated it at the end of abbreviations, and shown it at the end of declinations; and I have formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments used in it, with an addition to declination, as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
In the prologue of this Chapter, it is fitting to say summaries and headings that compel in the first place the artist to stop on their truth: namely so that you, and those who are eager to learn, may know or learn this noble art from you.
I say, therefore, that when someone suffers a fracture, dislocation, twisting, or fall, then it is necessary in the first place to hasten to bleed him and purge him, or both together: unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength; or he who suffered one of these things is a boy or an old man weakened by age; or it is a time of very intense heat or very intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, with cold vegetables, and the meat of birds and goats; and avoid wine, fatty meats, and filling himself with food and all foods that fill the veins with blood. Until you are certain of the heat of the abscess and no longer fear its discharge into the limb, then the sick person should return to his original diet with which he was accustomed.
When the broken bone begins to heal, then it is necessary for the sick person to eat nourishing foods that are rich, thick, and heavy, with a viscous quality, such as dishes made from cooked wheat with water, rice, heads, feet, and intestines of cows, eggs, fresh fish; and fatty wine, and similar things. With this diet, the fracture heals more quickly and better.
And know that the bones of men who are in their prime or decrepit cannot always be joined and healed according to their nature due to the dryness of their limbs; rather, they heal and join like the bones of young children.
However, nature adds something like glue all around the broken bone, which has thickness and holds it together until they are joined to each other and bound together, until it reaches the end of strength and firmness as it was in the beginning, so that nothing hinders it from its functions: and for this reason it is necessary to give the sick person food that contains heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are different according to the diversity of limbs, for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone; and similarly all other limbs are different from each other. And I have come with the description of every kind of fracture exposed in its own chapter, distinguished from others. And various kinds of bone fractures are also distinguished from each other.
Because the fracture is curved, besides which stars occur in it. And sometimes the fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and its fractures are splinters, that is, stars, and additions proceeding, as they are in a reed when it is fitted with a knife for writing. And the ones that do not protrude: and there is a fracture with a wound and tearing at the end; and there is a small crack fracture.
And each type has its own tool or implement for its restoration, as its description will come, distinguished in their respective places.
And from that by which a bone fracture is known is its twisting and protrusion; and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
When there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor a sound when you touch the bone with movement, and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture there; rather, it is possible that it is a slight twist or small crack. Therefore, you should not move it at all with your hand and compression, but place upon it some of the medicines whose description follows after the appropriate time for the place: then let it be bound tightly with a fine bandage.
And know that when a place is curved and divided into two, besides which splinters occur in it, yet every part is separated from its mate; then you should begin at the hour to straighten it, equalizing it before a hot abscess occurs.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs in it, then leave it for some days until the abscess quiets down. Then straighten it by whatever method you can from gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and alignment are easier than bone, in which fractures occur.
Then tie it according to how you remember it.
But if there are fractures in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two parts, whether it is a hand or a foot; either with your hand, if it is a small limb; or with two ropes, or with a rope and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb on an even surface according to its natural shape until the parts of the broken limb are extended; then strive with all your skill and in any way you can to return those additions to their places, and take care not to cause pain or injury to the weakened part through your operation. And strive with all your skill to join one of the two bones with its mate according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that moment for you to test and touch it with your hand. Therefore, if you see something different there, correct it and make it even according to the measure of your skill.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many foolish people do. For often through their operation they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen many times from their operations.
Then bind it after alignment and proper treatment, and the constriction of that limb, and its rest and tranquility; and advise the patient regarding it. And instruct him to take care not to move it during the hours of his watchfulness and sleep, and during his turning and tossing, and during his evacuation, and all his movements with all his might. 》 And to beware that the position of the limb is not one in which pain will follow. What this means is: "When one feels pain or injury in a limb's position and arrangement, it is necessary to change its position to another that does not cause pain.
"And take care with that, so its position is equal and straight; so that when it is restored, the limb does not become twisted.
"And the nature of a fractured limb's constriction is as I tell you.
"Know that fractured limbs are distinguished by their size and shape.
"Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, or cheekbone, then it is necessary for there to be a soft, thin wrapping of cloth. "But those that are large, such as the thigh and leg, it is necessary for there to be a wide, hard wrapping.
"Because a broad bandage secures a large limb and binds it equally from all sides, so that no blemish enters into it.
"Then after your evacuation from equalizing, begin to place an appropriate ointment onto soft tow for the site of the fracture itself. "Then begin to wrap the bandage over the site of the fracture with three or four turns, according to what the limb deserves; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
"Then go with it to the upper part away from the site of the fracture, and tighten less than your previous binding where you bound the broken place. "Then extend yourself with the wrapping slightly from the site of the fracture and gradually soften the binding until you take something great from the healthy place.
"Then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with turns. "Then go with the wrapping to the lower part away from the fracture. And let your operation be on the constriction of the invagination and its softening, as we have said in the first upper invagination.
Then place between the invaginations some soft wool or cloth, which matches the twist of the break, if there is any twist: and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then roll another one on top of that same thread. Afterwards, immediately apply to these invaginations equal and well-made plasters, if there is no swelling or hot abscess in the place.
But if there is swelling or an abscess in it: then place on it that which can settle the abscess and remove the swelling; and leave it for some days. Then bind plasters over it at that time.
And let the plasters be made from half-wide, planed, and wisely shaped reeds; or they may be of wood from which sieves are made, that is, made from pine or gerad, that is, palm branches or calingi; and let the breadth of each plaster be according to this form, and the same shape.
However, it is necessary that the plaster placed on the affected part be a little thicker and wider than the other plasters; and let the length of the plaster be according to what is permitted by the member, in terms of size and smallness.
Then bind another thread over the plasters, with the same first constriction. Then bind over it with fine threads as we have said about the constriction; and it is necessary that your constriction on the place of the break be more, and the farther away from the break it extends, the less the constriction should be.  />
And it is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and let them be from soft linen.
Because if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the work of foolish people placing their threads from twisted linen cords, it is a great mistake; for the constriction with them falls outside of its quality.
And fine threads are not suitable again because you will not achieve through them that which you desire due to constriction.
It is not necessary for the space between star and star to be less than a finger's width.
If the patient is harmed by the tips of the stars after constriction in healthy places, then place under them soft tow or carded wool until no harm occurs from it.
When there is a break with a wound and a disruption of the skin occurs, I will refer back to it in Chapter Two individually.
And know that not every broken bone should be constricted with stars from the first day. That is, because when a bone is large, you should not place stars on it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the occurrence of hot inflammation.
And this is the method of plasters by which the ancients used to restore [the limb], which are placed on a break and dislocation.
¶ The abbreviated common method of a restorative plaster for a break, suitable for most complexions, especially women and children: because it is made from that which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
It is made by taking the flour of a mill, which is the marrow of flour that hangs on the walls of the mill during the turning of the stone; and make it up just as it is, except that you should sift it with egg white; and place its preparation not thick nor fine. Then use it.
¶ Another method of a plaster restoring a break, dislocation, and twist. Take mastic, and laudanum, and acacia, and elecampane, and mugwort; and the juice of all of them, each 10 drams, myrrh 5 drams, and aloes, both 5 drams, and aleth (i.e., gum tragacanth) 20 drams. Let all be ground, sifted, and mixed with aleth water; or with egg white, if the patient's complexion is hot. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it quickly restores broken bones and benefits many people.
Another method of the plaster, which also benefits fracture and dislocation. Take mugwort, pennyroyal, and white hellebore, each 10 drams; of myrrh and both kinds of aloes, each 5 drams; acacia, 6 drams; Armenian bole, 20 drams. Reduce all to a fine powder, sift, and mix with water or egg white. Apply it as a plaster.
Method of the plaster for joints and separated bones from their places; and it alleviates the pain that occurs to them, and bone splitting, and fracture. Take freshly shorn wool and soak it in boiled vinegar and oil; then place it on the area.
And indeed this plaster does not have the power of restoration, but it is good for alleviating hot inflammation and properly drives away pain.
Method of the plaster that restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the deaf fig and wild poppy leaves; grind all together and make a plaster from both.
Method of the shortened plaster, applied for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you wish to resolve any remaining inflammation. Take the root of black hellebore, camomile, violet flowers, and grass flour, each one part; grind all together and mix with wine if the limb is not heated.
If it is heated, then mix it with water of fresh coriander or with water and use it.
Another method of a stronger plaster for resolving hard inflammation during the restoration of a limb, stronger than this first one. xspace A recipe of the root of altea, linseed, balaustia, mellilotus, samsuci, violet flower, and chamomile, all taken in equal parts. Grind everything and prepare with willow water or sweet water or wine. All of this according to the heat of the limb and the stillness of its heat.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration except a plaster made of wax and oil; nothing else, and he reported that it is a middle ground between coarseness and fineness.
But Galen thought that things should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration which contain the drying of heat, such as myrrh, aloes, and frankincense, and similar things from those which we have mentioned.
The manner of a poultice that helps with injury and pain. Take mugwort and chickpeas, and human hair that has been cut or bird feathers, and altea, and salt in equal parts; grind them and sift them and make a poultice from it.
The quantity by which it is necessary that the binding should remain, then be loosened, is this: you must see if no pain or itching happens to the patient, and the broken bone does not move from its place; then do not loosen it for many days. But if intense itching or troubling pain or swelling happens to him, then hurry and loosen it right away, and remove the poultice from him; and take a soft cloth or a soft sponge moistened with water, and dip it in warm water and wash the place with it until the itching quiets down and the pain is soothed.
But if intense itching or troubling pain or swelling happens to him, then hurry and loosen it right away, and remove the poultice from him; and take a soft cloth or a soft sponge moistened with water, and dip it in warm water and wash the place with it until the itching quiets down and the pain is soothed. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place upon it a piece of wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil; and bind it on for one night, until you are secure from hot swelling, the limb's swelling subsides, and its pain is relieved.
Then apply to it a fine bandage and a small plaster; and do not bind it too tightly at first, but keep the bandages loose with it until it heals.
If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and all other symptoms have been completely removed, and you no longer need plasters or bandages, then bind it as you did at first, evenly.
If nothing happens to the limb as we have said, then do not remove it before three days, or four, five, or seven. And when it is removed after twenty days, the whole thing according to what you see from the unwrapping of the limb, as we have said, until the bone has healed and the flesh has coagulated over it: add also then to the bandage more than your first entire bandage; and add also in feeding the patient, as his strength returns.
But if you see the place of the break becoming excessively dry and thin: then know that the nourishment is being prevented from reaching it. Moisten it with warm water every time you unwrap it, and let that be for all three days, and slightly loosen the bandage.
By this procedure the nourishment reaches the limb and it heals quickly.
The foolish restorers who do something else with a broken limb when it is not set correctly at first, and then set it crookedly, are making an error in their procedure and have great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said so in their books and would have practiced thus.
And I have not seen any trace of them there at all, and it is better that it should not be done.
On the making of a swelling on the head.
The kinds of fracture are various, and its shapes many; and its causes are distinct.
For there is a type of fracture which happens from a blow with a sword; and the blow removes the bone entirely to where it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an ax does in wood; and because of this, this type of fracture is named Axial. 
Or the sword strikes something of the bone, and it strikes its surface but does not penetrate as far as its end; and this type of fracture is named Kalaan, that is, Clean Incision.
And there are two types of fractures, either large or small.  
And there is a type of fracture which is a crushing or grinding; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or falling onto a stone, or similar things.
And this type of fracture can also be either penetrating, which is near the panniculum that is under the bone; or it can be on the surface of the bone; and there are two types of wounds from these two fractures: either small or large.
And there is a type of fracture which is hidden in the bone as fine as hair; and it is a small crack; and because of this, this type of fracture is named Hairline.
And among these is the type of fracture which happens from a fall or blow with a stone and similar things, which cause a layer of bone to sink into its depths, and a depression occurs; just as happens in copper pots when they are struck, and part of them goes inside.
And this happens most often in the heads of soft bones, such as children's heads.
And for all these kinds of fractures, splints are separated out, and I will come with the recollection of that entire thing in its place.
And we know all these kinds of fractures by examination of them, and investigation with probes, and removal of the corrupted flesh that is upon them.
Moreover, the hairy kind is known by this: it is uncovered on the bone, and it is cleansed and anointed with wax upon it; for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the conditions of the patient; then if you see from his condition something that clearly indicates danger, such as choleric vomiting, distension, loss of consciousness, hoarseness, fainting, acute fever, protrusion of the eyes, their redness, and similar symptoms in various constitutions, certainly he will not be cured; but if you see conditions that do not frighten you and you hope for a cure, then begin his treatment.
That is, if a wounded man comes to you first when he is wounded; and this is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you strive for the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every constitution.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, lest that which is under the bone from the membrane be corrupted and those symptoms which we have mentioned occur.
But if the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain and is accompanied by fragments and swelling; then it is necessary that you cut away the crushed and swollen part, as I tell you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded man and uncover the bone in whatever way possible for you; according to the shape of the wound and what is easy for the patient.
If there should be a flow of blood or a hot swelling while exposing the bone, then apply to it what is needed. And it is that you should fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and make the wound rest until the swelling subsides and you are secure from bleeding. Then begin with the cure of the bone and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
Indeed, the first method is that you cut the bone with a trephine of sharp edge, and this is its form.
Then administer another trephine after the first one, which is wider by a little bit, and this is its form.
Then also administer another trephine wider than the second one.
And the second method is that you have with you a number of different trephines, some wider than others and some shorter than others, and at their ends they are sharpened, and they are made from iron or steel. Use skill in striking on the trephine so that it does not shake the head and harm it.
But if the bone happens to be hard and tough, then you should bore around it with non-penetrating trephines called non-penetrating trephines, and they are not so called except because they do not pass through to the end of the bony part that is behind it; because the extremity of the trephine is rounded on what is under its sharp point, similar to a small circle, and it prevents sinking in and passing through the thickness of the bone. 》
And it is fitting for you to take from these trephines a great number, each of which fits the thickness of the bone, until you have a trephine for every skull, whose length and shortness of its sharp extremity is according to the thickness of that skull.
And these are the forms of three types of trepans, or trephines.
The quality of penetration around the broken bone is such that you place the trepan on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has already been penetrated. Then change the trepan to another place and put it between every opening a quantity equal to the thickness of the radius, or about that.
Then cut with the chisels that which is between every opening from the bone and make it thinner than you can above that to the limit of thinness, as we have said, until you extract the bone either by hand or with some other of the instruments which you have prepared for that purpose, such as tweezers and fine forceps.
And you must be very careful that the trepan or chisel does not touch anything of the brain.
Therefore, when you have circled the bone by penetrating and separated it from the brain, if it is continuous with it and you have extracted it, it is necessary to scrape and smooth the remaining end of it in the bone with another similar instrument like a chisel. However, it must be thin and more delicate than other chisels.
If there remains anything of the small fragments and splinters then take it easily with one of the instruments which you have with you. Then care for the wound afterwards with the linen cloths and ointments which we have mentioned.
And there is another method of operating which is completely free from danger, as Galen said and praised it greatly, saying this: It is necessary for you first to begin exposing the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is most severe and evident until, when you have exposed that part and placed under it the end of this lenticular chisel, whose form is as follows:
And the lens-shaped part, which is soft and does not cut anything, and its pointed part on both sides of it going along the length, as you see, so that the lens-shaped part rests against the cheek, with its pointed end striking the bone.
Then strike the jaw from one side with a small hammer until you cut through the entire bone easily, as I have shown: and you should be confident that nothing will happen to it that you fear at all.
And if the doctor were not more foolish than other men and less ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it off from there with the pointed end of the lens-shaped instrument itself, and free it easily. For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the bone's fracture does not reach to the cloth, and what is cut off from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and fine splinters remain in it; then you must scrape away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine scrapers of various sizes which you have prepared, so that you can use from them the most suitable and straightest ones for each place, according to what the operation requires, and the broken bone itself, and its shape. 》 And you must use first, with those scrapers, a broader one to scrape the bone. Then use a finer one after that.
And you must use first, with those scrapers, a broader one to scrape the bone. Then use a finer one after that. And do not stop doing this continuously until you reach using the thinnest and finest of all.
But in each of the remaining small hairline cracks and fine fractures, you must take care to straighten it according to what is needed for that.
And it is a matter that is not hidden to anyone who has any interest in this art, and we have written about it and demonstrated it in the case of large fractures.
But if the skin flap remains attached when you pull out a tooth: then you should take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil, and place it on the opening of the wound. Then take another piece of linen cloth, folded double or triple, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and place it on the first cloth: and make that as easy as possible on top of it, so that it does not cause pressure.
Then apply a broad bandage over it; and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds only the wound; and leave it with this care for one day or two days, until you are sure of avoiding hot inflammation. Then remove it and apply afterwards medications that are dry in nature, such as lily roots, henbane seed flour, frankincense powder, birthwort, and similar to these: and this is how you make a powder from these medications to be dusted on the wound like dry substances.
And universally apply on it every medication that has the property to cleanse and not to bite. And be careful according to your ability that the wound is clean in your treatment: and there is no dirt from oil or grease in it.
And be careful according to your ability that the wound is clean in your treatment: and there is no dirt from oil or grease in it. And do not allow pus to accumulate on it at all: for when pus accumulates on the skin of the brain, it corrupts and putrefies it, and a great evil happens to the patient afterwards; and this often happens with siphac of the head, when the bone is cleansed from it, especially when its care is neglected, a blackness appears on its surface.
"Therefore, if such events occur to the patient, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if the blackening does not occur except from the medicine that is placed on it, and it is in the power of that medicine to make that blackness: then you should take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and soak a cloth with them. Then place it on the sore; afterward, treat it with appropriate remedies until it heals.
"On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
"Know that only two parts of the nose can be broken at once or one of them: because there are two bones in it. The lower part is cartilaginous and cannot break; it only sustains rubbing, twisting, and compression.
If one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert a small finger into the nostril and with your index finger and thumb on the outside, adjust the fracture until the nose returns to its natural shape; this should be done skillfully and easily. And be careful that during your operation no pain occurs to the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the end of a small bone with some thickness to adjust it.
If the fracture is in both parts, then do the same; and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture if possible. 😊 And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has abated.
Then insert a cloth plug made of linen into the tip of the nose if the fracture is in one part; or insert two plugs if the fracture is in both parts: and let the thickness of the plug be such that it fills the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that they should be infused with linseed in butter and changed every day. That, however, does not seem right to me; rather, linseed should be infused with the white of an egg, made into a powder. Then leave the linseed until the bone hardens and the cartilage thickens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of linseed, after it has been wrapped with soft cloth; this holds the broken nose more firmly and allows the patient to breathe without obstruction.
And this is not necessary. If you want, do that; and if you want, do linseed.
But if the nose swells again while you are treating an abscess, apply a plaster to the nose with ketron or cotton soaked in rose oil or a little dyacon.
But if the abscess does not swell again, then you should apply plasters made with sesame flour and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. Then place soft wool on it, and do not bind the nose at all.
But if the bones of a small broken nose are fractured or shattered, then you should cut open and extract them with suitable instruments. Then either stitch it up or care for it with what promotes healing and consolidation from suitable plasters or ointments.
But if there is a wound between the nose and lip, then you should treat it with linseed poultices; and insert a lead tube until it heals.
¶ On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no wound: observe then if the fracture is only on the outside and does not break into two pieces but bends towards the inside; then its diagnosis is easy.  /> If, however, it breaks into two pieces, its diagnosis is difficult, except for when one piece is missing.
Therefore, if there is a fracture on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, then insert your right index finger. And with it, push the swelling of the fracture from within to the outside with your other hand from the inside of the bone, and make it even with that.
If the mandible is already divided into two parts, then it is necessary to administer traction from both sides in a straight line until equalization becomes possible.
However, if there is displacement or separation of the teeth, then tie them with gold thread, silver thread, or silk from which you hope they will remain. Then place a bandage on the fractured mandible. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and place a large, well-made spica over the cloth, or a piece of sole skin equal in length to the mandible.
Then bind it on top according to how its bandage is prepared and the application of it is suitable until it does not fall apart; and instruct the patient to be calm and at rest, and give him soft food.
However, if you think that the shape has changed in any way, then hurry and remove it on the third day. Then correct what has changed from it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour after you have removed the bandage; and place soft wool over the plaster.
Therefore, while that plaster remains attached to it and the arrangement of the bone does not change, leave it unremoved until healing occurs; or bind the fracture. Indeed, this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If a hot abscess should occur in the meantime, then administer what we have often said for its relief until that abscess is removed.
And if there is a fracture with a wound, consider this: For if already a fragment has separated from the bone, or from the saw: then be skillful in this, that you remove those fragments of the saw with what is suitable to you for their removal among instruments.
But if the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it with a fleam according to your need.
Then when you have removed the saw fragments and none of them remain, or if the opening of the wound is wide; and if not, place on it one of those plasters which are suitable for it, and let it grow flesh until it heals.
¶ On Restoring a Clavicle When It Is Broken
Indeed, a clavicle is most often broken from the anterior part of the arm, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Or it breaks and separates into two parts, besides which fragments may occur in it: and that is easier to restore.
Or so, that fragments occur in the bone: and that is more difficult to restore. 》
Or it may be a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it when there is a fracture without a wound is to have present two assistants, so that one of them holds up what follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
Then make the fracture equal with your fingers until it takes its proper shape: and there should not be any prominence or depression in it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place under the patient's armpit a sphere of cloth or wool: and its size should be according to your need; and extend and lift the clavicle, and squeeze the sphere with your hand until you make the fracture equal, as is necessary. 》 》 》 》 》 》 》
If you cannot pull the end of the fork to the outside, because it has reached the depths, then the patient must be turned onto their back; and place a medium-sized cushion under their shoulder, and have an assistant press down on the shoulder until the fork bone that is deep inside comes up. Then set right the fracture, and make it even with your fingers.
If you feel a splinter from the fork and it moves, then you should scrape over it and extract the splinter easily.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then make an incision over it with one of the knives you have prepared for this purpose. After placing under the fork a tool that preserves the marrow; and this tool should be made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It resembles a spoon without the hollow part.
And its width should be according to your need, based on the size and thinness of the bone. The length of it should also be as much as is possible for you to work with.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
But if the wound that you made when you extracted the bone splinter is wide and free from any hot inflammation, then join the two lips of the wound with a stitch.
And if the wound is small or you fear a hot inflammation, then fill the wound with cloths and soft wool according to the size of the cut in the wound.  \\*  \\
But if a hot inflammation occurs, then dip a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on the wound.
A narrowing of the bone without a wound or cut is treated by placing on the bone an ointment made from flour and egg white.  \\*  \\  \\*  \\ And place soft wool over it. Then place a sphere under his nipple, if you need that. Then take a roll of cotton, which is what the Saracens use, and let it be very long; its width should be that of one palm, or about that. Then place two layers of soft cloth over the wool and the plaster. Then take a small wooden board, whose width is three fingers, and likewise in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the board in the place of the himema which falls over the break and the wound. Then place the himema over the break as it is wrapped, and wrap it around his neck, under his sound nipple, and under his afflicted nipple; and bring it back over all the parts, so that the bandage holds the break firmly in place: and this should not be hidden from you, and all of it, so that the board is not removed from the broken part. And for this reason, you must look at the patient every day. Whenever the bandage becomes loose and you see that the board has moved, then correct it and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's sleep on his back, and put under his sound nipple, near his sleep, a small pillow at night, so that with it his help will be lifted from his side. Therefore, the break of the collarbone is healed by lifting the arm.
Or bind his arm to his own neck; and do not loosen the bandage, unless it happens in that place from itching or swelling, for twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see his face turning to it. And add to the bandage, and leave him alone until he is healed and solidifies.
And indeed the fracture of the collarbone is tied and strengthened for the most part within twenty-eight days, and sometimes it happens in some people within a shorter period.
On the treatment of a fractured rib.
Rarely is a rib broken, and only in its broad part; from it, only its edges are broken.
So when it is broken in that place and is broken in the middle of it and is not known except by touch, then prepare it according to the shape of the fracture. And reduce it to its natural shape by every means possible for you.
Then place on the site powder of ground linen seed with the membrane of an egg and soft tow and place a little cloth from a doubled garment over it. Then place on it a broad splint made of thin wood according to the size of the whole rib, or slightly larger than that.
If however there is any hollow under the splint in some part of the rib, then even it out with soft tow until the splint descends to an equal level.
Then tie it over with a long bandage of appropriate tightness and firmness of the splint, until it does not move from its place. ︎
And examine the bandage every day. Whenever therefore the bandage becomes loose, tighten it and even out the splint if it has moved from its place; and let the patient lie on his sound side. ︎ ︎ ︎
And a rib is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five days.
Therefore when this number is completed, then loosen the bandage; and you should be secure. For it [the rib] is of bones whose grinding is not feared, nor their dissolution.
But if a piece of bone protrudes and stings under the skin, then cut over it and remove it. And do what we said regarding a fracture of the collarbone from inflammation, if something happens due to that.
¶ On the restoration of a breast fracture.
The breast is sometimes broken in its middle, and this happens rarely. Its extremities are most often broken and crushed due to injuries.
When its middle is broken and it leans towards the lower part, a severe pain occurs, along with difficulty in breathing and coughing; and sometimes blood may be vomited, and there will be a concavity in the fractured bone, which will not escape notice. The treatment for this is to have the patient lie on his back; place cushions under his shoulders. ething
Then press his shoulders and bring the ribs together from both sides with your hands; and adjust it in equilibrium as much as possible and is suitable until the shape of the bone returns to what is necessary.
Then place a plaster and tow on him; then put a board made of thin willow or linden, or similar light materials on top after you wrap it in cloth. Then adjust the bandage over the fractured bone so that it does not move; and pass the bandage around to the back several times, tightening it with a proper tightness. ething ething
Then inspect the bandage every hour and whenever it loosens, tighten it; and if its necessity calls for its release due to itching or ulceration that occurs in the place, or pain, or inflammation, then hurry and release it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has happened with its treatment method. ething
Then reapply the plaster if you see it is needed; and fasten it with a bandage until it heals.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur except in the thick parts that follow the spine and from their ends on the anterior part. And abrasion does not happen to them except because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the senses when examined with the fingers.
And its restoration is done by evening out the fracture with the fingers to the extent possible, until the shape becomes even as it should be. Then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage if it needs it.
If the fracture of the ribs is slanted towards the inside: then a very severe pain and sharpness occurs to the patient, like that puncture which occurs in pleurisy; because the bone pricks the membrane. And also difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting much blood occurs. This is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have devised many ingenious methods for it; among them are those who say that you should give food to the patient which generates inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly may be inflamed and extended, and the fracture pushed outwards. )))
But we ourselves detest it, lest a quick cause be made for producing a hot abscess, if it has not happened already. If however it has already occurred, then it adds to it.
And some of them have said: a cupping glass should be placed on the location, then drawn out with force, and this is similar to reasoning. ))) However, it must be feared that the cupping may draw superfluities to the location because of the weakness of the condition. ))) )))
And some of them have said: the location should be covered with wool which has already been soaked in hot oil, and small pieces of cloth should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that there may be an equal bandage when you wrap it around according to its roundness. ))) ))) ))) )))
Then let the patient be treated with a diet and medicine for pleurisy.
If a strong force constricts the patient and he cannot tolerate it, and the skin is pricked with a wounding puncture, and we fear for the patient: then it is necessary that we cut open the place and expose the broken rib. Then let us place on it an instrument that conserves the siphon, of which we have previously spoken; and let us cut the skin easily, and let us cut into it.
Then we will bring together the two lips of the wound with a stitch, if it is large; and we will treat it with plasters until it heals.
If it happens that a hot abscess occurs in the meantime, then hasten and pour powder into rose oil, and place it on the spot; and let the patient be treated with what is suitable for an abscess.
In the meantime, he should also be turned onto his side where he sleeps, until it heals.
¶ On the restoration of a fracture of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Fracture indeed sometimes happens to the bones of the neck, and rarely do they suffer attrition; and similarly with the vertebrae of the back. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will heal or not heal, then make an examination. For if you see his hands already softened and insensible and dead; and he cannot move them or stretch them out, or draw them back together; and when you press them with your fingers and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and there is no pain in them: then know that he will not heal, and in many dispositions it is fatal.
But if he moves them and feels the pressure with his fingers and the prick: then know that the bone of the neck is sound, and that the patient will heal with treatment. ‌
If it happens similarly in the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider his feet: if you see that they have become soft, and the same thing happens to them as happened to the hands; when he lies on his back, flatulence and evacuation occur involuntarily; and when he is turned onto his stomach, evacuation occurs involuntarily; and when he is turned onto his back again and wishes to urinate but cannot, then you should know that it is fatal. Therefore, do not labor over his cure.
If, however, nothing of this kind happens to him, the case is less serious.
And the treatment for what has happened is to try to reduce the hot swelling in this way: place crushed rose oil alone on the vertebra, or mixed with cooked egg yolks.
Apply this to the site several times a day until the hot swelling is reduced; then place one of the strengthening plasters on the spot, and bind it tightly with a bandage. Advise the patient to rest quietly. Let him not sleep on the part where he finds pain until it is healed.
If the bone has been splintered or something that has already separated from it occurs; then it is necessary to open the skin and remove it. Then join the two lips of the wound with a stitch if it is large. Then treat him with some healing ointment until he heals.
If the last bone of the tail is broken, then it is necessary to insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus and align the broken bone with your other hand as best you can, and make another alignment.  />
Then place it on top and remove it; and treat the wound with the preceding treatment until it heals.
About the restoration of a hook.
Chapter XI.
The bones of hooks rarely break. If they do break, then their fracture is not complete, but rather a comminuted fracture at the ends themselves; and they split along their length and bend towards the inside; and there occurs pain in the area and a puncture wound; and the affected leg becomes numb.
And its restoration is as follows: extend your hand over it until you stand above the fracture so that you can see its shape.
If the fracture is only at its ends, then equalize that fracture with what you have prepared from the equalization, until it resembles its natural shape.
However, if the fracture is along its length or has already bent towards the inside, then have the patient lie on his back until you prepare for the restoration of that fracture. When you have equalized it, place a plaster on it; then place on it a splint made of wood or skin, and bind it tightly with a bandage that will not cause displacement of the fracture or dislodgement of the splint.
And fill the concavity of the bones with what will fill it until the bandage takes it up evenly; and instruct the patient to sleep on his back or on his sound side.
If a hot abscess forms on it, then cease from extending and restoring it until the hot abscess is settled; and place upon it what will settle it, as was mentioned before, and then return to its restoration; and bind it as is necessary.
If however a fragment of bone is driven into it or it becomes comminuted, extract it.
The restoration of a broken elbow.
The elbow is that part which is between the bend of the arm and the head of the ulna.
If it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, in the following shape.
And two ligaments are tied at both ends.
Then it is hung from a high place, and the patient sits on a seat. ➤ Then he throws his broken arm over the wood until it reaches its tip, in whose middle is attached the curvature of the wood.
Then something heavy is hung above it, so that the servant extends downwards. ➤ ➤ Then the physician straightens the break with his hands at the same time, until the break returns as it should. ➤ ➤ ➤
The other method is that the patient lies on his back with his head hanging over the edge of the bed. And the hand is suspended from the neck with a ligament. Then instruct two assistants to hold the one above the break with their hands, and the other below it, and each of them pulls towards his own side.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then tie with a ligament below the break and above it with another ligament, and each of the two assistants pulls towards his own side.
If the break is near the end of the arm, then a ligament should be placed under the tip, and another under the break towards the elbow. ➤
And similarly, if the break is near the elbow, then a ligament should be placed over that place and over the elbow itself. ➤ ➤ ➤
Then straighten the break with ease and without difficulty, until when the break is straightened as it should be and a good bandage is applied, then tie it if no hot swelling occurs. ➤ ➤ ➤ ➤
If indeed a warm abscess occurs, then release the constriction until the seventh day. And place on it a wool swab soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess subsides; then constrict it. And the way to constrict it is that you should place a plaster over the break.
Then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster. Then gather the arm to the support itself and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind it on the support of the arm, so that the arm may stand in place of crutches; if nothing prevents you from this, nor will anything be changed upon you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something will change upon you from the broken bone, then use crutches; and it is as if you place on the very break a crutch which is wider and stronger than the other crutches. And place between each crutch a width of one finger; and let the length of the crutches be according to the break with an addition of three fingers from every side.
Then bind over the crutches the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage should be tighter on the place of the break. And the more the break is lengthened, let the bandage be less.
Therefore, if you see the placement of the crutches and the bandage as we have said in the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do: and if you fear a warm abscess, then release the bandage and crutches until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then examine the bandage for three days to see if there is no itching or swelling in the place, or if food is prevented from reaching the limb because of the arrangement of the bandage. Therefore, correct all that as we have said.
If indeed it is due to a hernia, so that nothing should happen from it: then do not release the ligature until after three days or more.
And let the patient lie on his back, and place his hand on his abdomen; and put under the pillow a cushion filled with wool evenly.
And be careful at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb not be disturbed, or that the ligature become loose. Therefore correct it all with your strength.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have mentioned earlier, so that the first food be fine; and continue this until the mouth begins to close up; then it is necessary to thicken his food.
For the custom of the pillow and the leg is to be tight for forty days; therefore, it must then be released, and a bath should be administered, and it should be treated with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is foul and crushed, then do not remove from it the ligature or splints until after fifty days, or two months.
About the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two forearm bones.
Of these, one is small; and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed under the smaller from below.
Therefore perhaps only the larger forearm bone breaks, or perhaps only the smaller one; and perhaps they both break together.
Therefore when the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm bone is broken below, it is a bad break, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that, it is when the two bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone which is broken is slightly higher up, then it is suitable for the physician in restoring it to place a slight extension with ease, until he aligns it.
If however the bone that is broken is large, then it is necessary to place a very strong constriction.
And it is necessary to place the hand form for its restoration, and that extension extended over the elbow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lower joint be lower than all the fingers or the rest; and let the sitting sick person be square with himself, and let the elbow be before him or straight with him, in height so that the sick person does not fall into labor.
Then let the assistant stretch the arm from the lower part, either by his hand or with a bandage. And another assistant should stretch it likewise from above.
Then let the physician align the bone until he restores it to its best shape as possible. ➤
And if there are fragments in the bone's break, then strive to restore each piece to its own place with your strength.
But if a separated fragment appears in it, and pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration; then cut over it, and remove it according to the manner we have said above among those things which are premised. ➤
But if the break is with a wound, I have already made for it a separate chapter; therefore take its treatment from there.
But if a hot abscess happens at the beginning of your restoration, then place a cloth made with salve from rose oil and white wax, and let its thickness be between thinness and thickness; and bind the cloth over it with a fine constriction, until the abscess settles down. Then remove the salve and place a prepared plaster made from flour of millstones with egg whites. Then place a splint, and let the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in many arrangements: whether the fracture is in one third or two thirds simultaneously.
Then place your bandage over the fracture site stronger and more firmly, and slightly longer than the bandage at the upper part of the fracture or its lower part: or place a softer bandage for a moment, as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be soft, moist: and not hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of proper linen, medium between fine and coarse, as we have said.
And consider the limb and ligament after days. If something happens which requires correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb: then you should anoint the limb with heated water until the itching subsides, and leave the limb unbound until it subsides, then repeat the bandaging.
But if the bandage is already softened and the bone is already moved, and similar [conditions]: then correct all of it with your skill, and examine again.
For if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to the excess of bandaging, then you should soften it slightly and leave it for several days until the nourishment reaches it, then bind it again.
But if nothing of those things that we mentioned happens to the patient, then it is not necessary to loosen [the bandage], unless after twenty days or thereabouts.
Then let the hand of the patient be suspended from his neck. And let his neck be level and guarded with skill from sudden movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or perhaps in thirty-two days; and it may be restored in twenty-eight days. All of that depends on the temperament of the constitution and the strength of the individual.
¶ On the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that a fracture of the metacarpals and the interphalangeal joints of the fingers is rare. And it does not happen unless there has been much attrition.
When therefore a fracture or attrition of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the invalid sits with his legs extended, and before him there should be a seat of equal height. Then place his hand on top of it, extended.
Then the assistant should stretch the broken bones and the physician should make them even until they join with a good union.
Then it is necessary to place a plaster and tow on it, if no hot abscess occurs. Then place a bandage on top according to the size of the place, and you have already wrapped it in a linen cloth.  ·
But if the fracture is in the lower part of the sole, then make something similar to a sphere from cloth; and instruct the invalid to press it with his broken sole; then let it be tied with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be made of leather, in which there is softness, so that it may be applied to the body of the sole; and tie it as necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary to place one bandage on top and another below on the sole; and let the hand be open and straight. Then place a tie as the hand is wrapped; and stretch between the fingers with a bandage.  ·  ·
If a fracture occurs to any of the interphalangeal joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be made even as necessary.  ·  · Then let it be strung with a thin cord, and if you wish, place a small straight splint on it, so that the break may be lifted up and not move about.
If the fracture is in one of the other fingers, such as the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or little finger, then it should be made equal and tied to the healthy finger following it; or all of them can be tied together according to their continuation, and this is better. Or place a small splint upon it, as we have said concerning the thumb.
And take into account in your restoration and after the initial arrangement of the warm swelling; and do what is necessary when something happens because of it, according to the recurrence of its narrative.
¶ On the Restoration of a Fractured Hip.
The hip bone is often broken and it becomes apparent to the sense because it turns toward the interior and posterior part.
And its restoration is done thus: that a ligament be placed above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And the patient should be laid on his face. }}}
Then let the assistant stretch every ligament to its corresponding part equally; and this is when the break is in the middle of the bone.
But if the break is near the root of the hip, then let one ligament be to the pubis bone so that the extension may fall upward; and another ligament below the fracture.
And in a similar way if the break is near the knee, then let one ligament be near the knee so that the extension may be toward below.
Then let the physician make the bone equal with both of his hands until he brings it back to its natural form; and let the bone be joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary that you place a plaster and bandage on it if there is no swelling of the limb. }}} If an abscess occurs in it: then leave it for some days until the abscess becomes hot, then return to your treatment.
Its constriction should be thus. It is necessary that you roll upon the fracture a thick bandage, that is, a binding, and fold it double or triple, and let there be some excess from it. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the base of the big toe.
And introduce a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee on the lower side, and let the ends of the thread come out from the upper part in two places. Then wrap the excess of the bandage over the leg and hip.
Then place on the hip in the place of the fracture itself a bandage, and from it place one bandage over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that the constriction may be equal.
Then begin with a strong constriction from the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four windings.  /> And as you extend more with the bandage from the place of the fracture, let your constriction be smaller and softer and gentler.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread that you introduced between the hip and leg.  /> Tie therefore with both three bandages which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower part until you reach the ankle bone of the foot. And with both tie also the ends of the bandages on the other side, so that the bandage may not be moved from its place.  />
Then leave the constriction upon it while it remains, so that no rotting, nor abscess, nor swelling, or things similar to these may occur in the limb. If something from this occurs.  /> Therefore, hasten with its treatment, and correct whatever has happened because of it entirely, as we have said many times.
If there is a fragment of bone that is causing pain: then you must smooth it out if possible. And if not, cover it with something and remove it; and treat it as was mentioned before, until it heals.
And sometimes this hip fracture is bandaged, apart from what was added to it as we said for the thigh and arm.
However, when this sort of broken bone is healed, the patient does not limp. And if it is healed alone, without the other leg being attached to it, then it is necessary that the person always limps.
And know that the hip is bandaged for fifty days, or less if it is added to or reduced too little; all this depends on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
¶ On the healing of a patella fracture of the knee.
Know that a fracture of the patella rarely happens, but it is often bruised.
If it does happen to be fractured, then it is only either a crack or a hole in its parts. And this can occur with a wound and without a wound. And a cast is placed over it entirely while it has feeling. ・
And its healing is such that the broken parts are made equal with the fingers, until they unite and join together as much as possible through equality and ease of handling.
Then place a plaster made from sesame flour or wheat flour with egg white on it; and if you need one, put a round bandage over it; and bind it tightly with a suitable bandage. ・ ・
Then consider all the conditions that we have described for other fractures; such as a hot abscess and others like it; so that you may counteract every incident with what is suitable for it until it heals.
"On the repair of a broken leg.
You should know that the leg has two bones. One is thick and is called by the name of the leg, while the other is thin and is called the fibula.
And it happens to both types of fractures that happen to the two bones of the arm, and for this reason its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is the same.
But if both bones are broken at the same time, the leg is turned towards all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is turned toward the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it will be found lower down; and this is one of those things which are not hidden from you.
Then apply traction, and reduction, and bandaging of the bones in the same manner as described before. 》
However, if the fracture of the leg is a dirty fracture with many wounds, you should make the traction less and gentler in your reduction.
And in the case of the leg, there is something different from the arm: that when you have made the bones equal and completed all your work, then take two strips of pine wood that are used for making floors, which are placed under boards, and are made from a shovel's width. Choose those that have a small thickness and are not too thin; their length should be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards. 》
Then wrap each strip with two layers of cloth along its length. 》 》 And place one part on one side of the leg and the other on the opposite side; they should extend from the knee to below the foot.
Then tie the two strips in three places: at both ends and in the middle. 》 》 》 》 When the stricture is blocked, the leg turns towards the right and left, and it stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a cradle be administered from wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it may preserve it from motion.
And indeed it is very important to do this in a fracture when there is also a wound properly [with the fracture].
Then observe the leg every day, and study it with the utmost care against swelling or inflammation or other things. If therefore any of these things happen, then apply to it what is necessary until it heals, if God wills.
On the Fracture of the Bones of the Foot and Toes.
Indeed, a fracture does not occur in the heel at all.
A fracture occurs indeed in the bones of the foot and in the toes only rarely, and it does not occur in them except as a bruise in many dispositions.  ()
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see those bones being raised over each other, then let the sick person place his foot on prepared ground as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot on that part which is raised from those bones; afterwards press or tread it down and make it even until it returns to its place.
Then apply a poultice and wool on top; and place under the sole of the foot a small board that has two flat ends.
Then bind it with a decent bandage after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things that are needed.
Therefore, when three or four days pass for him, then release the bandage. For you will find the bones leveled out, whether they were broken or dislocated. The operation on them is as you see.
But if any of the fingers are broken, then restore them, and make them equal as I have told you in the restoration of the hand's fingers. Then place a finger-shaped splint according to the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider; then place under the foot the board I have told you about, and bind it with a decent bandage.
But if the broken parts are two or three or more, then place a splint of appropriate size on each finger wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the lower board under the foot: and let it stand on one end of the board outside the belly of the foot so that it may hold well in position.
And it is fitting for you not to forget to consider all that I have said to you concerning other breaks from the injuries we mentioned: and counter each injury with what is appropriate.
¶ Concerning the break of a woman's vulva, and the bone of the pubis, and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is broken, then make her sit upright. Then slightly lean her back toward her own back, and let her be held from behind.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with cotton until it is full; and let there be in the vulva from this as if a sphere.
Then let the woman be shaken, and gradually raise up her back. /> For that cotton comes out until it is at the vulva's entrance like a sphere, and the broken part returns.
Then place a small pillow behind her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cotton with ease; then let her urinate; and put it back in the same way you first inserted it; then let her return to her original pillow.
Do this for seven days, or thereabouts, so that the wound itself may be restored.
And if you wish, take the bladder of a sheep and tie a small reed tube over its opening, then insert the entire bladder into the wound. Then blow through the tube with strength until the bladder inflates within the wound, for the broken bone will return to its place.
Then fill it with cotton and let it remain for several days, as we have said, until it heals.
And when the rib of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and alignment that which we have mentioned regarding the jawbone.
And there is no secret in these nearby fractures that hardly touch each other, especially for those who have some knowledge or skill.
And understand this book of mine well, for the fracture itself indicates to you the correct way in its restoration and union in many arrangements; therefore, understand.
But when a man's penis is broken, take the neck of a goose and insert the penis into it. Then place a bandage of cloth over it and bind it, and leave it for three days or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills it.
About the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is appropriate for one who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone or thigh bone, or similar to that, to hasten and bleed them immediately: if the conditions of bleeding are evident to you, as we have said previously.
