If the wound bleeds, then you should hasten to cauterize it by applying powdered myrrh over it: if nothing else is available.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause bad things to happen to it.
But if the broken bone is protruding and visible through the skin: then you should try to reduce it and make it even with your hands, using ease and slight extension.
But if its extension and equalization by hand are not prepared: then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is the size of seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the size of the wound. And because of this it is necessary for the doctor to take from them three or four according to the size he needs for that purpose in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it does not double when pressed upon during the operation: and let its end be sharp, which has an edge at its ends. And let its upper part be in thickness from half of it to its lower part.
And this is its form. ›
It is necessary in this operation to place the sharp curved end of it on the protruding bone; and push it with one movement, until the bone returns and becomes even to some degree.
Try therefore to make the ends of the fracture equal to each other.
If the end of the fracture is thin and the instrument does not take it with a good grip, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If you cannot return them with what we have described, then cut it with one of the incisions that we have mentioned, or saw it with a saw in whatever way possible for you.
Then scrape off what remains on the bone from roughness and small shavings.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced and the patient afterwards finds great pain that harms him, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do this. For you provide the patient with a great help, when your restoration of the bone is completed.  >
Then soak the cloth in black wine vinegar. And properly if it is summer. And do not place a cheese-cloth on the wound, nor anything containing oil: lest it cause putrefaction and corruption there.
Then administer astells at the end of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound open: so that you make a cut with scissors in the bandages according to the size of the wound.
And take care not to bind the wound with the break. For often foolish physicians have done this: and caused either death or corruption or zochem, that is, pus, to happen to their patients. And let your binding be soft and gentle, different from the binding of other breaks.
If the wound is bad or is a large wound, and you fear some of the bad outcomes that we have mentioned may happen to it, and you find lingering pain in the place: then it is not right for you to put astells on it. And make bandages of tough cloth in place of astells and bind the wound with them.
But after one day or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove from it the cloth which you placed on it with wine. Then administer liniments and ointments which we are accustomed to use for treating wounds; such as the four-ingredient ointment and similar ones.
And it is fitting that you should loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every morning and evening until it heals and is made whole.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that the pus runs off easily.
But if many days have passed and the wound has not healed, nor has the pus been discharged from it; then you should know that there are small splinters of bone there. Therefore, it is necessary to explore the wound with a probe. However many of those splinters are separated out, remove them and extract them. And those that are not separated and prick the flesh, causing pain, try to cut them out and remove them in every way possible for you to do so.
But if the wound should become inflamed or have pustules, that is, suppuration, or another sign of corruption and putrefaction; then you must apply to each of them that which is appropriate for treating it, as has been described before in its own chapter.
And concerning what I have said and to which you should give your attention: when a bone is broken and protrudes from the flesh, such as the thighbone or hipbone, and similar large bones; it is that you should not attempt to reduce it, for many times death occurs from this; rather leave it alone until it becomes putrid.
For perhaps it may fall out on its own after twenty days or thirty.
Then, if it seems to you that the wound is healing, take care of it; but if not, leave it alone.
¶ On treating a swelling at the site of a fracture.
Multiple times this swelling occurs during the healing of a fracture. Especially when it is near a joint: and from this configuration the shape of the limb becomes ugly. And perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Therefore, if the swelling is recent, apply to it medicines that have an astringent effect, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, scammony, acacia, and similar substances.
Thus, take some or all of these substances and mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or vinegar. And place them on the swelling on wool, and bind them well on the swelling itself. xspace
And leave the binding in place; do not remove it for many days. Then release it, and repeat another one, until the swelling is removed. xspace
Or bind a lead plate neatly over it. For lead has the property of removing anything feared from the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned into a hard mass and requires immediate removal, then cut open its top part and cut away the prominent excess: or scrape it with some scrapers until it is removed; and care for it until it heals. xspace 
¶ On the treatment when a limb is restored but remains thin and not according to its natural shape.
When a bone fracture is restored and the limb remains thin and weak afterwards, this occurs only for many reasons. One of these reasons is the frequent loosening and tightening of the ligaments not as it should be, or because of an excessively tight binding of the ligaments, which prevents nourishment from reaching the limb; or because of an excessive amount of ointment applied at the wrong time; or because of a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and his weakness.  xspace
And its treatment is to feed the patient and make his body soft until the blood increases in him. And apply a bath. And introduce joy and happiness upon him.
Then place pitch on the member, so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. And continue its embrocation with warm water until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural shape.
§ On the treatment of broken bones when they are restored crookedly.
When a member already restored after its healing has a crookedness or prominence in the broken bone, or knotting, and the form of the member is disfigured by it, yet the member does not prevent its natural operation, then one should not accept the opinion of someone who thinks that the member should be broken again.
And indeed there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who did this in our land. ➤
And this operation is very blameworthy, leading to death or great fear, as if it were fatal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary that an embrocation be made with warm water in which have been boiled softening herbs, such as althea leaves and its root, and melilot, and similar things. And a plaster should be made for him with softening plaster, such as a well-made diachylon.
Or the mucilage of the althea root should be shaken with chicken fat or sesame oil, and it should be plastered with it. ➤
Or take ripe figs and grind them with dove dung and similar substances that are called in the medicines as ones that decrease hardening.
And sometimes the knotting is dissolved with constant, old, and gentle friction, which is done with wet hands. And let motion be applied to every limb in every hour.
But if the tortuosity has already taken hold and become strong, and turned into a hard mass requiring surgical intervention: then it is necessary to cut through it from above, and sever the bone's continuity with thin incisors; and ease should be applied to it with care and diligence.
On Speech Regarding Dislocation
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its proper place. Therefore, it is prevented from moving and the figure of the limb is disfigured. And causes severe pain and injuries to afflict the patient.
Therefore, when someone experiences a dislocation, it is necessary to hasten for its restoration and not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the site becomes swollen, and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation as well.
Because of this, it should not be moved or extended during the hour of its swelling. Since often it causes cramp and painful injuries to occur.
However, when this occurs, then it is necessary for you to hasten to bleed the patient.  /> Nam sic fit, ut restauretur dislocatio in eo tempore, et non perducit in nephritidem. Then it should be left alone until the swelling subsides.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And every part of the limb should be scraped, as much as is remembered to return to its proper place.
I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation even as it was discussed previously in the section on fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower part.  /> Et secundum ordinem locorum, et secundum quod est necessarium.
¶ On the Care of Dislocated Lower Jaw
The two jaws are seldom dislocated except rarely.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to two methods. For either they are moved from their places by a slight removal and are softened briefly. Or they are dislocated by complete dislocation until they soften to the chest part, until the patient's saliva runs, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue rolls with speech.
However, when its dislocation is slight, then it returns by itself in many positions with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete, then it is necessary to administer its reduction quickly and not delay it at all: and it is as if the attendant holds the head of the patient, and the physician inserts his thumb of one hand into the root of the jaw between his teeth, if the dislocation is from one side.  Or he inserts both thumbs at once, if the dislocation is from two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands are outside, with which he pushes evenly.
And instruct the patient to clean his jaws and open them fully as if to eat.  And the physician pushes the jaw evenly and presses it until it returns to its place.  
But if its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are together; then administer fomentation with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy; do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
Therefore, when they return and are leveled, and the patient's mouth is closed and does not soften; then place upon them small pieces of cloth, with a compress made from wax and rose oil.   
Then it should be tied with ease using soft ligatures.
And let the patient sleep on his back; and his head should be upright between two cushions, so that it does not move to the right or left; nor should he attempt, or be forced, to chew anything; rather, let his food be a gentle sipping until, when the pains are removed, and the jaw is fixed, then he may eat what he wishes, and it should be administered with ease.
He should not exert himself over the surgery of his mouth while eating and drinking and swallowing, until the jaw is fixed and healed.
But if the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their proper places, then fevers and continuous sweats often occur from this, and perhaps the patient’s stomach will loosen, and perhaps he will vomit pure bile.
Therefore, when you see this happening, know that it is a loss, and often the patient dies from it within ten days.
¶ On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the end of the upper arm.
Indeed, the collarbone is not dislocated from inside the side due to its connection with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates outwardly, and this is apparent to the senses.
Its restoration is done as follows: let the patient lie on his back, and stretch out his arms. › Then strongly press down on the place with your palm, for it will return by itself.
Then apply a plaster and cushions to it, and bind it tightly.
However, its ends which follow the elbow and are connected with it, do not become dislocated, except rarely. › › But if it is dislocated on some day, then it must be reduced and set according to what we have said and prepared for you.
Then place upon it a plaster, pills, and a bandage. And bid the patient to adhere to rest and quiet until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the end of the arm is reduced when it has also been separated from its place.
¶ On the reduction of the dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder cannot be dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it may be dislocated towards the lower part of the axilla.  The second way is for it to be dislocated towards the chest. And perhaps it may be dislocated towards the upper part of the arm, which happens rarely.
And it cannot be dislocated towards the back, because of the glenoid cavity. And it cannot be dislocated towards the front, because of the nerves.
And most often it is indeed dislocated and exits towards the lower part of the axilla; especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because in them it exits quickly and returns quickly.  
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite of that, for it exits with difficulty and returns with difficulty. 
And perhaps in some people, a blow or a fall occurs and the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, and it is thought on account of that, because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you should test that until you stand upon the certainty of it, and then proceed to its treatment.  
And a dislocation is known when it is towards the lower part of the axilla; and you should distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy one. For you will find between them a manifest difference, and in the head of the shoulder there is a concavity, and under the axilla when touched it feels as if it were an egg; and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear nor move it in all its movements.
And likewise, if it is dislocated toward the chest or upward, then you will find a clear touch, and it will not be hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent or if the boy is weak.
And its reduction is that the assistant should raise his hand upward. Then place your thumbs under his nipple, and lift the joint with strength upward to its place: and the assistant should raise his hand and extend it upward. Then let him place it below, for it will return quickly.
But if it does not return through what has been said, and many days have passed since the dislocation first occurred, then it is necessary for the patient to bathe in warm water and be given an infusion that softens and soothes, such as boiling althea root, fenugreek, and melilot in water and administering it.
Then let the patient be laid on his back, and place under his nipple a ball of wool that is equally balanced between softness and tightness.
Then let the physician place his heel on the ball, and lift the head of the humerus with strength, and pull the patient's hand downward.  />  />  />  />
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method: and it is that you should have present a man who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on the side, and insert his humerus under the patient's nipple; and lift the patient's nipple upward until the patient is suspended in the air.  />  />  />  />  />  />  />  /> And another assistant should pull the patient's hand downward toward his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary to hang something with him so that he becomes heavier; for the dislocation will return immediately.
And it can also be reduced by another method: and that is to fix a long piece of wood into the ground, with a rounded head like the top of a mortar, not too thick, nor too thin. Then, after placing the patient on top of the head of the wood, soft cloths should be put under the patient along the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be extended downward on one side and his body should be pulled in the opposite direction with strength, for the dislocation returns to its place quickly.
If its reduction is difficult by all the means we have mentioned, then use this method: and that is to take a piece of wood whose length is equal to two arms, and whose width is equal to four fingers, and whose thickness is equal to two fingers; it should have a rounded head to facilitate its insertion deep into the ear canal, as shown in this figure.
Then soft cloths should be tied over the rounded head, so that the wood does not injure the patient.
Then it should be placed under the patient's ear, so that the hand or arm can be extended over the wood until it reaches below; and the wood should be tied to an assistant, the arm, and the tip of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and the hand should be extended downward, and the rest of the body should be left hanging on the other side. For the dislocation returns immediately.
Therefore, when its passage is completed by whatever possible method, then it is necessary to place a woolen sphere of equal size under the ear. Then apply a plaster prepared from millstone powder and frankincense with egg white over the entire shoulder, as shown in the figure above.
Then tie a proper bandage under the ear and roll the bandage over the plaster as shown above; and fasten his hand to his own neck, and leave it there without moving his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that the food of the sick person be scarce, until the limb is strengthened; because it is swifter for its healing.
Then let more [food] be given after seven days or five, and let it be consumed with movement. Therefore, if it becomes firm and does not soften, then it is already healed.
If, however, the joint is dislocated many times, either because of the moisture that affects it or for another reason, then it is necessary to apply a three-pronged cautery to it, as has been said previously in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done and the ligament is released after seven days and the joint does not firm up, and the plaster and stricture are repeatedly applied to it but do not become firm, and fall off, and cannot be raised upwards; then know that the nerves which are at the head of the shoulder have already been cut or stretched or softened; and know that the joint will never become fixed in its place.
But a dislocation which occurs towards the chest and breast or back is restored with pushing and extending by hand until it returns, and then the rest of the plaster and care are administered to it until it heals.
If, after healing, there is heaviness in the limb and slowness of movement, then let the sick person use baths many times until that heaviness is lessened and it returns to its original nature.
¶ On the Treatment of Elbow Dislocation
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and likewise returns with difficulty; indeed it can be dislocated in all directions, especially anteriorly or posteriorly.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you; for it falls under sight and touch, in whatever form it may be dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is clearly shown to you. For the joint will be drawn together, and it cannot double the arm nor touch its own shoulder with it.
And you must hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess develops in it.
For if a hot abscess develops in it, the reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it does not heal at all, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all other types of dislocations and causes more intense pain for them; and death often comes with it.
And the restoration of it when it is among those that can be reduced is to extend its hand with two hands: and let its arm be stretched out, and the doctor's hands be above the elbow and below it, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is towards the front: then it returns by doubling its hand with his own hand, until he strikes the dislocated elbow with the root of his palm.
If the dislocation does not obey in its reduction, then administer a very strong and forceful extension; and it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and two assistants should also hold him so that he cannot move during the extension.
Then the arm should be rolled to all parts after a double-length cloth or a wide bandage is wrapped around its hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil: so that it is among those things that help in the smoothness of the joint with ease. Then he should push the joint with a strong thrust until it returns. 》
And after its reduction it is necessary to place a poultice on it, which contains astringency and drying with the white of an egg; and let it be bound with a proper bandage. And let the arm be hung from the neck of the sick person; and leave it for some days.
Then untie it. If then the joint is strengthened in its place, then untie the bandage from it and leave it alone.
And if you see that the joint is not well tightened, then repeat the plaster and the bandage, and leave it alone for some days until it is tightened. Then untie it.
But if heaviness happens to it after the reduction of the joint and the arrangement is in motion: then apply moistening with bathing, fine friction, and compression until it becomes lined, or place on the joint liliatum, that is, the fatty tail of a ram. Then let it be bound, and leave it on it day and night. Then remove it, and put him into a bath. When he sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat over it liliatum once, and a second time, and a third with entering the bath, until it is relieved.
And if you wish, place over it fresh cow dung, moistened and heated with butter, and bind it over it.
Do this several times. For it is soothed, and returns to its own state.
¶ On the treatment of dislocation according to Al-Zahrawi.
The small finger of the hand is often dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And its reduction is easy.  /> Unlike other joints.  />  \\ Nevertheless, it is necessary that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place becomes swollen; or a hot swelling occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place a Mahassem on the infirm hand on a board, and let the assistant extend his hand; and let the doctor place his palm over the prominence of the joint, and press it until it returns.
However, it should be considered whether the palm has already receded to the abdomen of the hand; then let the patient place the back of his hand on the board for extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be internally on the board, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the board so that the doctor's hand falls upon the very prominence of the joint.
Therefore, if it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then bind it with a plaster that draws out the swelling, and leave it, do not move it.
For if he does not tolerate it and you are unable to accomplish the reduction after some days have passed, although the joint remains according to its own twist, and nothing harms the patient except that his hand becomes soft and cannot grasp anything, then know that the nerves have been cut or damaged.
There is no skill in this, except to bind it with a hot iron near its injury. And perhaps that does not help at all.
Therefore, when the Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster which we have described. Then let it be bound and left for five days; afterwards let it be unbound and left, and the hand should be exercised.
But if its motion is hindered and something heavy happens to it, then soften it with warm water and frequent rubbing until it is soothed.
About the treatment of dislocated fingers.
When one of the fingers is dislocated in any direction, or when a finger is dislocated to the back of the palm or its abdomen, then extend the finger and press on the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger and hang it towards the side to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it straightens on that day.
When it is night, bind it according to the account itself; and do not cease unbinding it during the day; and exercise it with movement; and bind it at night.
Do this for several days until it tightens.
And do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; and bind it to that part, and perform your first procedure with it until it heals.
And do the same with it when it is dislocated in other parts.
¶ About the treatment of the dislocation of the back vertebrae.
When someone has a complete dislocation of the back or neck vertebrae, or many vertebrae are moved from their places, then there is no cure for it, because death hastens to the sick person.
And the sign of this is that the sick person's excretion comes out involuntarily, and he cannot control it; and often some of his limbs are paralyzed from it, or both his legs, or both his arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is moved from its place.  />
For often they are moved, because often their removal happens in four parts.  />
That which is moved to the back is called humpbackedness.
But its cure is that you should consider then, if the humpbackedness happened from childhood; then there is no cure for it, and there is absolutely no healing from it.
But in that which happens by chance, or by a blow, or similar things, the ancients have already spoken about methods of cure at length.  />  />  />  /> Whose benefit is least.
But I have already shortened it from that [version] whose inadequacy is excused by its closeness to me and my intention and exposition which I used and made the instrument in a different way than they showed and explained it.
Therefore, I say that in a hump which occurs from the front part, there is no cure or healing from it.
And similarly in one which forms on both sides.
And it is not cured by them, except the one which occurs in the back properly, as I will narrate: and it is to extend the sick person on his face on a level platform near the wall; and to spread moist straw under him so that his chest is not hurt.
Then a straight stick fixed in a hole in the ground should be placed at the end of the platform near his head; and another stick near his feet at the other end of the platform; and an assistant should hold the stick and it should not be firmly fixed in the hole. And another assistant should hold the other [stick].
Then roll [the bandage] over the head of the sick person and under his chin tie a soft but strong ligature; and extend the end of the ligature to the stick which is near his head, and tie it on that.
Then bind another ligature above his ankles, and above his knees, and near his groin. Then gather all the ligatures together and tie them on the other stick which is near his feet.
Then let each assistant pull on his portion of the ligature toward the stick and the two sticks should not be moved from their places where they are fixed; however, they should not be firmly fixed, as we said.  />  Deinde extendat minister ut supra, et trahat infirmum per medium scapulas eius ad dorsum eius; et sic debebit curari.
And the physician should place his hands on the spondyl with strength until he returns; or he should place a board on it. Then he attaches his feet to the board until he returns.
If he does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits. And hollow out in the wall we spoke of, which is near the patient, a place into which the end of the board can enter. Then place the middle of the board on the pillow. And let the physician place his feet on the other end. And he should bind it well until it constricts the pillow and then return to his place. 》
Then if you wish, work with a lathe that is operated by hand; and it should be such that you fix in the ground near the head of the patient, at the end of the bed, two wooden stakes whose length is one cubit each: and also two wooden stakes at his head, at the end of the bed.
And let the distance between each stake be one handbreadth: and now a hole has been made in every place through which the lathe can run; and let all the wood be firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all. 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》
And insert a round wooden rod; and this is the lathe on which the bandage is wound between the two holes of the two stakes.
And at its end is a hole into which the wood, whose length is one handbreadth with what is wound around it, is fixed; and similarly on the two other stakes.
Then bind the bandages that you have bound on the patient's chest with the lathe at his head; and the bandages that you have bound on his legs with the lathe at his feet.
Then let there be an assistant standing by each lathe who turns his hand with the winding cord by which the lathe is wound; and the physician adjusts the pillow according to what we have said. 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》 》
This is the form of a lathe, which follows.
Then, after the return of the lathe and the place is leveled, it is necessary to put on a desiccating plaster with egg white. Then straw. Then place on top of the plaster a little plate of wood whose width is three fingers or about that. And its length should be such that it fits the swollen place and extends over the healthy part of the lathe.
Then let it be bound with the bandage mentioned earlier. And let the patient use a light diet until he is cured.
If any swelling remains in the place at the end of the treatment, then it is necessary to administer softening and soothing medicines for a long time together with the application of the board we have described. And now lead is applied on that plate.
And sometimes a swelling occurs in the last vertebra of the back; and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that bone which now protrudes. Therefore, one should not presume to treat it with this medicine; for perhaps it will cause death.
¶ On the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the joint of the ankle and the joint of the shoulder never occur except as a dislocation, nor does what happens to other joints through slight separation and concavity happen to them. 
And indeed, the ankle joint can be dislocated in four parts. That is, because it can be dislocated forward and backward.   
And the ankle joint is most commonly dislocated inward, and rarely outward or backward.        
And the treatment for its inward dislocation is this: because when you join the healthy leg of the patient with his sick one, it is longer; and his knee protrudes more than the healthy one, and he cannot bend his foot at the groin; and there is a swelling in the place following the groin, because the head of the thigh bone has already arrived there.                        
And the sign that it has happened on the outside is that its accidents are contrary to these accidents.
And the sign that it has happened on the front is that it extends the healthy leg to its full length; however, it cannot double it without pain in the knee. And if it tries to walk, it cannot step over it to the front, and its urine is retained, and an inguinal swelling appears, and during walking there is a stepping on its heel.
And the sign that it has happened on the back is that it does not extend the knee nor can it double it before doubling the groin. And its leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and there is swelling at the place of the hip bone.
The reduction of these kinds of dislocations is, to consider then, if the dislocation is old and has already begun to heal in the patient. Then if you administer its reduction and it remains according to its position, then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, it should not be presumed upon completely.
But in the one whose dislocation is recent and is from one of the four kinds of dislocations, then hurry to do this: roll the joint and extend it to the inside and outside, and move it to the right and left; perhaps it will return and not need any other care.
But if it does not return this way, then a strong helper should be prepared and extend his leg from the lower part; either with his hands or with a ligament that is tied over his leg above the knee. And another helper extends his hand over it, so that he inserts his hands under its armpits. Then bind it with a gentle ligature over the hip joint, and hold the end of another ligature.
And its extension should be from the front of the groin to the collarbone, or from behind to the spine.
And let their extension be all in one go, until the patient is lifted off the ground with his body, and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is a common type among four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then each type must be treated according to its own specific method of healing.
In the proper reduction of it when the dislocation is inward, one must make the patient lie on his sound side. Then place a ligature over the hip joint, between the head of the thigh and the place that is under the groin. 》 》 Then extend the ligature upward from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the collarbone.
Then another strong assistant should take hold of his arms, and reduce the thickened part of the dislocated hip with a violent extension outward. Now it has returned to its place.
And this method is the easiest of all the methods for reducing this limb. 》
But if the dislocation remains stubborn and does not submit to this method of treatment, then it is necessary that both feet of the patient be bound together with a strong ligature over both heels and both knees; and let each ligature have a length of four fingerbreadths from its companion; and let the infirm leg be extended by more than one and a half times the length.
Then let the patient be suspended over the head of a beam that is in the house; and let its length from the ground be two arm's lengths.
Then command a strong young man to support the head of the thigh; and let another young man be suspended with the patient; and let another young man push, supporting the thigh with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly. 》
In its own proper reduction, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary for the patient to lie on a table, as we have narrated regarding the hunchbacked person. And let the ligament be tightened above his leg, properly, and above his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one by his feet, and the other by his head. Then let an additional piece of wood be placed in the middle of the table, fixed well, upon which moistened cloths may be wound, so that it does not harm the patient: and let the wood be between his thighs, so that it is not drawn downwards during the extension.
Then let each assistant pull towards his side, and the physician shall equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it submits to the return, it will be good.
And if not, place another table on top of it and press it evenly, as we have said regarding the hunchbacked condition. However, it is necessary for the patient to lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary for the leg of the patient to be extended quickly: and this is according to the same account on the table: and let the physician place the palm of his right hand over the groin of the patient.  /> Then let him compress it with the other hand, and with that compression place the leg extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the back, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended downwards, but rather he should be raised off the ground; nay, it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is also necessary if his ankle is dislocated to the outside, according to what we have said regarding his lying on two ankles: and this is face down with the ligaments extended, as we have recently said.
And it is necessary that a compress be applied with a board also on the place from which the joint exits, when the reduction is completed as it should be.
And the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you; and it is that you stretch out the two legs of the patient: and when you see them equal, and the patient tightens his leg and extends it without hesitation: then know that the limb has already returned as it should: then join the two thighs, and put on a poultice, and bind with a bandage, which is, a splint: thus, so that no joint moves to any part: and let the patient adhere to rest for three days, or four.
Then release the bandage and poultice, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in position, then know that the dislocation is now fixed: so dismiss the patient to walk.
And if you see any swelling: then return, and poultice it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it also for three days.
Then release it and make the patient delay walking on both legs for some days, until it is well strengthened.
¶ On Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee can be dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated to the outer side, to the inner side, and to the lower side, that is, to the back.
And it does not dislocate to the front. ‌
And the sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply his leg to his thigh. ‌ ‌ If it does not stick with the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all ways of its dislocation is that you make the patient sit upright, so that he can already extend his leg over it if he can, and press your back against his face; and put his foot between your feet.
Then press his knee with your palms, and stretch them between your fingers over his knee. Then apply both sides of his knee with your palm strongly. And another assistant should extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction is that the leg adheres to the hip gently, not forcefully.
Then bandage it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with a band for three days, or four. Then unbind them.
And the patient should not walk for some days until he is healed.
If the reduction is refused by you, then apply strong traction with ligaments, as has been mentioned previously in the treatment of the ankle, until it returns.
¶ On the treatment of the dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes moved by a slight displacement and sometimes dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either to the inner or outer side.  />
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, protruding towards the side to which it has been displaced.  />  />
The treatment of its slight displacement is an easy reduction: and it is to stretch it gently with the hands and pull until it returns.  />  />  />  />  />  />  />
In the treatment of it when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary to make the patient sit upright; and let a strong assistant hold him very firmly from behind his back, in its middle part.
Then hold with your right hand the upper part of his foot and with your left hand the lower part of his foot in the place of the heel.  />  />  />  />  />  />  />  />  />  />
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. "Then with the left hand two times.
"Then extend with the left hand, and press the breast of the foot with the right hand towards the shin without difficulty.
"Do that twice, as we have said.
"Then press the breast of the foot towards the shin a third time, and you pull out the tendon.
"If then it returns in one time or two according to this manner, and you see the foot even, it is good. And if not, then repeat the operation on him, because he will return.
"But if you are prevented from bringing it back as we have said, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and drive a stake into the ground very firmly, so that it falls between his thighs; and you have already rolled a cloth over him, so that it does not hurt the patient.
"Then let the assistant take hold of his thigh; afterwards, let the other assistant extend the foot, either with his hands or with a bandage that he binds around the ankle. "Then let every assistant pull against the extension of his companion; and the stake shall stand between the two thighs of the patient, holding him so that his body is not drawn downwards during the extension.
"Then let the physician equalize the dislocation with his hands, and the other assistant shall hold the healthy leg below. "For the dislocation returns quickly.
"When therefore the dislocation returns and its soundness is made clear to you, then place a poultice and wool, and bind it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot to the shin with a bandage.
"And you must be careful or fearful of the nerves which are above the heel at the back, lest there be a tight bandage over them and they injure them.
"Then leave him for two days, or three. If the sinew is softened, bind it; then release it on the third or fourth day, and prevent the patient from walking for forty days. But if he attempts to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not dissolve and be destroyed, so that afterward he may not receive healing.
But if he develops a hot abscess, then it is necessary that you administer to him the substance previously mentioned in our treatise on healing, and apply a poultice until it is removed.
On the Dislocation of the Toes and its Treatment
It is necessary to straighten what has been dislocated among them with a slight extension without difficulty; indeed this is not difficult but easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the foot's back, then it is necessary for the patient to sit with his foot on a level surface or on a board, and he should stand as if walking. ️
Then you should stand, and place your foot on the part that protrudes from those joints. Then step on it with your foot with strength until it returns and you see it has been straightened, and no protrusion appears in that place.
Then place under his foot a board that fits the entire foot, which has two handles. Then bind it with a decent bandage for three days. Then release it, and make him refrain from walking for many days until the joint is bound and securely healed.
On the Types of Dislocations with Wounds or Fractures
When something like this happens and you attempt to heal it and restore it, death often results; therefore, one should not undertake the healing of such a case unless he is skilled in the art with long experience, merciful, pious, cautious, not rash or presumptuous: and that he use at the beginning medicines which only suppress hot abscesses: and leave the patient to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for health from destruction with gentleness of illness, and in which something of hope appears. Try, therefore, to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If the member returns as we wish, then administer a treatment that settles the abscess, and care for the wound with one of the drying ointments suitable to it.
But if the dislocation is with fracture, and separate fragments occur in the bone, then try to remove them, and do what we have said about simple diseases from that which precedes in their places.
And be careful according to your strength, and keep yourself from entering the way of fear; just as my teaching has gone before you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and safer for your body among those at your time.
